The Bulletin
An Earlier Omar
The Khayyam habit has become a modern
vice ; it is as easy nowadays to Rubaiyat as
to split an infinitive ; but it is somewhat
depressing to learn that the disease first
appeared 40 years before Omar started to
Khayyam. The namfe of'the first patient
was Abu’l-Ala, the Syrian, who was born 44
years before Omar, the tent-maker. Abu’l-
Ala was a great poet, much reverenced
by a multitude of disciples at Baghdad, and
among his works were The Falling Spark of
Tinder and The Necessity of What is Un-
necessary—which sound decidedly Syrian.
From these works Henry Baerlein has com-
piled a remarkable “diwan,” or selection.
In his translation Baerlein has wisely dis-
carded the original quantitative metre, and
utilised a stanza similar to, though not the
same as, that immortalised by Fitzgerald.
And his selections disclose a poet of the
same stamp as Omar, often of the same
poetic merit. But before a final judgment
of the standing of Abu’l-Ala is possible it
will be necessary to have literal translations
of his works. Omar owed much to the trans-
fusion of his thought with that of Fitzgerald
—as we know it, the Rubaiyat is really a
collaboration between the poet and the trans-
lator ; and it may be that Henry Baerlein
has done for his pet poet what Fitzgerald did
for his. But that larger work is promised
by Baerlein, together with a full account
of the life of the Syrian. Meantime we are
thankful for what we have got in this little
book, The Diwan of Abu’l-Ala, in “The
Wisdom of the East” series, published by
Murray at Is.
For this, however much it owes to the
creator or to the translator, is true poetry,
and for true poetry we are too grateful to
pry into origins. Abu’l-Ala’s poem in places
anticipates, and with the same poetic
thought, the wrnrk of Omar. Hear his
delicious raillery at religion :
Do !> there are many ways and many traps
And many guides, and which of them is lord ?
For verily Mahomet has the sword,
And he may have the truth—perhaps ! perhaps 1
Now this religion happens to prevail
Until by that one it is overthrown—
Because men dare not live with men alone,
But always with another fairy-tale.
Religion is a charming girl, I say ;
But over this poor threshold will not pass,
Because I can’t unveil her, and alas !
The bridal gift I cfin’t afford to pay.
\V
He has, too, the same philosophy, or,
rather, since Omar came after Abu’l-Ala,
the tent-maker but gives another and
equally poetic rendering of his predecessor’s
philosophic doubt.
Have conversation with the wind that goes
Bearing a pack of loveliness and pain :
The golden exultation of the grain,
And the last, sacred whisper of the rose,
But if in some enchanted garden bloom
The rose imperial that will not fade.
Ah ! shall I go with desecrating spade,
And underneath her glories build a tomb ?
Shall I that am as dust upon the plain
Think with unloosened hurricanes to fight?
Or shall I that was ravished from the night
Fall on the bosom of the night again?
T?et if all things that vanish in their noon
Are but the part of some eternal scheme,
Of what the nightingale may chance to dream,
Or what the lotus murmurs to the moon !
Have I not heard sagacious ones repeat
An irresistibly grim argument:
That we for all our blustering content
Are as the silent shadows at our feet.
No Sultan at his pleasure shall erect
A dwelling less obedient to decay
Than I, to whom all mysteries obey.
Build with the twilight for an architect.
Our fortune is, like mariners, to float
Amid the perils of dim waterways ;
Shall then our seamanship have aught of praise
If the great anchor drags behind the boat?
My faith it is that all the wanton pack
Of living shall be—hush, poor heart!—with-
drawn,
As even to the camel comes a dawn
Without a burden for his wounded back.
Some yellow sand all hunger shall assuage,
And for my thirst no cloud have need to roll,
And ah ! the drooping bird, which is my soul,
No longer shall be prisoned in a cage.
One is behind the draperies of life,
One who will tear these tanglements away—
No dark assassin, for the dawn of day
Leaps out, as leapeth laughter, from the knife.
He was the glazier out of Erzeflm,
Whose wizardry would make the children cry—
There will be no such wizardry when I
Am broken by the chariot-wheel’s of Doom.
The chariot-wheels of Doom ! Now, hear them roll
Across the .desert and the noisy mart,
Across the silent places of your heart—
Smile on the driver you will not cajole.
Live well ! Be wary of this life, I say ;
Do not o’erload yourself with righteousness.
Behold ! the sword we polish in excess,
We gradually polish it away.
But for the grandest flame our God prepares
The breast of man, which is the grandest urn;
Yet is that flame so powerless to burn
Those butterflies, the swarm of little cares.
W
And Abu’l-Ala has other facets than those
of his follower. There is, for instance, his
charming fable of the fattened worms in
“the silent chamber of the rose ” ; and here
are two stanzas that express an old thought
with a marvellous precision :
The songs we fashion from our new delight
Are echoes. When the first of men sang out
He shuddered, hearing not alone the shout
Of hills, but of the peoples of the night.
And all the marvels that our eyes behold
Are pictures. There has happened some event
For each of them, and this they represent—
Our lives are like a tale that has been told.
Finally, these striking stanzas, selected
out of many, need no comment :
There is a palace, and the ruined wall
Divides the sand, a very home of tears,
Arid where love whispered of a thousand years
The silken-footed caterpillars crawl.
I think our world is not a place of rest,
But where a man many take his little ease,
Until the landlord, whom he never sees,
Gives that apartment to another guest.
Say that you come to life as ’twere a feast,
Prepared to pay whatever is the bill
Of death or tears —surely, my friend, you will
Not shrink at death, which is among the least?
God pities him who pities. Ah, pursue
No longer now the children of the wood ;
Or have you not, poor huntsman, understood
That somebody is overtaking you ?
Now fear the rose ! You travel to the gloom
Of which the roses sing and sing so fair.
And, but for them, you’d have a certain share
In life : your name be read upon the tomb.
No sword will summon Death, and he will stay
For neither■■helm nor shield his falling rod.
We are the crooked alphabet of God,
And He will read us ere He wipes away.
How strange that we, perambulating dust,
■ Should be the vessels of eternal fire,
That such unfading passion for desire
Should be within our fading bodies thrust.
The shadows come, and they will come to bless
Their brother and his dwelling and his fame,
When I shall soil no more with any blame
Or any praise the silence I possess.
Enough has been given to prove that in
this Diwan there are pearls that are worth
the finding, pearls'for whose setting we thank
Henry Baerlein. No. lover of poetry, no
person who has delighted in Omar, can
afford to overlook this cheap and remark-
able book.
The American Wife
The American Woman is beginning to get
on the nerves of the American novelist.
Surfeited with the frontispiece woman of the
“ best-sellers,” he has turned to analyse the
other kind—the selfish, sexless, useless wife
of the rich. So in Together (Macmillan, New
York) Robert Herrick laboriously, and a
little dully, discloses the canker of heartless-
ness at the heart of the modern American
marriage. His book is large and leisurely
and inchoate—which, after all, is what Life
is. He takes a parcel of American wives
and deals with them with a detail that is
often tiresome ; but out of his novel emerges
an indictment against the American woman,
with a hint of the way for her reform. These
wives, all except one, vaguely yearn for a
“large, full life” (which usually has The
Other Man specified in it); and this large,
full life is hot filled up with children.
And the one exception fills her life with
nothing but love—and babies ; and grows fat.
When Isabelle learns that a child is coming
“she was conscious that she was made for
much more, for her own joy and her own
activity, and she hated to part with even a
little of it.”
The trouble is that in America the line is
drawn so sharply between a man’s work and
his wife’s play.
“I see you women all at play, and I see
the men only when they are wearily watch-
ing you play or playing with you. One hears
so much about business in America. But
with you people it is as much suppressed as
if your husbands or brothers went off to some
other star -every day to do their work, and
came back at night by airships to see their
families. ”
The American wife cannot complain that
in this book she is overlooked. She comes
in for criticism of the sharpest kind, not only
by the author, but by his characters. Says
one shrewd speaker :
“ Do you know what ybu are, women like
you, who have money and freedom to ‘live
your own lives ’ ? You are sexless ; you
haven’t nature’s great apology for the animal
—desire. Such women sin, when they sin,
with their minds. . . . You make marriage a
sort of intelligent and intellectual prosti-
tution—and you develop divorce. ... And
man, what of him ? You leave him to his
too gross temptations —Power and Lust.
Man is given you to protect, and you drive
him into the market-place, where he fights
for your ease, and then relaxes in the refined
sensualities you offer him as the reward for
his toil. . . . They cannot travel apart ; they
must pull together.”
\t>
And Herrick, in a summing-up chapter,
paints this unpleasant, yet quite possible,
picture :
“ In the marriage state, the union of Men
and Women is based on effort in common,
together ; not on sentiment, not on emo-
tion, not on passion, not on individual grati-
fication of sense or soul. The two are
partners in living, and the fruit of their
bodies is but another proof of their partner-
ship. And now .. . Woman, no longer the
Pioneer, no longer the defender of the house,
no longer the economist, blossoms—as what l
The Spender! The Man has the money,
and the Woman has—herself, her body and
her charm. She traffics with man for what
he will give, and she pays with her soul. . . .
She has found something softer than labor
with her hands, easier than the pains of
childbirth —she has found the secret of rule
—mastery over her former master, the slave
ruling her lord. Like the last wife of the
barbarian king, she is heaped with jewels
and served with fine wines and foods, and
lives in a palace—the favorite. .. . Small
comradeship here ! Marriage to this woman
is a state of personal gratification—the best
bargain she can make with mail. . . .
“ And thus, unwittingly, Woman becomes
again in the revolution of the ages what she
was at first, the female creature, the thing
for lust and amusement, —the cherished
slave. Woman in America, splendidly
free and Queen ! What have you done with
the men given into your charge ? Clever,
brilliant, beautiful, —our most shining prize,
—but what have you done for the souls of
the men given into your keeping ? The
answer roars up from the city streets, —the
most material age and the most material
men and the least lovely civilisation on God’s
earth. No longer the fighting compahion at
man’s side, but reaching out for yourselves,
after your own desires, you have become the
slave of the Brute as you were before. And
a neurotic slave. For when Woman is no
longer comrade of man in the struggle, she
is either Nothing or a—but blot the word !
... In the dumb animal hordes, >vho labor
and breed children, lies the future. For
theirs will be the land, when the blond hunter
of the market and his pampered female are
swept into the dust-heap.”
And the remedy for the American woman,
as Herrick shows, is the old and well-tried
remedy of Work—hard Work.
13 Interesting
A Neu) System of English Grammar is the
forgotten work by a forgotten American
humorist named Captain Derby, which Prof.
Lounsbury (whose work, The Standard of
Usage in English, was recently commented
upon in this page) has happily recalled to
the readers of Harper's. Captain Derby was
worried by the fact that, the English
language does not enable a man to tell the
exact truth. The adjective suffers from a
painful lack of precision. We have in that
only three degrees of comparison. But it is
evident that this limited number does
not suffice to represent adequately the
many essential differences in the article
described. Still less can three degrees
of comparison express the varying shades
of feeling about those objects that exist in
the mind of the speaker. Also, an
adjective will convey to one man
ideas dissimilar to those that the same
tired adjective will convey to another.
Really, for exact terminology, the language
is in a shocking state of disrepair. (The
cause of this, as has been pointed out in a
Bulletin editorial, is that the language is a
creation of Private Enterprise.)
v!/
For example, you speak of a girl as beauti-
ful, or very beautiful, or perfectly beautiful.
Now, I have lievqr seen the girl you
speak of; I have seen several beautiful
girls, but I have never found any two of
them equally beautiful. So I really can
have only the vaguest, and not a qualitative,
idea of your girl. Which is annoying to a
strictly mathematical mind. This defect,
inherent in all languages, Captain Derby
proposed to remedy by having all descrip-
tions and epithets marked on a sufficient scale
of comparison. Instead of only three degrees
of comparison, good, better, best (in Aus-
tralia, four: good, better, best, and boshter),
we might take a hundred. The lowest
conceivable amount of any human quality
consistent with its being at all would be in-
dicated by one. Its maximum would be
represented by one hundred. Within this
scale we have a wide range for all shades of
meaning of any given adjective.
‘‘ By prefixing to each epithet a figure
between one and one hundred, the precise
truth in regard to it, as it appears to him
uttering it, would be conveyed to him hear-
ing it. For example, you are askpd about
your health. Instead of replying pretty
well, tolerably well, very well, or some other
ambiguous expression, you would say, bear-
ing in mind that 100 indicates perfect health,
‘I am 15, or 50, or 75, or 90 well.’ By this
means the inquirer learns definitely what he
wants to know. . . The moment this method
of expression comes into general use, we
shall become exact, mathematical, truth-
telling, in the highest degree.”
Lounsbury gives from the defunct humorist
the following example of his method :
“On a 76 fine morning I was 55 slowly
walking down the 33 clean street, when I
chanced to meet the 22 young and 85 charm-
ing Miss Smith. We at once exchanged the
91 usual meteorological observations. ‘lt is
a 76 beautiful day,’ I remarked. ‘lndeed,
it is a 95 beautiful day, ’ she replied, f and I
am 97 glad to meet you, for it is a 99 long
time since I have seen you.’ I felt 77 flat-
tered by words like these coming from a 79
lovely girl, and proceeded to make the 71
usual inquiries about her health. She told
me that it had been 78 poor, but she was
100 glad to say that it was now 87 good.”
It will be noted that this method not only
imparts the facts of the case, but lets
us into the exact thoughts of the two
speakers. It will be observed, too, how this
method brings out the feminine addiction to
superlatives. And from this very fact the
device is sure to fail. Every woman who
uses it will be 100 sure to use the “100”
adjective every time ; and when a 78 loving
girl falls in love with the 81 average bloke
she will address him as the 101 dearest chap
in the world. Which is 32 illogical, and
therefore 99 feminine. So, 63 regretfully,
we must go back to our 81 meagre methods
of comparison. However, it may be 9 worth
while to try the method on the poets. The
poets are 66 vague. How 82 much more
exact is Keats when 100 logically inter-
preted !
64 deep in the 35 shady Sadness of a vale,
17 far sunken from the.4o healthy breath of morn,
83 far from the 77 fiery noon, and eve’s one star,
Sat 79 grey-hair’d Saturn, 42f quiet as a stone.
Pet-chance the 100 self-same song that found a path
Through the 81 sad heart of Ruth, when 92 sick
for home,
She stood id tears amid the 73 alien corn;
The 100 same that 22 oft-times hath
Charm’d 71 magic casements, opening in the foam
Of 69 perilous seas, in 95 faery lands 27 forlorn.
Now we know 100 exactly' what Keats
meant, or rather, what the present writer
thinks that 83 vague bard meant. It seems
a 69 great pity that Keats did not leave a
100 exact mathematic connotation of his 92
vague meaning.

In his rather “precious” preface to
Laughter • and Tears, Frank Morton says some
hard things of Maoriland, the, dour casket
that perforce enshrines this pagan heart.
He terms it nicely, “ this strange land of
cherished solemnities and furtive joys.”
‘ ‘ When we banned Leighton’s ‘ Bath of
Psyche,’and Mr. Bernard Hall’s ‘Sleep,’ as
indecent pictures, we simply did it in order
that the world might be assured' that we
have a sense of humor. ... I expected much
when I came to this country ; but I cannot
yet persuade myself that I am in Arcady.
You Maorilanders have not the Australian’s
cordial spirit, his growing dislike of cant,
his bigness of grasp. You breathe less
deeply, and speak with a colder calculation.
You have fewer apparent vices, and fewer
positive virtues. . . . You are less lavish in
your hospitality, more bitter in your judg-
ments. YoUr indignation, aroused by
slighter causes, is more acrid and old-
womanish. Inde.ed, Maoriland is giving far
too much attention to the cult of the Old
Woman. For myself, I always regarded
your imitation rooster as a most ungainly
and indecent fowl. .. . To some extent,
your Worker’s Paradise is a fool’s paradise. . .
The widely advertised freedom of your in-
stitutions is somewhat of a forced exotic;
for there can be no real freedom in a com-
munity of which the units are not free.
Here and there about this country there is
a growth of bad weeds-—intolerance, espion-
age, black suspicion.”

An Overtaxed System
Extreme Nervousness Completely Cured.
An epoch-making achievement is here dealt
with by Mr. Michael Somers, of 47 Camden
Street, Newtown, who speaks of final success
being inevitable in similar cases if like measures
are adopted, ills narrative takes the following
form :
“ I was sitting down writing a letter one day
in Brisbane, feeling as well as a man could wish to
be, when I suddenly was seized with a dreadful
sensation, and collapsed. Previous to this I had
been leading a very strenuous life, working at top
pressure, and, presumably, I had overdone it.
The strongest systems will break down when
taxed too much, as I have learned to my cost.”
“What happened,” asked our representative,
“ when you were thus attacked ? ”
“ The case looked to be such a serious one that
I was taken to a hospital in an ambulance, and
there I received the very best attention they
could devote to me. Medicine was ordered, and
everything done they could think of. In all my
travels, and 1 have journeyed about a good deal,
I have never met a similar case to my own. It
certainly puzzled the doctors at the institution,
for they could not make head nor tail of the cause
of my affliction.”
“Gan you describe to me the feelings you had,
Mr. Somers ? ”
“Constantly recurring feelings of faintness were
as ti’oublesome as anyth.ng—such horrible sensa-
tions as would make a man think he was going off
at any moment, and, besides, I was afflicted with
the most extreme nervousness it had ever been
my misfortune to come into contact with. When
everybody else was in bed, comfortably asleep,
was the time that my nerve troubles assumed the
greatest dimensions, and I would have given all I
possessed in the world to have been able to doze
off and get rid, for a while, of the harassing
thoughts that disturbed my mind. I could think
of nothing pleasant; every idea that came was
created in an atmosphere of adversity. I felt that
I was hemmed in by hardships and trials of all
kinds, and I think I was, too. Perhaps I was on
the point of falling asleep when, with a little
convulsive jump, I’d start up again. It was a
feeling as if my heart had momentarily stopped,
and there I’d be dreading the same thing
happening again, for fear of what might follow.
That’s the wretched way most nights went by, so
imagine my despondent, worn-out condition
during the day. Suffocating feelings badly affected
my chest, and with difficulty I succeeded in
getting my breath. Meanwhile, the sudde*
jumping sensations in the heart’s region con-
tinued, accompanied with a terrible faintness, which
made me often think that my time had come, and
that I surely was going to die. Sometimes a
feeling came as if I were actually dead in parts-r
a horrible kind of numbness, it was—in the
stomach, chin, and back of the neck, and again
and again I was seized with the terrible conviction
that I was going to drop dead. I was really
afraid to fall asleep in case one of these seizures
would come on, and I would never wake up again.
Oh ! how awful it was. The extreme weakness
and constant twitching of my nerves could not be
checked by anything the doctors prescribed for
me, and I had long since despaired of ever being
about in the world again, when I started, through
reading a testimonial in the paper, taking the
medicine called Clements Tonic.”
“‘ And can you conscientiously state that
Clements Tonic made you as sound as you are
now ? ” queried the writer, looking at Mr. Somers
in amazement.
“ I can, indeed ; though it may seem incredible ;
but my wife and son here can vouch for what I
say being the gospel truth. The symptoms I have
described did not leave me for a long, long time,
and then only through the wonderful agency of
Clements Tonic. That is the truth, as God is my
judge. It seems like a dream to look back upon
—the hospital, the doctors, the numerous medicines
that did not apply to me at all beneficially, the
fainting bouts, the feelings of paralysis in parts of
my body, the unhappy frame of mind 1 was in.
the sleepless nights, the twitching of my every
nerve, the awful heart affections, and the intense
feebleness withal—yes, it seems like a hideous
dream that has passed and gone, for which I have
nothing in the world to thank but Clements
Tonic; so please publish the facts as you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, MICHAEL SOMERS, of 47 Camden Street, Newtown,
in the State of New South Wales, CoinmonrA-alth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Newtown this twenty-
seventh day of February, one thousand nine hundred and.
eight, before me,
JAS. J. THOMSON, J.r.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Darius at the Door.
If you turn Time’s pages o’er,
You will hear their frantic roar—
You will hear the Wades of Sparta cursing Athens
bitterly ;
You will note the Theban’s scowl,
And likewise the Argive’s howl —
And Athens speaking bitterly to Corinth o’er the
sea ;
They were fools, those Greeks of old—
Filled with spite and malice cold,
So they raged and cursed and wrangled all along
their ancient shore ;
When at last the Persian came,
They were crippled, halt and lame—
They were weak, alas ! and weary when his Axe
Burst in the Door !
Yes, that fool State-Frighter crew
Knew not what nor which to do—
Athens wailed'for help from Sparta in those days
long dead and gone :
“Spartans, help us !” rang their cry,
“ Help us quickly or we. die ! ”
But alas ! They fought without them on the field
of Marathon !
Came no aid at Athens’ call,
So its legion, weak and small,
Faced alone the ranks of Asia in that hour of
conflict sore ;
There was not a friend around
For the city violet-crowned.
Envy scowled and malice mocked them when the
Foe was at the Door.
’Twas a swift courageous band
That preserved the Grecian land —
’Twas their strength and grit that snatched it
from the Asiatics’ sway ;
’Twas one city—only one,
That took down its ancient gun,
And the flood that surges southward in this later
fighting day :
Met the old Athenian tar,
While the wranglers lagged afar,
Whilst the Theban sat disgruntled Athens bled
in days of yore ;
Just one struggling State alone
Made the Brown Man grieve and moan r
And drove the hairy Persian and his Axe from
Europe’s Door. . i j
. f
Now, upon this country’s coast
The State-Frighters are a host,
Hear the Wades and Kidstons shouting at Aus-
tralia bitterly ;
See the Insects, how they scowl.
How they shriek “ Secesh ” and howl— |
But remember ancient Sparta cursing Athens o’er
the sea !
Like those foolish Greeks of old,
Our State-Frighters whine and scold —
Hear them bellow, rave and wrangle all along
Australia’s shore;
When at last the foernan comes
Will ye beat Australia’s drums —
Will he find ye squalling vainly when his Axe
Bursts in the Door ?
VIC.
GRANT HERYEY.
Maisie.
Maisie surfs and flirts with ns
Daily, save on Wednesdays,
Brown and slim and frivolous—
But she’s grave on Wednesdays ;
Grave and dressed and orthodox,
In tan shoes and cotton frocks ;
We are graceless vagabonds
Of the wave on Wednesdays.
Maisie’s lavish smiles are more
Fugitive on Wednesdays,
Rash caprices of the shore
We forgive on Wednesdays ;
Surf-wet, she nor cares nor knows
Who may count her naked toes,
But belief in fashion is
Positive on Wednesdays.
Sunbrowned Maisie uptown is
Smart enough on Weduesdays ;
Dines on dainty anchovies
And such stuff on Wednesdays ;
Though her soft skin, brown and salt
As the seaweed, hath no fault,
Maisie always carries her
Powder-puff on Wednesdays.
Maisie’s hat and skirt are de
Gustibus on Wednesdays,
Once uptown no thought does she
Waste on us on Wednesdays ;
Never did a two-piece suit
In the surf-line look so cute !
But ’tis unavailing to
Make a fuss on Wednesdays.
Uptown, groomed and glib of speech,
Lurks for Maisie, Wednesdays ;
Eyes that beckon on the beach
Seem too lazy, Wednesdays ;
Dazed admirers either hand
Watch her walk the Block and Strand ;
Sidelong glances, teasing looks,
Drive them crazy, Wednesdays.
Maisie, with her dear mamma,
Does the Block on Wednesdays;
In the surf she laughs—but ah,
Loves to mock on Wednesdays !
Maisie, with her ma and me,
Lunches at the A.B.C. —
Folly looks too good to live,
In a frock, on Wednesdays !
N.s.w.
W. M. WHITNEY.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 4, 1909.
PniOK Gd
THE NEWEST IDEA OF A FEDERAL CITY.
The Sydney Telegraph cordially recommends a certain place near Canberra as a capital site on account of the excellent shooting and fishing to
be obtained there. Apparently the paper thinks that the wild animals will remain even after the city is built, and that the metropolis of
Australia will lead a sporting outdoor life like this.

Jloiv to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiik Bulletin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regal’d to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to '
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and. even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojlicials. 'The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 4, 1909.
THE NORTHERN TERRITORY AND
OTHER LARGE MATTERS.
Australia i 8 a large country, though quite a
number of its inhabitants have hardly discovered
its existence yet, and regard it, in a vague sort of
way, as a suburb of Peckham Rye, England.
A vast number of small men infest this very
large country, and neither they nor their ideas fit
their surroundings. A general impression pre-
vails that Melbourne is at the south end of this
big continent, and that Sydney is half-way up the
side wall of it, and that Brisbane is away up
north near the roof, and that Adelaide is half-way
along the base-line. And out of this geographical
misconception arise State Frights, a small view
of things, a ridiculous inability to realise the size :
of the dangers ahead of us or the greatness of the
work to be done, an unduly limited idea of
the importance of our country, and a ridiculously
inflated conception of the importance of
people like Bent and Murray and Jacky
Evans and Wade and Kidston, who clatter
in a small way in a small corner of the
big country. So, by way of a text for this
homily, The Bulletin attaches three maps. The
first represents Australia and Tasmania as they
are. The second, which looks an almost imper-
ceptible fraction smaller, represents the Common-
wealth as it would be if Sydney, Melbourne,
Adelaide, Hobart, Launceston, Bendigo, Ballarat,
and most of N.S.W.’s and all Victoria’s and
Tasmania’s big provincial towns and 2| complete
States were cut out of it. The third, which
shows a rather more perceptible slice hacked off
the outline, explains what the Commonwealth
would still be if all Tasmania, Victoria, and the
habitable part of N.S.W., and the most fertile sec-
tion of S.A. and a bit of Queensland were cut out —
what it would be without Brisbane, Newcastle,
Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, Hobart, Launces-
ton, and almost every other place that we hear of
in a frequent sort of way. The vanishment of all
these places seems to leave the Commonwealth
almost unimpaired in point of size. Yet
that insignificant-looking excision would cut
away almost the entire population ; it would shift
five State Premiers— Kidston, Wade, Murray,
Evans and Price —off the scene; it would prac-
tically hack away the whole State Frights move-
ment, and the State “ grievances,” and the papers
which support the movement and devise the
grievances; it would shift the Federal capital
question, and the row about the division of the
tariff revenue, and the Broken Hill lock-out, and
Tom Mann, and all manner of disturbances. Yet
with all these gone the Commonwealth would
still remain—just diminished enough to attract
attention. And if a fourth map were made, cut-
ting off Perth as well as all the other capitals,
still the Commonwealth would remain very little
impaired.
• • * *
It is because of Map 111. that The Bulletin
urges this community to take the Large Austra-
lian View of things. In the first place, it is on
that account that it urges the immediate
taking over of the Northern Territory, the build-
ing of the transcontinental railway by the shortest
possible route, and the establishment of a strenu-
ous Federal policy of immigration and land settle-
ment in the North. When one considers how
little difference the excision of all the imposing
distance from Melbourne to Brisbane makes upon
the stupendous aggregate distances of Australia,
it becomes possible to realise how very far off is our
neglected northern outpost, and how essential it
is to' garrison it with a strong, faithful white
population, and to place efficient sentinels there
No. I. —This is the
Commonwealth.
to keep an eye on the Beyond. Nothing that the
little concentrated patch of population in the
South-West could do under present circumstances
would serve to dislodge an enemy planted in the
empty North ; there must be a considerable armed
population on the spot, and a railway. Without
them an Asiatic enemy could land a whole series
of semi-military colonies there, and he could
build towns and erect fortifications, and lay out
farms, and then set himself to appease Britain and
secure its assent to his occupation with the promise
that the settlers would be loyal and obedient sub-
jects and good taxpayers. And as Britain owns the
Territory still —this fool country having so far
been too tired to take it over when offered—the
decision would rest wholly with Britain. In the
absence of white Australian settlers the law of
“effective occupation’’ is against us ; and in the
absence of a transcontinental railway, our little
army, taking feeble walking exercise at the
farthest end of the scheme of things, could do
nothing.
* • • *
It is also partly because of Map 111. that The
Bulletin has no connection whatsoever with the
State Frights push. There are other reasons, but
Map 111. is a reason in itself. The whole State
Frights movement can be cut out of the plan or
diagram of Australia and yet leave it looking very
little smaller than before. That movement
is merely the discordant barking of one
corner of the atlas —the small, half-built
Present baying at the illimitable Future.
And for the same reason The Bulletin is
emphatically opposed to the absurd demands of the
State Premiers as to the division of the tariff
revenue after the expiration of the Braddon
clause. William Ltne’s proposal that the Com-
monwealth should keep the whole tariff revenue,
and relieve the States of their debts—the offer
which the State Premiers rejected with scorn—
was in reality excessively liberal ; the State Pre-
miers’ idea that they should have the bulk of the
revenue for certain, and a mortgage on all the rest,
was insanely ridiculous. The Commonwealth
stands for most of that huge section of Australia
which remains on Map ill. For the safety of
Australia that great region requires railways,
towns, population, defence requires almost
everything that it hasn’t got. If it is to get these
things in any reasonable time the Australian
Government must take a hand, for the feeble, im-
pecunious little Governments at Brisbane, Perth,
and Adelaide, away in the South, can do
very little. Therefore, the Commonwealth wants
money—much money. There are other claims to
be considered besides those of the bit of Aus-
tralia which makes so little visible difference when
it is cut right off the map, though it makes so
No. II.—This is the Commonwealth as it would
be if Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, Launceston,
Hobart, and a multitude of small towns and 2½
States were cut off. There is still a good deal left.
much noise that it is often mistaken for all Aus-
tralia—its Past and Present and Future, and the
entire show generally.
* * * *
Map 111. is an argument for many other things
as well. It represents the practically-unpopulated
Australia, while the little bit that is cut off repre-
sents the thinly-populated Australia. Thestupend-
ousarea expounds more eloquently than anything
else could the need for universal military training
and the insensate folly of those who oppose that
beneficent idea. Britain has already given us
abundant warning to provide for our own defence,
and when the men are so few and the square
miles so many—Britain’s whole army, even
if multiplied by ten, would only be a lost
circumstance in a corner of it—it is plain
that the only sane or sensible precaution we
can take is to train every man we have, and
thus make the best of a defence which, even at
the best, will be very poor. Further, the same
sketch of our continent shrieks aloud for a navy
of our own. Even to regular coastal travellers
the distance from Adelaide to Sydney seems a
very long stretch ; yet all that expanse of coast
can be cut out, and so much remains that the
excision makes but little difference. If a really
serviceable British squadron did come out here to
deal with an enemy, it might chase him vainly
round that stupendous coast-line for weeks, and
by that time, in all probability, the ordinary Euro-
pean complication would call it back to British
waters to help guarantee Bull’s own safety.
Only a squadron that could be relied upon to be
here, to stay here, and to look after the enemy to
the last, could provide for the safety of a country
which suffers from such an excess of the long wash
of Australasian seas.
* * * *
In addition to these things Map 111. represents
a voice crying in the wilderness for the policy of
the Unification and Reconstruction party. That
party urges a temporary unification —a temporary
handing over to the Australian Parliament of all
authority, all public services, all debts and assets
and sundries. Then it is to be the business of the
Parliament to reconstruct the country, dividing it
into smaller States, each with its own capital and
its own sane little Democratic Legislature of one
House, endowed with such powers and such assets
as it seems wise that the States should possess.
Australia requires subdivision and reconstruction
almost as badly as it requires population. The
cutting of a humble fragment off the south-east
corner of Australia’s page in the atlas obliterates
five out of the six State capitals, as well as the
proposed Federal capital site. If a line
were drawn from Brisbane to Perth it
would obliterate all the State capitals, and
yet - four-fifths, or possibly five-sixths, of
Australia would remain. There is a huddle of
capitals in the extreme south of Australia—in
fact, so closely are they huddled towards the
south coast that that line drawn from a little north
of Brisbane to a little north of Perth nearly falls
into the Southern Ocean at one part of its career.
And from these far south capitals the requirements
of the country further north are considered through
long-range telescopes till about the centre of
Australia is reached. Beyond that the curvature
of the earth makes it impossible to see anything.
A subdivision into comparatively small States,
each with its own State capital, would at least
give the places north of the big curve in the earth
a chance to receive some consideration. Further-
more, Map 111. is a whole essay on the need for a
vigorous white immigration policy. When it is
remembered that the cutting of a mere fragment
out of the outline of Australia leaves the gigantic
balance of a con tinen t with only aboulBoo,ooo people
in all its stupendous length and bread th, the demand
for the importation of the capable white stranger
becomes very evident. In fact, there is in that
little diagram of Australia the basis of a
complete, democratic, all-Australian policy. It
shows a little population brawling in a little corner
of a continent, and mistaking it for the whole
country. Down in that corner there is Australia’s
little network of railways, and its little State
Frights push, and its little capital site shindy, and
No. III.—This is the Commonwealth as it would
he if Brisbane, Newcastle, Sydney, Melbourne,
Adelaide, Launceston, Hobart, and 52 out of
Australia’s 71 provincial towns of 3000 popula-
tion and upwards were cut out, along with two
whole States and portions of three more. The
great bulk of the Commonwealth yet remains.
there is also the little State to which a big hairy
man .with a little mind once proposed to annex
the name of the whole country. To be sure the
little corner is a big enough corner by comparison
with most European countries, but as compared
with Australia, which is very large, the corner is
wretchedly small. That is why Australia
requires men with large ideas. It is such a vast
country that a small idea gets lost in it like a germ
in a mammoth’s stomach.
AGAIN THE WARNING.
The amount of aid which Australia can expect from the
Motherland is necessarily limited, and Australia must be
largely dependent upon herself for defence.— Ex-Governor -
Oeneral Northcote.
If Australia’s day of trouble finds it unprepared
it certainly won’t be able to say that it didn’t get
abundant warning. The British Government and
the British press used to hint the news that this
country must not depend on Britain for defence.
Now it puts up eminent public men to yell the
information in Australia’s hairy and unheeding
ear. The other day it was ex-Prime Minister
Rosebery who told us clearly and emphatically
that Britain’s position is now such that the guard-
ing of its own isles against foreign aggression
has become a sufficient task to absorb the
Gold Country’s energies, and with that busi-
ness on hand nothing that could happen to
Australia would justify Britain in sending
away a fleet to this country’s assistance. This
time it is ex-Governor-General Northcote who
mentions that, so far as we are concerned, Britain
is a broken reed to depend upon, and that we
must look to our own defence. With warnings
like these coming so thick and fast from obviously
inspired sources, one might have expected to hear
that the Fisher Government had summoned Par-
liament together in haste, and that it proposed in
an emergency session to attempt to deal with
some at least of the five great questions which
bear upon defence—universal military training,
the construction of an Australian navy to be built
in Australian dockyards, the taking over of the
Northern Territory, the building of the two
transcontinental railways, and the peopling of
the empty North with white immigrants of our
own race. A country which has really no de-
fence of its own, which has less than two
people to the square mile wherewith to con-
struct a defence, and which has been told
on good authority that all its dreams of being
absolutely protected by the mother-land are mere
phantasies, and that it is little better than a
rioh hulk adrift on the political waters, and
waiting for the first pirate to come along and
take possession—a country in such a position
might be excused if it called an emergency session
of Parliament to consider things. But nothing of
the kind has happened. There hasn’t even been
a serious ripple on the surface of things. The
only emergency session was that of the Cricket
Board of Control.
* * W *
The dreadful party of which Willy Kelly,
M.H.R., is a representative, tries to believe that
Britain doesn’t mean what it says in this matter,
and that its great naval strength will assuredly
come to our assistance if an Asiatic or other
Power attacks us. Yet it is only necessary to con-
sider again the size of the probable enemy whose
existence ties Britain’s ships to its own shores in
order to realise the absurdity of the Willy Kelly
belief. Germany has nearly 50 per cent, more
people than Britain, and its population is increas-
ing fully twiceasquickly. Itsarmy is vastly stronger
than Britain’s, yet it costs hardly any more. Its
total revenues are more than twice as large as
Britain’s. The six Dreadnoughts which are to be
launched in 1911 (Britain only launches two that
year) are expected to give Germany an actual
preponderance in big new fighting ships. Ger-
many is believed to be already ahead of Britain in
manufactures certainly it now has double
Britain’s output of iron and steel. The two
greatest steamship lines in the world are both
German—the largest German steamship co. owns
more than twice the tonnage of the largest British
one. Germany has £633,000,000 in its savings
banks against Britain’s £204,000,000. And all these
points of superiority have been gained in twenty
years, under high Protection and a fairly sensible
land policy; if the progress continues, another
ten or twenty years should see amazing results.
In addition to everything else there is the feel-
ing that Germany’s turn is coming. Greece,
Turkey, Italy, Spain, Portugal, France,
Holland, Denmark, Sweden, Russia, Britain
—each one of them has had its hour as
a great conquering and colonising power, and
on the principle that every dog—especially every
large dog—gets its day, there is a prevalent senti-
ment that Germany’s day must be near. It
seems to be in the nature of things that it should.
And, if a fight comes, Britain must fight at a
serious disadvantage, for its almost complete de-
pendence on foreign countries for its food supplies
makes it necessary to defend, not only itself, but
the sea highways along which its victuals plod
wearily to the shores of Bull. In such circum-
stances it is a very sanguine person who is pre-
pared to rest on the theory that Britain doesn’t
mean what it says when it warns Australia to
make ready for its own defence.
■* * * •
The Anglo-Jap alliance expires in six years. It
is already known that there is little probability of
its renewal, and it may suffer breakage before
1915. If the amazing growth of Germany con-
tinues, John Bull, whose hands are already full
of trouble, is likely about 1915 to find them still
fuller, and to be even less able to aid us than
he is now. Japan is already doing things
which suggest a clearing of the way for
action. It has fixed up treaties with France,
Russia and the United States, and thus done all
that is possible to keep these nations quiet, and to
assure them that there is no need to maintain sea
armaments in the Pacific, which is becoming more
and more a Jap lake. This in itself is a powerful
hint to Australia—in fact, Australia is getting so
many hints, one way and another, that it
must depend on its own resources, and that it may
need these resources very soon —that its state of
somnolence is almost unbelievable. Of course,
there may, after all, be ?some assistance to be
obtained from outside in the day of trouble, but
the kind of assistance that seems most probable
would be almost as bad as none at all. Britain
may spare us 100,000 or 200,000 nigger troops from
India, and, if they secure us against conquest, it
is difficult to see how they can be prevented from
settling down in this country when their period of
service expires, should they feel that way inclined.
Britain brought a nigger army to Europe in 1878,
which is something in the way of a precedent.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A New Species of Gag.
A. N. Barnett, S.M., after committing T.
Mann and four others to take their trial on a
charge of rioting at Broken Hill, granted Mann
bail on condition that he took no further part in
the strike or lockout proceedings. Subsequently,
upon Mann refusing to tic himself down to any
such condition, the magistrate chopped the tail
off his remarkable dictum, and said that Mann
might spout if he wanted to so long as he didn’t
spout in N. S. Wales. The Bulletin probably puts
less value on Mann’s spouting than even A. N.
Barnett, S.M., does; it would silence him, if it
could, by the simple process of persuading the
workers to leave off listening to his long-winded
and impracticable and out-of-place foolishness.
But Barnett’s plan is full of danger. Mann’s
business is that of an organiser; rightly or
wrongly the men who boss the Broken Hill unions
think that he is a good organiser, and they have
hired him for that purpose. What Barnett,
S.M., decides is therefore that Mann shall not be
permitted to carry on his business. With Mann’s
lawful business asan organiser neither Barnett nor
the law is in the least degree concerned. In fact,
the case had hardly started before Barnett him-
self announced that he had nothing to do with a
strike or a lockout—all he had to do with was an
alleged riot. Yet though he held that he had got
nothing to do with a strike or a lockout while the
case was on, directly it was over he took the
opportunity of muzzling the strike leader. It
wasn’t merely a matter of binding him down
not to riot any more, assuming that he had
rioted once; that, in fact, might have been a
perfectly reasonable thing to do. The situation is
exactly the same as if an accountant, charged with
larceny which he denies, were granted bail on
condition that he didn’t do any more accounting ;
or as if a newspaper publisher, charged with a
libel which he repudiates, were granted bail on
condition that he left off publishing his paper
until after the trial. If it is possible for Mann to
be silenced in this way, it ought to be possible to
silence any newspaper or any politician during a
general election or any other crisis; and
that is not a power which ought to lie in
any magistrate’s hands. Whether Barnett
acted under instructions from, or after con-
sultation with, the Crown Law Office which
has C. G. Wade for its temporary head hasn’t
yet transpired; but as it is just the sort of
decision at which C. G. Wade would be likely to
arrive, somebody ought to take the earliest oppor-
tunity in Parliament to find out where the
responsibility lies. In any case,. whether Barnett
or Wade was responsible, Parliament ought to
make an opportunity to express its opinion on the
subject. And when the time comes, it is to be
hoped that it will forget that it was T. Mann who
was gagged, and will remember that next time,
on this precedent, it will probably be somebody
whom the country cannot so well afford to have
muzzled.


A Result of Abiding by the Law.
It seems to be the general conviction that one of
the most shocking miscarriages of justice which
N.S.W. has known in recent years occurred last
week, when Neil Hansen, master of the Dun-
more, had his certificate suspended for 12 months
by the N.S.W. Marine Court in connection with
the naval disaster that took place in Sydney har-
bor on January 5. There is no question about the
facts. The Dunmore was rounding Mrs. Mac-
quarie’s Chair on its proper course, en route to a
berth on the west side of Woolloomooloo Bay.
The naval launch, with a pinnace in tow, was
heading towards the Dunmore on its way to the
Man o’ War Steps. Now, Article 19 of the Regu-
lations says this:—
When two vessels are crossing so as to involve risk of
collision, the vessel which has the other on its own star-
board side shall keep out of the way of the other.
It was not disputed by anyone that the launch
had the Dunmore on its starboard bow. Article
21 lays down that—
Where one of two vessels is to keep out of the way, the
OTHER SHALL KEEP ITS COURSE AND SPEED.
The Dunmore obeyed the law. It kept its course
and speed, assuming, as it had a right to assume,
that the on-coming launch would observe the rule
of the road and “keep out of the way.” The
launch didn’t. When almost under the big ship’s
bows it cut across to port, and the holocaust
began. It is assumed by a certain class of man on
the tram (and the Marine Court had seemingly a
vague idea to the same effect at the back of its
rather ancient head) that Hansen should have
had a divine inspiration to the effect that the
naval men meant to go fanti and cross him, and
that he should have ported his helm hard in con-
sequence. Failing a vehement message from
Above, it is the last thing a careful seaman who
knew his business would do. And even if he
obeyed a message from Heaven in this regard, he
would still be liable for breaking the law of
N.S.W. Marsden, commenting on Article 27,
uses these words of horse sense:—
A vessel is not justified in departing from the Regula-
tions because it fears that the other ship Will not comply
with them. In a case decided under the Trinity Rules of
1840 Dr. Lushington thus laid down the general principle :
“ I cannot conceive that anything would be more likely
to lead to mischievous consequences than to suppose that
a vessel whose duty it is to keep its course should antici-
pate that another vessel will not give way, and so give
way itself. The consequence would be that there would
be no certainty. The certainty which results from ad-
herence to general rules is, in my opinion, absolutely
essential to the safety of navigation,
Barrister Broomfield put the case for Hansen
thus succinctly and correctly :
What Hansen did was absolutely correct, and what he
was bound to do. The collision could have been avoided
at any time if the launch had ported its helm, which was
what it should have done. If the Dunmore had star-
boarded, and the launch had done what it should have
done, ported, and there was a collision, the master of the
Dunmore would have been solely to blame under the
Regulations, which set out that once a master saw on his
port side a vessel crossing his bows he was bound to keep
his course and speed. The other boat was entitled to
suppose he was going to keep his course and speed, and
entitled to shape its own course accordingly. In this case
the master of the Dunmore had done nothing negligently,
for what he did he would be bound to do again in similar
circumstances.
Judge Backhouse (President of the Marine Court)
went out of his way, at this stage, to say :
The launch could have cleared you any time he liked,
that is certain.
Yet a few minutes later this amazing Court was
employed taking away for a year the livelihood
of a man who was not aboard the guilty launch.
The sole reason given by the Marine Court for
condemning Hansen is contained in these words
of Judge Backhouse’s judgment
The Court is not able to understand how the collision
could have taken place if a proper lookout had been
kept on the Dunmore, and for that default the master’s
certificate has been suspended for the period named.
Yet two men belonging to the naval launch and
five men on board the Dunmore swore that the
mate —himself a certificated master —was keeping
a lookout on the forecastle head of the Dunmore
when the collision occurred. Hansen has
apparently been sacrificed to the bad old super-
stition that the British navy can never be wholly
in the wrong under any circumstances. It is the
only explanation of the wild travesty of justice
that has smitten that unlucky mariner. If the
judgment isn’t reversed by a higher Court,
N.S.W. will be advised to burn or dynamite its
text-books on maritime law, and stay ashore. For
the water within Mother’s jurisdiction will not be
a safe place for any man to adventure upon.

Some Animals and a Federal Capital.
The person who is searching Yassberra to see if it
has a Federal capital site concealed about it, has
dug up a splendid argument in favor of placing
the headquarters of a continent within that dis-
tressful area. The Sydney Telegraph correspond-
ent—who seems to have been at the capital site
detective’s elbow drew his attention to the
alleged ad vantages of something called the Warroo
site, and the lone searcher in the wilderness said
that it would be a fine place for the capital on
account of the fishing and shooting to be got
thereabouts. This is the result of sending a man
with a one-horse-power imagination on one of the
biggest quests in history—the selection of a
place which is to be for all time the head-
quarters of a whole continent. The indivi-
dual now adrift in the neighborhood of
Yass is perspiring under a misapprehension
and should be recalled. Australia doesn’t want a
good spot for a hunting lodge, or a tourists’ pre-
varication bureau, or even a blacks’ camp. The
amounts of rabbits, fish, white elephants, or
giraffes to be shot, hooked, snared, chloroformed,
or stunned thereabouts doesn’t count in this
matter. Even if they are there in shoals and
herds at the present time, they will avaunt to the
wilderness as soon as a big city starts to uprear
itself in their vicinity. Probably enough the site
of London was originally selected on account of
the splendid bear-hunting to be had in White-
chapel; if so, the Telegraph’s remarks only
tend to show that that paper is to-day in just
the same mental state as the early Briton was
over 2000 years ago. Anyhow the pioneers of the
Federal city aren’t going down there with rifles
and fishing lines. They are going with dynamos
and machinery, bricks, mortar, girders, railway
engines, typewriters, and the whole kit of civilisa-
tion. They have no delusion to the effect that,
because the kangaroo is there now, he will stop
there when all this machinery arrives, and breed
freely in the main street right in the middle of
the tram-line, and be available to be shot from the
windows of Parliament House. To drag a heap
of animals into the discussion of a subject of this
size indicates either colossal stupidity or a lack of
reverence that deserves to be sternly discouraged.

The Price of Tory Support.
The disastrous applause of the Tory press—that
applause which leadeth unto destruction —con-
tinues to fall heavily on the Fisher Government.
Minister Mahon purchased the oordial approval -
of the backwash papers when he did his remark-
able act of adulation to N.S.W. State Premier
Wade, and assured him that he represented a
State which stands for 37 per cent, of the Com-
monwealth’s population, and the Australian
Government can’t afford to ignore it. N.S. vN .
probably stands for 37 per cent, of the Australian
population, and for that reason it has 27 Represent-
atives and six Senators to look after its inter-
ests, and they see that it is not ignored, nor
has anyone ever attempted to ignore it.
Mahon’s statement was a gross insult-by-impli-
cation to the Australian Parliament, conveying, as
it did, the impression that though the otherbtates
were quite safe in the hands of their lederal
members, N.S.W. required the constant super-
vision qf State Premier Wade to save its
Federal interests; and when Mahon carried
his humble assurance to the feet of Wade,
who has nothing at all to do with the Federal
affairs of N.S.W., it was a foolish case of pan-
dering to the vanity of a bumptious and
interfering nuisance. Still it fetched trie
applause of the Tory and State Frights publica-
tions. So did Attorney-General Hughes’ needless
repudiation of the Unification and Reconstruc-
tion policy. And last week the Hebald greased
the Federal Labor party in a leading article for
its shameful reversal of attitude over the I ederal
capital question, and its complete surrender to
Wade and his Congested Population craze, and
assured it that it was a great and intelligent crowd
which (by climbing down) had solved a difficulty
that had defied all previous Australian Govern-
ments ; and then the Herald explained that the
Fisher Government could earn its further ap-
plause and support if it did another complete sur-
render to the State Frights crowd over the divi-
sion of the tariff revenue. AndtheFiSHEßMinistry
is getting so accustomed now to the applause of
the Tory press that this surrender, though
it may look impossible, doesn’t look so
impossible as it should do. The ques-
tion of the division of the surplus stands
to-day where it was left when the State Premiers
foolishly rejected William Lyne’s handsome
offer. After the end of next year the whole Cus-
toms and Excise revenue can be legally retained
by the Commonwealth. William Lyne pro-
posed that the States should receive a permanent
fixed subsidy equal to £7,000,000 a year to begin
with, and rising by instalments to nearly
£9,000,000 a year. This subsidy would about
equal what they have been receiving, but, instead
of being an uncertain thing, dependent on the
rise or fall of the tariff receipts —and just now
they are falling heavily—it would be definitely
guaranteed. The balance, whether large or
small, the Commonwealth was to retain. Now
the Commonwealth has (1) to pay old-age
pensions ; (2) to take over the Northern Territory,
open it up with roads, make the land available for
settlement, and bring in a vast number of
desirable white immigrants to populate it and
hold it against the Asiatic; (3) to pay interest
on the Territory’s debt; (4) to find £2,000,000
for postal and telegraph works ; (5) to build two
immense transcontinental railways ; (6) to provide
as best it can military and naval defence for a
country almost as large as all Europe ; (7) to create
a Federal capital and build a railway to connect
it with the coast. With these enormous responsi-
bilities ahead, Lyne’s offer to the States was
ridiculously lavish, yet the cantankerous State
Premiers threw it aside with scorn and rage.
Their demand was for something like £7,000,000
a year certain—this amount to be paid even
if the tariff didn’t yield £7,ooo,ooo—and for at
least half the remainder of the Customs and Excise
revenue. It was a deliberate attempt to cripple
and beggar the Commonwealth, and if the State
Premiers’ scheme were adopted, the Northern
Territory, the transcontinental railways, postal
and telegraph extension and defence would
have to go overboard in one disastrous
act. Now the Herald hints broadly that this
wholesale surrender is the price at which the Tory
press will give further support to the Fisher
Government. The surrender of the interests of
Australia over the Federal capital matter got it
enough Tory support to carry it into recess, and
getting there meant several months’ assurance of
office. Another and a bigger surrender, it appears,
might so far purchase the forbearance of Toryism
as to enable the Fisher Ministry to get along as
far as the General Election. The Tory press puts
the price of its support pretty high.

The State to the Rescue.
Anti-Sosh rose up and rent its frock-coat, figur-
atively speaking, and jumped on its plug hat in
agony when the Fisher Government’s Common-
wealth horse-breeding proposals were made clear to
it last week :
T. I. Campbell, secretary of the Farmers’ and
Settlers’ Association, urged that the breeding of
military horseflesh should be left to Private En-
terprise. He told the papers that he could not con-
ceive that the Government would adopt any such
scheme as a Commonwealth Horse-breeding Farm
“ unless,” said he in a jocular way, “ they want to make
use of the Federal capital site.” He could not see how it
would b ' possible for a Government to undertake horse-
breeding. It was not a system that could be followed with
any degree of certainty. It might happen that the breeds
would be altogether unsuitable for Government require-
ments. Fifty machines might be made alike, but there
could be no 50 horses bred alike.
Which is tragic rot of the most flaming variety.
The Russian and Austrian Governments have
been breeding horses for military purposes,
with success, for years. The Russian Govern-
ment gave British racing-owner J. Gubbins
£30,000 for Galtee More (winner of the ’97 Derby)
with the object of breeding from that fine
horse. The Argentine Government bought Orme,
and the French Government Flying Fox, for the
same purpose. As to the wild allegation that
horse-breeding is “ not a system that can be fol-
lowed with any degree of certainty,” it may be
said that the knowledgable breeder’s power to get
an unlimited humber of horses so near to type as
not to matter is a scientific, social, and every other
sort of truism. A good example in point is the
American trotting-horse. The trotter has evolved
from the Mambrino stallion Messenger in a little
over a century. In conformation and paces
this horse is sui generis. So with the Eng-
lish thoroughbred hackney, the polo pony,
the Clydesdale, and the Irish hunter. The
Commonwealth horse-breeding proposals are
the direct result of Private Enterprise’s
ghastly failure of late years to produce a
fair-class weight-carrying charger at anything
like a reasonable price. The horse has been
treated in most of the States with the lavish in-
difference that a State Premier accords to loan
moneys. In N.S.W. things have reached such a
pass that shopkeepers have to lend or hire their
van horses to the local cavalrymen on parade days.
If they didn’t oblige in this way Ma’s cavalry de-
fenders would have to go on parade aboard tri-
cycles or on foot. It was said by the morning
papers of acrack Light Horse regiment which came
from the back blocks to camp at Liverpool six
months ago that “not one unit in ten was decently
mounted. The cause of this state of things in a
country whose “ general utility ” horses were once
world-famed is a matter of notoriety among horsey
men. The S’African war denuded Australia of a big
proportion of its remounts. The Russ-Jap disturb-
ance denuded itsomemore. And eversince a steady
stream of high-class cavalry and mounted infantry
stock, in addition to blood sires and dams, has
been flowing Asiawards and to the Philippines.
Private Enterprise on this side has assisted the
movement with all its might. It has welcomed
the Jap and other foreign horse-buyers with warm
hand-grips and iced drinks. For ten years or
more individualism has done magnificently out of
its horse deals. Now the aftermath is at hand.
The jamboree is coming to an end. The country’s
equine capital is about exhausted. The big
stations are overrun with “weeds” —the rejected
of the oversea veterinary experts —and the progeny
thereof. There is no reason to assume that the
Government is going to administer its horse-breed-
ing establishment one whit less satisfactorily than
it administers the trams, the railways, the gaols,
the asylums, and the various other Socialistic
concerns under its control. The production of an
unfailing stream, year by year, of sound weight-
carrying horses for home use is an affair of national
urgency. Private Enterprise has tried its hand
at the job and made a failure of it, mainly because
its greed of present gain has led it to trade off the
stock that should have been retained for breeding
purposes. In a manner of speaking, it has sold
the horse that laid the golden foal. It is the
State’s bounden duty to step in and rectify
matters.
Too Sane To Be True.
At Clontarf (9/11/’08) Bill Wilks, Freetectionist
and Orangeman, said this :
But to-day we have something else to do than look back
at the Battle of the Boyne. We have to start a battle of
our own. The great crowd in front of me is crammed
with a great sentiment, which is only waiting an oppor-
tunity to burst all bonds. . . . Orangemen do not come
together merely to show their gratification over victories
earned 200 years ago. I believe they have been waiting
for some prominent, courageous statement from their
grand-masters. If something is not done within the next
two months I am a most disappointed man.
It was William’s Tom MANN-like way of fore-
shadowing a change in the Yellow Pup’s political
attitude. The two months have elapsed, the
Orange battle-programme has been drawn up, and
at Clifton Gardens (23/l/’O9) Parson Dill Macky
read it out to the company assembled. Consider
it:—
(1) So far, and just so far, as Continental Socialism
denies religion and works for atheism —Anti-Socialism.
(2) To return to the worker as nearly as may be the full
product of his labor.
These two are the main principles on which the planks
rest. Two minor principles are :
(3) For defence on land—an army raised in Australia ;
the effective training of Australians to defend themselves
if necessary.
[lt is understood, remarks S. M. Hbrald (25/l/’O9)
apropos principle No. 3, that there is a section of the
Orange authorities which holds that the only training
which can be effective is compulsory.]
(4) For defence on sea—a subsidy to the Royal Navy.
Eliminate the subsidy foolishness, and the pro-
gramme looks promising, though vague. Prin-
ciple 1 means nothing if it doesn’t mean that the
DILL-ites propose to adopt the Labor attitude
towards industrial matters in its entirety. ‘ ‘ Con-
tinental” Sosh, the “denial of religion,” and
“ works ” on behalf of “ Atheism,” are things no
sane Laborite in this country bothers his head
about. Principle 2is a further affirmation of the
desirability of the policy G. Reid and the Watch-
man held to be Anarchy in 1906. Principle 3is
cheeringly sane and patriotic, issuing from the
anti-Australian source it does. The doubtful
question is—how much of the programme is likely
to be honestly striven for by its authors when the
elections come again ? Hitherto the party has bar-
racked with misguided fervor for the cause of the
well-to-do importer, of the hoggish land-mono-
polist, and of the opulent absentee person. It
hasn’t worried about the fact that 90 per
cent, of its members, sympathisers and potential
sympathisers are of the poorer classes. It has
just gone blindly for the wealthy man’s cause in
the insane belief that by so doing it has been
delivering a series of shocks to the affluent Pope.
Seemingly the party has realised in some degree
the unreason of its past conduct. But a brace of
conflicting interests divide it. The Lodges need
urgently to swell their membership tallies, which
consist for the most part of old women, older
men and cranky fanatics. The only hope
they have of attracting desirable recruits is
to display such a democratic programme
as shall appeal to the normal Australian
working-man—the type, that is, who doesn’t want
the walls of Derry dragged into his legislative
ideals, and who aims at industrial rather than
Vatican reform. But the party is bossed by
clerics whose kirks, chapels, tabernacles and what
not need the financial support of the Vickekys
and Robsons of the community. They depend
largely on prosperous importers with businesses
and unconquerable prejudices regarding the
“ curse of Protection ” and “Labor domination,”
and with perfect trust in the reality of G. Reid’s
Menace. Hitherto (owing to the “pull” of the
well-to-do Church members) the Orange folk have
been tireless barrackers for the Romish workers of
Europe. The Lodges have toiled at election after
election for Freetrade candidates, and at
election after election they have, in conse-
quence, assisted to hand Australian Protestants’
jobs to Roman Catholics in Italy and in France.
They have railed at Arbitration Acts, Factory
Acts and Early Closing Acts as “ un-English ”
interferences with “liberty”—though the only
liberty these measures have interfered with has
been that of the wealthy church members to sweat
their indigent co-religionists. Also they have
opposed the compulsory training of Australians —
the measure which Lord Robebts so strenuously
advocates for the Cold Land —as being “against
the spirit of the British race ” and a plot of the
Cardinal’s. Are Orangemen going to change their
political spots thus suddenly on tbe mere say-so of
Dill and his friends? And, anyhow, are the
Anti-Sosh DiLL-ites, who hold the money-bags,
going to let the heads of the Yellow churches
project themselves into the Labor camp without a
stiff struggle? And if the struggle proceeds to
such a point of acerbity as to wring hints from
the Anti-Sosh quarters of sturdy Protestantism
in re diminished collection plate returns, are
the leaders of the new movement in a position
to resist ? The Bulletin opines not. The crowd
is, in a political sense, incorrigible. Tradition,
habit, bigotry, and a dozen other factors chain it
to the Tory side of the fence. When this paper
learns on reliable authority that Dill has gone on
the election committee of a Parliamentary candi-
date named Hourigan solely for the reason that
Hourigan advocates the principles that were
voiced recently at Clifton, it will believe that the
Yellow Pup, as a body, has found reasonableness.

The letter of “ 0.K.” :
I would like one fine morning to go out with my gun
and kill a “representative Australian” who has just
returned from his first trip to Europe—the sort of repre-
sentative Australian who is usually a mayor. He goes
forth swollen with a personal sense of importance, which
is, after all, quite natural and pardonable and which has
a patriotic spirit in it. He returns with a diminished
sense of personal importance, and no national spirit
whatever, as it seems to me. He has learned in his
travels that all countries are too busy with their own
affairs to be deeply concerned about the mayors and other
matters of a nation that is forming itself 10,000 miles
away. But he has not discovered the simple fact that
those far-off peoples pay absolutely no heed to the pro-
vincial divisions of the country he comes from. He is
still incapable of understanding that the average British,
German, or French conception of Australia—the more
especially since it became a Commonwealth—is as
vaguely general as the average Australian’s idea of
Germany or France. I except Britain, because the pro-
vincialism of the Scottish and Welsh sections of the in-
habitants clings to its national traditions, and has been
imported to this country. The average Australian con-
tinues to think of Britain as three little countries joined to-
gether, whereas itsinhabitants—English, Scotch, or Welsh
—think of Australia as one large continent, without regard
to its State classifications. The mind of a mayor cannot
grasp the Commonwealth situation from a foreign point
of view. Yet he is interviewed by newspaper reporters
when he comes back to his own particular suburb, and
“the impressions he has gained on his travels” are
solemnly recorded for the instruction of his untravelled
fellow citizens.
The Mayor of Camberwell (Melbourne) is one of the
latest of these misleading authorities, and his “ interest-
ing statements ” were given half a column in the Age.
For the most part, he repeated a number of old truths in
a less aptly informative manner than that employed by
the ordinary commercial traveller. When he talked of
emigration he lapsed into the untruthful foolishness of
bigoted provincialism. “ The tide of emigration,” said
worshipful Mr. Mayor, “ has now set in towards
Australia. It is to be regretted, therefore, that
Victoria by her miserable show at the late Exhibition, had
placed herself in a most disadvantageous position as com-
pared with N.S.W., Queensland and Westralia, whose
vigorous policy pursued at the Exhibition had made these
States well known throughout the Kingdom.”
Here is the same old lamentable instance of the Aus-
tralian in England looking at things Australian through
the colored spectacles he brought with him, and persuad-
ing himself that he regards them “ with the eyes of the
world.” As far as the tide of emigration to Australia was
influenced by the allied displays of Australian pro-
duce at the Exhibition in question, the labelling of
the various courts was valueless for advertisement
purposes. The Commonwealth got the collective ad.
If any emigrants were thereby attracted they shaped
their ends in accordance with other considerations
than the difference between the gold “ trophies ” of Vic.
and W.A. and the wool “trophy” of N.S.W., or what
not. Had the Victorian section of the show been magni-
ficent instead of “ miserable ” (which, in the eyes of the
foreigner, it wasn’t), intending emigrants to Australia
wouldn’t have gone to Victoria to take up unavailable
land on the strength of a gilded wooden pyramid in Lon-
don. The Mayor of Camberwell doesn’t even suggest that
the Commonwealth at large suffered any loss by the
alleged ineffectiveness of the Vic. Court at the Exhibi-
tion, but in the supposition that other States gained
emigrants who might have been induced to go to Vic-
toria, the worshipful person betrays his provincialism
whilst showing his ignorance of the country where he
formed his precious impressions.

transpired. The fleet then made a call on Japan,

[FOR THE BULLETINS.]
The sweated woman of Great Britain is probably the most
hopeless creature in the world. The Eskimos of the
frozen North, living on oil and dried fish, in the snowed-
up fashion of their winter prisons, cut off from almost
every phase of moral and spiritual culture, know not the
extent of the hopeless dreariness of life of these sweated
mothers of opulent England.-— Daily paper.
While news of the scarcity of employment comes from
all parts of the country, we find that £11,000,000 worth of
foreign manufactured goods were imported in the
short space of 30 days. Not less than half of
that total represents wages paid to foreign workmen.
The depressed state of British trade, the unemployment
problem, and the prospective heavy deficiency in the
national finances are obviously alarming the Government
and its supporters in the House of Commons and the
press. They see that it will be more than ever difficult in
the near future to avoid some change in the fiscal policy
of the country . — Daily paper.
The people starve behind the guns.
(Protect them , O England!)
The millions wail behind the guns,
“ Protect us or we die ! ”
Oh ! the peril that is toward
Is no jealous foeman’s sword ;
Hunger is our cruel lord—

can do when abetted by the most favorable con-
ditions. And the best conditions for achievement

something particular did happen. The visible
result of the visit was an agreement between the
itagle and the Chrysanthemum. The principal
provisions of said agreement are that the two
countries are pledged to:—
(1) Respect each other’s trade and territory.

(4) Encourage the development of Jap and Yankee

Even the question of colored immigration into the
Western States was dropped in the Eagle’s haste
to make friends with the Chrysanthemum. This
arrangement leaves Australia even more isolated
than before. Having fixed things up with the
Ape-Chrysanthemum person, Uncle Sam’s fleet
will probably go quite out of the Pacific now, in
order to guard larger interests in the Atlantic.
And as Britain is already out of the Pacific, save
for a few inferior vessels of the venerable parent
description, retained chiefly for the purpose of
bombarding the little nigger’s cocoa-nut tree in
times of stress, the Ape-Chrysanthemum flag and
the Chilian banner are left to decorate the greatest
of the oceans between them; and though the
Chilian is a reckless and magnificent fighter, he
knows his own size too well to give needless sass
to Japan on the high seas. For all practical pur-
poses there is only one flag in these waters.
A False Prophet and a Fool’s Paradise.
Spkaking on the subject of Defence at the
A.N.A. luncheon in Melbourne, Prime Minis-
ter Fisher made some remarks that were wise,
and some that were not nearly so wise. When
Fisher was wise he said that Australia ought to
bog in and do things in the way of training its
people and getting together the materials of war.
When he was not so wise he hinted that, after all,
we might pull through in the matter of Defence
even with our present scanty population, and that
there was no urgency in the matter of immigra-
tion from the Defence point of view. Said Fisher :
Nobody doubts the ability of the people of Australia to
defend themselves. Scotland, with only a tenth of the
population of Australia, defended itself against Europe.
(Cheers.) ... lam anxious for population ; but I won’t
mix up population with defence.
It was a most unfortunate statement for quite a
lot of reasons. Here are some of the reasons :
(1) Scotland didn’t defend itself against Europe, for
Europe never attacked it. The troubles with the Danes
ended about 1040. Then, barring one small scrimmage
with the inefficient Haco of Norway, it had no enemy save
England. And France provided it with a most sturdy and
persistent ally. We have no such ally.
(2) Even when Scotland had only 500,000 people it had
17 to the square mile. We have less than two.
(3) The Scot was as well armed as his enemy in those
days of swords and spears and axes and coats of mail. To
all intents and purposes, we aren’t armed at all.
(4) The Scot certainly maintained a sort of independence
up to 1651. That is to say, his capital was occupied at
times by the enemy. The whole low-lying part of the
country was made a wilderness over and over again, and
foreign armies camped for years in the country. Aus-
tralia wants a much better kind of defence than that.
(5) Finally Scotland was conquered by Oliver Crom-
well, and it never definitely recovered its freedom.
Charles 11. and James 11. kept it in a state of subjection,
and harried it with religious persecutions. Then a revo-
lution in England supplied it with a Dutch king, and it
lost its national Legislature, and became more and more
an appanage of England. Even its national costume was
prohibited by a Parliament sitting in London. That also
is not the kind of defence that Australia desires.
Taking one thing with another, there is nothing
in Scottish history which is calculated to make one
take a cheerful view of Prime Minister Fisher’s
idea that immigration and defence are two dis-
tinct subjects, and that Australia’s present hand-
ful of population is sufficient to save it should
trouble come this way. It is a very small popu-
lation, and the trouble is liable to be very big.
