Some Unreliable Figures.
The Melbourne Argus, in its strenuous fight on
behalf of the large Victorian landholder, isn’t
scrupulous as to its methods. In a recent issue,
by way of proving chat there isn’t any big land-
holder at all, it printed a table of large estates in
which it was shown that there were only four
properties in Victoria over 50,000 acres in area ;
only 48 over 20,000 acres ; and only another 49 of
between 15,000 and 20,000 acres. Now the Warr-
nambool Standard, a Tory paper that circulates
in the Western district, and that cannot be sus-
pected of playing tricks on its squatter friends,
prints in its almanac a list of the large landowners
in its own district only. This list includes no
fewer than ten squatters who have over 50,000
acres. They are:—
Robert Chirnsidr, Streatham 51,721
Walter Laidlaw, Coleraine 56,865
Executors of John Moffat, Mortlake .. .. 90,104
Executors of Macpherson, Streatham .. 62,164
Robertson Bros., Balmoral 87,325
W. Phillips, Cavendish 57,553
John Ware, Ararat 50,457
Executors of Sam Wilson, Ballarat .. .. 73,844
Wm. Weatherley, Mortlake .. .-. .. 52,725
Edward Manifold, Camperdown .. .. 56,190
The same publication gives, in its list, no fewer
than 52 stations in its own district with an area
of over 20,000 acres each. And this is one dis-
trict only! It seems, therefore, a fair thing to
ask the Argus where it got its figures from. The
Standard list throws a vivid light on the scan-
dalous manner in which the land is locked up in
the Western district. Some of the “ big families”
own enough land to make a principality. Amongst
them, the Manifolds are the owners of 115,891
acres, divided into six stations. The Robertsons,
of Hamilton, have 151,481 acres, in eight stations.
It takes nearly a page of the almanac to hold the
Robertsons and Manifolds. The Moffats
have 120,000 acres, and the Laidlaws something
like 100,000. And practically the whole of this land
is suitable for small farmers. Still, according to
the Argus, nothing drastic is required.
Plain, Whitewashed Gaol.
The Melbourne Board of Health begins to express
its weariness of the J.P. who crowds on the
Bench whenever any tradesman’s case is on, and
who, while, there, imposes a fine of £5 at most on
the swindling adulterator of milk. The Board’s
inspectors go to a whole lot of trouble to catch the
rascally offenders, and then the sympathetic J.P.
nullifies their efforts. They know milk-dealers
who have reduced the business of adulteration to
a fine art, and who are making a dishonest profit
of from £6OO to £lOOO a year by selling Yan Yean
as milk, and they realise that an occasional £5 or
£lO fine is a mere detail of the man’s balance-
sheet, just like damages to harness or breakages
of cans. Also, they have convincing proof that
this adulteration is wilful; and they say that the
wilful adulterator deserves no mercy. Yet they
know he will get mercy, and more than mercy,
so long as the punishment is left to the J.P.’s indis-
cretion. What is wanted is an amendment of the
Pure Food Act fixing the penalties just as they
are fixed in the Licensing Act—£2 for a first
offence, £lO for a second, and loss of license for a
third ; only as the offence of selling beer after
11.30 p.m. is trivial compared with the crime of
adulterating the sole sustenance of helpless babes,
the penalties should be made vastly more severe.
Even if a fine should be prescribed for a first
offence of wilful adulteration, nothing but gaol
should await a second offence—plain, cold, white-
washed gaol. Then if a man were guilty of a
third offence, it might very well be taken as proof
of just such a kink as the Indeterminate Sentence
Act has been designed to straighten out. These
penalties would not drive any decent citizen out of
the business any more than the heavy penalties of
the Licensing Act deter any honest publican from
continuing to sell beer. Further, whereas the
publican continues to break the law because he is
perpetually urged thereto by his customers, the
milkman’s customers hold out no such induce-
ment ; in his case it is only his greed and
inborn rascality that make him persist in his
adulteration, and the Indeterminate Sentence is
supposed to be the latest and most improved cor-
rective to a persistent and wilful habit of villainy.

John Quick, M.H.R.,'made some scatter-brained
remarks, the other day, at Castlemaine (Vic.),
about what he was pleased to call “ the evils of
centralisation.” John Quick is a fine old Tory
these days, and the remarks were worthy of him.
Here is a specimen :
An agitation had been started by the Labor party to
alter and enlarge the scope of the Federal authority by
adding to it powers of a purely local and domestic
character, which at present belonged to the States.
Going even beyond this, an association had been started
in Sydney, having for one of its objects the total abolition
of State Parliaments and State Constitutions in one clean
sweep. Such scatter-brained proposals should be resisted
by every patriotic Australian. Centralisation would
result in such evils as were the causes of the separation
of Tasmania, Maoriland, South Australia, Victoria, and
Queensland from New South Wales in the old days. Such
centralisation would lead to strife, resistance, and rebel-
lion, and in the end the disruption of the Commonwealth.
The Bulletin takes the liberty, just here, to men-
tion to Mr. John Quick that his statements aren’t
true. There is no such association in Sydney as
he describes. The league, of which Mr. John B.
Steel, of 192 Castlereagh-street, Sydney, is the
president, is a Unification and Reconstruction
league. It only asks for temporary unification so
that the Australian Parliament may have power
to subdivide Australia into States of manageable
size, and endow them with really democratic Con-
stitutions —in short, as a means whereby there
may be done in a year reforms which might other-
wise require two centuries. The league recognises
the foolishness of having all the six State capitals
so huddled together in the extreme south that if
one drew a line from Brisbane to Perth, and
thereby cut all the capitals off the map, the Com-
monwealth would look almost as big as before.
It considers that a State containing 976, ooo square
miles—a State so huge that one part of it is a good
1400 miles away from the State capital—is a hor-
rible example of centralisation, and is “ calculated
to lead to such evils as were the causes of
the separation of Tasmania, Maoriland, S.A.,
Victoria and Queensland from N.S.W. in the old
days.” In fact, the so-called centralisation party
is really a subdivision party. It is Mr. Quick,
with his yearning to have nearly 1,000,000 square
miles of territory administered from the one city
of Perth as at present, who is the centralisation
party.
Dr. Cockburn, once Premier of S. Aus., and a
member of the Federal Convention, was chairman
at the annual meeting of the Australasian Cham-
ber of Commerce in London, held just before the
last mail left with the invoice for the calico ; and,
though he was amongst a crowd which mostly
lives by exporting things to Australia, there
wasn’t any Stinking Fish in his basket. Cock-
burn, in fact, took up the new Australian tariff
and said that he was surprised at its moderation.
There never would have been any howl against it,
he remarked in effeot, if Australia hadn’t pre-
viously been foolish enough to have an “abnor-
mally low tariff.” Also, he observed that
two very special shrieks had been raised
over carpets (20 per cent.) and printed
matter catalogues, etc. (3d. per lb.); and
then, hauling out the Canadian tariff, he
showed that the Dominion’s Protection on the
one was 35 per cent, and on the other
“ Australia’s great offence is that of distance ; if
she were only a little nearer, a great many of
these misunderstandings would be avoided,” he
added. Apparently he was too polite to say that
the “misunderstandings” wouldn’t exist at all if
Australia wasn’t cursed with a set of either
horribly ignorant or horribly unscrupulous
Foreign-trade newspapers, and if it didn’t export
considerable quantities of unpatriotic globe-
trotters who, once on board a P. and O. boat,
reckon that it is “good form” to traduce and
sneer at the country which fills their pockets.

The cheap and nasty P. and O. Indian arrives
yet once more in the limelight through the pub-
licity whioh attaches to the usual knifing incident.
This time it is a rather minor affair—a girl
wounded slightly in the leg whilst walking down
Circular Quay, Sydney, in a crowd. All the
same, it serves to emphasise the fact that the
shambling person who helps to ferry the local
nobility backwards and forwards upon the ocean is
an unreliable savage with all the savage’s evil
proclivities plus most of the vices of a newer
and more ingenious civilisation. The P. and O.
has long enjoyed the aid of the Lascar;
he has helped it to earn its dividends tor
many years past until he must be reckoned as
one of the P. and O. family. Unfortunately for
the P. and 0., that highly-respectable co. has to
shoulder the responsibility of the coffee-colored
person it clutches so violently to its shirt-front;
and of late the responsibility has been particularly
heavy. In swift succession we have had (a) a
murder on the high seas, ( b) a sort of mutiny, and
(o) a stabbing incident. All these have come or
been dragged to light within a very short period.
How many small but dangerous scuffles were
smothered up and punched out in the darkness of
the fo’castle when the wind was high and the
ocean was all round can only be surmised.
If the travelling public is a thinking animal at
all, it must obtain a deal of diversion from the oft-
recurring paragraph which tells how Ram Chunder
inflicted grievous bodily harm or attempted
murder with three knives and an iron bar. Re-
garded as a shipmate for a long voyage, Chunder
is about the most unpromising thing alive. A
crew of trained baboons with a python as bos n
would almost be preferable to a gang of Lascars
whose feelings had been badly ruffled.
The scheme of the “All-Ned” mail service is
receiving some sort of serious consideration
through the specious representations of Dr.
Coulter, Canadian Deputy-Postmaster-General,
who has been buzzing about Australia like
a patent medicine agent. He, or rather
his Government, proposes that a service
from Britain to Australia, via the Atlantic,
Canada, the Pacific, and Maoriland, should be
subsidised to the tune or oratorio of £1,000,000 per
annum, the amount to be contributed in this
way: Britain, £500,000; Canada, £325,000;
Maoriland, £100,000; and Australia, £75,000.
Those amounts are to assist in providing for a
service that will land letters from London in
Sydney in 27 days, and in Adelaide in 29
days; though how that is to be attained,
when the service between Britain and Canada
is weekly, and the service between Canada
and Australia and Maoriland is fortnightly (as
suggested), the voluble Dr. Coulter does
not explain. It is obvious that to save delay
a weekly service on one side of the conti-
nent would have to connect with a weekly service
on the other side, otherwise mails would half
the time lie a week in Vancouver, waiting for a
steamer. However, Australia has already con-
tracted with the Orient Co. for ten years, to land
London mails in Adelaide in 29 days. Consider-
ing that fact, the “ All-Ned” service is, beyond
being of some little advantage to Sydney
and Queensland, practically a sentimental
fad; and, thinking of what that fad is to
cost, Australia should stick to its money.
The Commonwealth already pays a very large
sum in order to get the invoice for the cotton and
the receipt for the sardines rushed at great speed
to the other side ; and that it is tuppence better
off for the rush isn’t clear. Why it should pay
another tuppence for much the same thing, and
then not get the thing after all, isn’t any clearer.
The South Australian coast has had much
the same sort of shipping disaster as the Sydney-
side had lately when the Bega refused to stand up
straight. This time the vessel to heel over is the
Clan Ranald, a huge wheat-carrier ; and it hadn’t
got outside St. Vincent’s Gulf when the trouble
happened. What the trouble really was hasn’t
been made clear. When Lloyd’s surveyor looked
in just before the vessel finished loading, it
had a list to port ; shortly after, when
the few final tons of John Darling’s wheat
had been dropped in, the Clan inclined to
starboard. Evidently Lloyd’s surveyor reckoned
that the vessel had been lying on the bot-
tom, which might account for the see-sawing.
But the 3500-ton ship hadn’t been out of port 12
hours when the list got bigger, and the deck be-
came like the side of an iceberg for precipitous-
ness, and the mostly Lascar and Chinese crew, as
usual, wasn’t much help in time of trouble ;
wherefore no boat was safely launched. Of a
crew of about 65, only four white men and a score
of Asiatics got ashore, partly by swimming, partly
by wreckage, and mostly by lucky accident. In
the way of loss of life, it is the most disastrous
thing that has happened on the S. Australian
coast since the Admella was wrecked 50 years
ago.
The case of the Melbourne coal-lumper
who, for six months past, has been helping to load
steamers when, by all ordinary rules, he should
have been dead and buried, or paralysed, is an
instance of the vitality possessed by the excep-
tional man. It is also of value as showing the
advantage of not knowing what is wrong with
you. for it is fairly safe to bet that Thomas Shaw
would not have been able to go on toiling
amongst other people’s coal had he known
that he was a practically killed man with
a broken neck. Even supposing that the
pain occasioned by the second accident hadn’t
driven him into hospital, it is hard to imagine
that a man’s nerves could long have resisted the
awful knowledge that his vertebrae was out of
joint. Unless the pain is very severe, Thomas is
probably lying in bed feeling dashed sorry that he
ever wandered near the hospital, or allowed the
doctors to turn the rays on to his injured neck.
He can’t very well depart from the institution in
his present damaged condition, for, even if his
neck would hang together enough to keep him
going, his nerves are now probably in that frayed
condition that makes for accident; and it’s all
owing to the dismal knowledge that he has been
alive under false pretences for half-a-year.
In the past, Australia has been flooded
with medical literature issued by. quacks ; and
the damage done has been incalculable. Most of
this pernicious literature has been merely adver-
tising matter which, after detailing a list of
horrors eclipsing the Book of Revelation, stated
that £1 was sufficient to send for the medicines
that would be forwarded securely sealed. Good
medical literature would do a lot of good and
command a ready market. People who cannot
walk round the corner to a doctor require to have
some reliable medical authority on the book-shelf.
This is now available, The Bulletin Co.
having issued a set of Dr. P. E. Muskett’s works
complete for £3. Dr. Muskett is a recognised
authority of high standing, and his work on the
management and feeding of infants has probably
saved many valuable little lives.*

Napoleon continues to dream great dreams
and overlook his washing bill at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney); and J.C.W. has now made so much
profit out of the omission that he could very well
afford to square up with the lady and paste the
receipt on the forgetful gentleman’s tombstone.
There is no slackening of interest in “The
Duchess of Dantzic ” ; for the public realises that
it gets two shows for the money—a drama and a
musical comedy performance ; and both of them
are good, even though the musical comedy part is
very sober except in the matter of clothes.

For but eight nights more—calculating
from Thursday, February 4, inclusive—will
Julius Knight appear as a pale, worn outcast at
Sydney Royal. For but eight nights more will
he be arrested, insulted, branded with red-hot
irons, and generally treated as a social neglige-
able. On Saturday week he becomes the anti-
thesis of The Rat —to wit, the florid, bibulous,
gorgeously-frocked schemer who was the Scarlet
Pimpernel. Bulletin readers are urged to hurry
up before it is too late, and observe Julius in the
r6le of oppressed villain with a heart of gold.
*■ * * *
‘‘ A Modern Adventuress ” at Sydney
Palace is too swift a female pirate for this paper.
She rushed on to the Palace stage on Saturday
(January 30), which was too late for last week’s
issue ; and she disappeared into the canvas wings
on Wednesday (February 3), which was some
eight or nine hours before The Bulletin reader
was due to purchase his copy of this issue. Any-
how, she was merely a revived Adventuress; the
peach-bloom complexion that denotes the new
play was absent from her cheek, and her unimport-
ance may be gauged by the fact that the
management divided a week between her and the
anguished howls of “East Lynne.” Appropriately
enough, the weather has collapsed utterly at the
approach of the longest sustained sob in the
British language; beyond this scribe’s grimy
window, the skies are leaden and the streets are
slush, so that when the cab-horse can be induced
to hurry, his feet make a low-spirited, slopping
sound which suggests that he has forgotten to lace
up his hoofs. On the afternoon of Friday (Feb-
ruary 5) a benefit will be tendered to Mr. Harry
Diver—the capable actor who stepped into “The
Broken Home” when that cracked edifice was in
urgent need of assistance, owing to an accident
that happened to Jefferson Taite.
• * • *
“ The Old Folks at Home ” will arise with
grey hairs on their heads, and their mouths
full of sentiment and good advice, on Saturday
(Feb. 13), at the Palace. A company of mimes,
including Beatrice Holloway, J. B. Atholwood,
Conway Wingfield and others, will impersonate
the Old Folks, for a brief season, at the behest of
the four-name firm of Rupert Clarke, John Wren,
Meynell and Gunn. The Aged Parties are at
present abroad at Newcastle, but they can be de-
pended on to arrive at the appointed time in a
good state of preservation, and with all their
faculties intact.

As the Bilanderson advts. modestly admit
regarding “Man to Man,” now occurring nightly
at Sydney Criterion, “the crowded houses, the
continued and overpowering applause, the enthu-
siastic calls and recalls amply testify to the in-
herent vitality of this popular and strikingly
powerful play.” Nevertheless, there was talk of
“Romeo and Juliet ” displacing the more modern
masterpiece. It was understood that Miss
Eugenie Duggan would sustain the r6le of the
maid whose birthday greetings were due annually
on Lammas night, and the hopes of local Sliak-
spearians ran high accordingly. But what
with the tariff, Federal mismanagement, the
domination of Sosh, the outbreak of fruit-fly, and
other injurious influences, the scheme was found
impracticable. And so on Saturday night next
“The Squatter’s Daughter ” will be revived. It is
the chef d’ceuvre of Bert Bailey and Edmund
Duggan. Comment were superfluous.

To Sydney Tivoli this week have come
sketch artists Geoffrey and Benson, but the bill is
otherwise full brother to the one the usher handed
the nobility and gentry when the skirts of January
were disappearing down the stairs. The title of
G. and B.’s item is “Spoofed,” and although it is
moderately hilarious, it is hardly sufficient induce-
ment for the “patron” who has seen the rest of
the show previously to lay his cash on the box
office. The management should enforce the move-
on clause —that is, if such a thing is handy. A
second part with two or three new items looks a
lot fresher than a second part with only one. As
one of Rickards’ own artists was wont to remark
as he carefully revolved in front of the footlights,
‘ ‘ One good turn deserves another ! ” Lawson and
Namon, Amalia and Leonora, Cristofolo, Good-
fellow and Gregson, and Mackinnon’s Scottish
Meisters, as before, comprise the five chapters of
an oft-told notice.

The popularity of the moving pictures
grows and grows ; and the viewing of them has
risen to the dignity of a vice. People who used
to go to a theatre once in a month now spend every
night in the week meandering round a score of
picture shows. Therefore the Glaciarium and the
Lyceum, in Sydney, are both packed to the doors
with the people who have the set face and
wrinkled eyes of the picture maniac. At Spencer’s
Lyceum the chronomegaphone is still whirring,
and Harry Lauder and Albert Whelan, the imita-
tionist, are the star spooks. Writer prefers
Albert Whelan. He is the cleverer and more enter-
taining spook of the two. Both shows are giving
fine representations of the ruinwhelmed districts
of Sicily and the mainland of Italy, as they were

&* # *
This is the seventh week of “ Jack and
Jill” at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), but that
well-matured panto, continues to kick up its heels
with all the vigor of a young beginner. Eight
times a week the estimable spectacle comes to
pass, and 16 times a fortnight the house is either
full or overcrowded, the surplus population being
particularly numerous on Saturday nights. Thus-
far the matter of a change of bill has not been
even faintly suggested by the advt. Probably
“Jack and Jill” will not last for ever. Its
glories will fade and die, or it will have to be
withdrawn from Melbourne ere its body has
ceased to pulsate with profit. Nevertheless the
Williamsonian panto, presents in its seventh week
the appearance of immortality.
* * * *
The foregoing remarks are equally true of
“ Cinderella.” Excepting the interpolation of one
or two new songs, the best of which is supplied by
Miss Ruth Lincoln, the performance at the Royal
is just what it began to be the Saturday before
the Goose died. It is still going strong. The
advt. has a way of saying “Better see ‘Cinder-
ella,’” and The Bulletin rises to support the
remark. There are shows that one would rather
not have witnessed, but “Cinderella” belongs to
the other class. Better see it more than once.
AT MELBOURNE KING'S.

“ Sailor Jack ” Baker is to prove his
in-no-cence for yet another week at Mel-
bourne King’s. Not that the proof would
carry any weight in a criminal court. The
virtuous mariner in question (who never, never
goes to sea) practically accused himself of a
murder by clearing out before the coroner’s jury
could return a verdict against him. The old man
who did the deed nullifies the effect of his con-
fession by dying before it can be taken down in
writing, and of the two men who saw it perpe-
trated the respectable one (who is a wealthy vil-
lain in an undeniable tall hat) professes to think
that Baker was the murderer, whilst the counter-
swearer is a boozer with a shocking character. In
fact, there is nothing in Baker’s innocence
that looks likely to save him from convic-
tion, though he might possibly get his sen-
tence commuted to a “ lifer.” Also, it is obvious
that Baker can’t attempt to explain his flight from
the inquest without acknowledging himself an
accessory to the murder after the fact. His tardy
champion, the boozer, charges himself with the
same criminal offence, whereas the influential
villain is the only person concerned in the affair
who could get off scot-free by denying everything.
Regarded from a legal point of view, the villain
in Baker’s drama is the least guilty person that
ever brought trouble into a farmer’s family, and
the most sought after for matrimonial purposes.
Mabel wants to marry him on account of
his wickedness to her, and sister Mary is
prepared to become the mother of his
family when Baker proposes to hand her
over to the gentleman in order to make things
pleasant all round. Another notable peculiarity
of Baker’s drama is its glorification of Jabez
Slouch, the thirsty miscreant. The amphitheatre
positively adores Jabez at the finish, owing to his
snakelike methods. When he promises to bite
the hand that had bribed him he is hailed as the
good angel of the piece. Up to then Bert Bailey
gets all the love and esteem of the Anderson
public, but Bailey proves valueless as a prover of
innocence, therefore he is relegated to a back seat,
whilst the shabbiest evil-doer in the crowd is
placed on a pinnacle of approbation, with the lime-
light streaming on his insobriety.
» * ■* *
Following up the success of this his first
effort in drama-malting, Baker will bring his
personal supervision to bear upon a more rational
undertaking. Bartley Campbell’s old play, “My
Partner,” is in rehearsal at the King’s, and the
tradition of George Rignold’s mighty fine per-
formance in the character of Joe Saunders should
recommend the revival to serious notice. Mean-
while “ Sailor Jack ” is getting serious notice from
all who can find humorous diversion in its comic
relief. To be tickled by the “funny” scenes is to
be thrilled by the unintentionally droll ones.
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House a comely white
squaw known as Yeotha is performing feats of
William Tell archery with the connivance of Cap-
tain Grade, who is a specialist in a different manner
of impaling apples and things. The five St.
Leons are a new “turn” of considerable merit
and no novelty. As their old family name indi-
cates, the St. Leons are circus acrobats addicted to
turningsomersaults;butinthecleverestthingsthey
do they are only moderately proficient, and their
brilliant successes last week only served to break
the monotony of their unsplendid failures.
Winton and McGinty have returned after long
absence with a few new departures in polite con-
versation. Also Rickabv, the Latonas, and the
company are received with thanks or resignation.
In the two first-named instances, the thanks are
hearty and general.
** * m
Melbourne is invited to renew acquaint-
ance with its old friend Philip Newbury at the
Town Hall next Saturday (6th). The tenor voice
of Newbury and the cultured art of the vocalist
are treasured recollections in Melbourne. His
was the standard voice by which subsequent
warblers have been judged (and condemned in
most cases), and as there will be only two oppor-
tunities of hearing it before it voyages to Maori-
land, the Town Hall should not be too large for
the audiences. The second concert will happen
on Tuesday.
** * *
The A.N.A. Anniversary Day procession
through Melbourne on Monday was almost dis-
appointing enough to be treated as a bad joke.
Firstly, the officials made the mistake of including
in the march a number of grimy cyclists who
seemed not over-clean and distinguished. Then
they broke the procession—or the tram co., clutch-
ing ever for dividends, broke it for them—for
there was a tiring interval between the advance
guard of unsoaped bikists and the main body of
highly-decorated humans and devices. This main
body might be described in the language of a
primer, thoughitispropertoemploy the most exotic
English in praise of the sentiment animating it.
Here and there it had placarded itself, putting a
label on its patriotism, as it were ; and, as
if that were not enough, it studded its meagre
length with whites masquerading as thickly-
whislcered King Billies, sometimes near to nature
in a complete suit of burnt cork, and sometimes
near to comfort in not too sumptuously filled
black tights. Then there were the police troopers
of antiquity forming a supposed gold escort,
and quite a number of those alleged bushmen
who affect pale drab riding breeches, and those
warm red shirts that ought to be put aside to
cool. The pastoral, agricultural, and mining
industries were distinctly hinted at on lorries
festooned with greenery, and a butcher, for no
apparent reason, had trotted out five or six of
his carts decorated with that dreamy elegance of
which only a butcher would be capable. Of
course, the allegorical tableaux, in which pale
maidens pose in severe classical costume and
a little paint, were not altogether absent.
The Spirit of Australia pushing into the future
had to be symbolised by a half-dozen damsels in
a boat, with another standing magnificently in the
stern, her countenance buried in reverie. Another
lady, who tried to appear Grecian in a bath robe
of pale canary, stood on a pedestal, bleak and
grim as any Venus with a pain. The poor lady
was representing some great spirit or idea, but
didn’t seem to like it. There was little else in the
procession except trams, and they pushed in and
out with the usual indifference of Private Enter-
prise to everything but filthy lucre.
*# * *
T. Burns is putting in a strenuous week
at Adelaide Tivoli, giving exhibition spars with
Baker and others. There is also a shadowy Burns,
who repeats on a screen his fine spectacular
knoeking-out of Gunner Moir in London.
It is impossible to let bygones be by-
gones now that the biograph has come to
stay, and a little incident that one might
wish to forget becomes (1) World-wide and (2)
Eternal. Down at Glenelg, the Pierrots are
putting up a clean and clever (and highly profit-
able) show to cheap open-air houses on the beach ;
also, in the same place, two competent humorists
have given a realistic burlesque of the Burns-
Johnson disturbance. When the “police”
interfered, they were immediately thrown into
deep water, the referee was hurled after them,
then the fighters ; in fact, the only thing not
thrown in was the towel.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Tommy Burns, who was always a showman, is about to
break out in a tresh place. The Bijou Theatre, Mel-
bourne, will be known as the Burns Theatre from March 1,
and Thomas will figure as lessee and manager. Also
Thomas is going to act, he says. He intends to tour
Australia in a drama, by George Darrell, entitled “Fame
and Fortune,” unless Johnson is prepared to “give him
another chance ” —in London. One may safely, predict
that Johnson will offer him another chance, and if Burns
accepts it the tour of “ Fame and Fortune” will, presum-
ably, be interrupted. But these are questions quite
apart from the interesting announcement of what the new
theatrical entrepreneur intends to do at his theatres —for
he will want more than one of them, of course. He in-
tends “ going in for a high-class kind of drama, and with
that object in view will bring out from America some of
the leading companies.” When he isn’t importing fust-
rate talent from Amurka he will make a point of en-
couraging Australian actors, of whom he has a good
opinion, it seems. Therefore this country may look for-
ward to the day when Mr. Burns’s specially selected
Shakspearian co. will appear in “ Hamlet,” with himself
as the melancholy Dane.
“0.K.”: The talk of Tommy Burns appearing: on the
stage sets one thinking of a mightier pug than
Tommy who came from America to Australia for
histrionic purposes. John L. Sullivan was that re-
markable “ star ” actor. He played the blacksmith hero
in a play entitled “ Honest Hearts and Willing Hands,”
or something, and on the first night, if I remember
rightly, the brawny blacksmith presented himself in a
dress suit and patent-leather shoes. He was very indig-
nant when the audience at Melbourne Opera House
treated him as a joke, but his own exalted opinion of
Jonel Sullivan didn’t save the season from disastrous
failure. Apparently Jonel didn’t think himself called
upon to try to act, but, anyhow, the man was destitute of
the dramatic instinct. A professional champion boxer and ■
wrestler who might have earned a crust on the theatrical
stage was Professor Miller. Now and again he took the
part of Charles the Wrestler in “As You Like It,” and his
method of succumbing to a strangle hold by Orlando
(whom he could have easily picked up and killed a fiddler
with) was impressively artistic. The last time I saw Miller
as a mummer he played a dumb-show part in Geo. Miln’s
production of “ Antony and Cleopatra,” and did it very
well.
“10-2-4”: I note that Tommy Burns intends touring
Australia with a Darrell play entitled, “Fame and For-
tune.” I can’t quite visualise Thomas in bellowdrama.
It is not his line. He is too stolid, too wholly and en-
tirely Teutonic to play the part of one of those verbose,
emotional, insanely-chivalrous young fellows whom
Darrell loves to create. Noah Brusso has a personality,
without doubt, and a strong one. But it is not the sort
that will carry him with credit through a heated scene
with a villain who has behaved shamefully, and caused
him (Brusso) to be gaoled for crimes of which he
was as innocent as a bloque unborn. If Mr. Johnson’s
late adversary is not to be made to appear utterly fatuous
when he first faces the footlights in a grease-painted state
he must have his roles specially manufactured for
him. Something in this line should suit Thomas and the
theatre-going public. The villain (a tall black scoundrel)
tries to best Noah Brusso (hero) in a business deal. An
argument follows. Ethiopia is routed. The two
men are shown in their respective offices hard at
work. The untutored savage would fain defeat
the cultured and commerce-wise white in a
Stock Exchange deal. The white ■is quiet, cold,
confident. He tells his sweetheart, his faithful retainers,
the audience, everybody, that the inky hound in the
next room is in for a severe financial discrediting.
Act 111. would disclose the Stock Exchange, with Noah
and his colored foe manoeuvring. The doomed black
might here be allowed something in the nature of a mo-
mentary triumph. But in Act IV. Africa’s stock must
slump heavily. An effective finale to this act would be
the “ hammering” of the Dark Cloud before a stupefied
band of city men, the while two comic pugilists and their
brides jeered at the fallen one. During this process Noah
would stand, with Napoleonic impassivity, in the centre
of the stage. It would be a graceful and telling move on
his part if he gently bade the comedians to desist from
merriment after a while, and urged them to recall that,
“though black, the defeated one was yet a man and a
brother.” In conclusion, the pulverised nig might be
shown filing his schedule, or enduring a stinging tongue-
lashing from the Master in Bankruptcy. Noah, contrari-
wise—still suave, quiet and cool—would appear in a scene
called “ Interior of the Bank of All Australia.” Adoring
cashiers would flock round him, and clerks would protrude
their heads over their partitions and cheer. Amid
frenzied enthusiasm he would proceed towards the “ pay-
ing-in ” counter and put £2,000,000 to his current account
and £6,000,000 on fixed deposit. Then the curtain would
fall.
While in Adelaide the Julius Knight mummers enlisted
the services of a local horse to add realism to the opening
scene of “The Scarlet Pimpernel.” It was hired for
three nights from the owner, one of the bottle-oh
brigade, and the rent of the beast was 30s. It was a
sorry-looking steed, all bones and a handful of skin, but
the proprietor said it would just do for the “ Pimplenut,”
as he would insist in calling the drama. The horse dis-
puted honors with Julius for two nights, but on
the third evening it did not appear, nor did
it send a letter explaining its absence. The owner
took it to the theatre, as usual, tied it up outside the
stage door, and went into an adjacent pub for a re-
fresher. When he returned, the horse was gone. The
co. was in despair, and played the “ Pimpernel ” as best
it could, minus the horse. Next day was a holiday, and
the local students’ saturnalia. Most of the Knight
mummers were watching the foolishness from the win-
dows of a leading hotel. Suddenly arose from them all
a great shout of “ The Pimplenut.” There in the proces-
sion, garlanded with flowers, was the old gee-gee that ought
to have drawn Julius safely through the painted streets of
Paris the night before. Inquiries were subsequently
made as to how and why the animal had accepted a new
engagement, and it transpired that some youngsters had
“ pinched ” it from the theatre door, and hired it out for
a bob to the mortar-boards. The horse was never
destined for histrionic honors again. When it was re-
turned to the bottle-oh man it died.
“ Stargazer ” : When Manager Ed. Geach’s leading man
was outed by a cab, or a furniture van, and a pro. had to
be snared off the street to play the hero of “ The Broken
Home,” I was reminded of the experience of “ The Last
Crime” Continental and American Dramatic co., at
Lefroy (Tas.). When this gifted co. was ready to give its
initial performance, the male star was out on the adjacent
hills, chasing new and original fantods to glue in his
specimen book. The idea of closing the house and
returning £25 15s. was too dreadful to be entertained,
and Colvin, the ponderous “ heavy,” decided to double
villain and hero. It was a herculean task, but Colvin
was a dramatic Hercules, and went at it with confidence
and zeal. The fact that hero and villain had many argu-
ments and a few fights did not disconcert Colvin. He
had a black wig in his left hand and a fair wig in
his right. The black wig stood for vice, the fair one
for virtue. When the sagacious actor ceased ranting
as the rascal, he tore off the black head of hair, and, sub-
stituting the golden coiffure, cooed as the virtuous young
person. In moments of fierce debate Colvin had only
time to make alternate dabs at his naturally bald head
with the distinctive wigs, and the Lefroy miners, regard-
ing the effort as a feat of juggling, applauded uproari-
ously. Of course there were dubious moments. For
instance, we do not know to this day whether it was the
villain who threw the hero into the raging torrent, or the
hero who threw the villain, or whether each threw the
other. But when, in the last act, Colvin as the hero slew
Colvin as the villain, and then arose, donned the fair wig,
and married the heroine, the house rocked with merri-
ment, and the hurt and indignant actor, who stepped
forward to enter a protest, was received with a shower of
silver. “ The Last Crime ” ran for a week, and Colvin
had to double those parts every night. The audience in-
sisted on it, and on the second night howled the substi-
tuted hero out of the house.
“0.K.”: The comparatively crude semi-nude glidings
and posturings of Maud Allan, accepted by Lon-
don as revelations in art, are not even new in
idea. Similar suggestions of the ancient Greek
dancing were introduced in Melbourne by Florrie
Green before Maud Allan had been heard of. The
latter, a plain-looking girl, barefooted and scantily
draped about the body, performs to the music of Mendels-
sohn’s Spring Song. The Melbourne girl, pretty in face
and figure, and a beautifully skilled dancer, puts all her
art into a poetic interpretation of the same Mendelssohn
music. In a cant way of speaking, Florrie Green has
forgotten more than Maud Allan ever learned about any
sort of dance movement; but she never forgot to dress
herself gracefully. And as for the humbug of barefooted
dancing—if shoes are an impediment to the wearer’s pro-
gress in any performance where “lightness of foot” is
a first essential, why do tennis-players wear ’em ?
“Star” : Eight out of ten actors, and almost as many
actresses, are sore footed. Why is this thus? Is there
something in continual walking of the boards that entails
a sad derangement of the human hoof? Or can it be that
dire pedestrian efforts entailed in walking back after
disastrous provincial tours play up with the extremities ?
The fact that popular and profitable pros are similarly
afflicted is no argument against the latter theory, as even
the most successful of them may have been walking
gentlemen in the earlier stages. Anyhow, actors are a
footsore race, and I can mention a number of well-known
people who, for this reason alone, would greatly prefer to
do it on their heads.
“S.T.S.”: I have had the rapid development of the
British drama impressed on me by seeing; a good-enough
amateur rendering 1 of a comedy once famous, “ The Guv -
nor,” of which one line survives in the public memory :
“ Your ’and, Guv’nor, your ’and!” This play was first
staged in 1880. Thatis, it isatrifle older than “ The Private
Secretary ” and a trifle younper than “ Our Boys, to
which it is a near relative. Yet it seems to come from an
altogether past age. All the elderly people are comic;
all the young people are romantic. The girls have
no interest in life beyond love-making, and
at' 18 are engaged to be married. The author,
a little-known person called Lankester, thought-
fully gives the age of each of his characters. The fathers,
at 48 and 52 respectively, are old men, infirm and rather
a nuisance to their families. One of them has taken a
second wife, aged 25, and must, as a consequence of thv_
wild venture, expect her to desert him for someone less
patriarchal. The dialogue is thickly studded with far-
fetched puns. Coincidence is rampant, everyone being mis-
taken for someone else who was due at the same moment,
and nobody having the sense to suspect a misunderstand-
ing. Last and worst, the play breaks out at the end into
16 lines of rhymed couplets, every character getting a' line
or two. The device of finishing off with a tag of verse is
at least as old as Shakspeare, and I should fancy “ The
Guv’nor ’’ must have been the very last example of it.
Only 28 years back ! Our comedies of to-day may not be
great things, but we have moved on some way since 1880.
“Busk”: The authorised biography of Irving, by
Brereton (2 vols.), is just to hand in Australia. He re-
ceived every help from Irving’s sons, Irving’s widow, and
all Irving’s friends and acquaintances, being also himself
a friend, or rather friendly acquaintance, of the great
actor. All his papers were apparently handed over to
Brereton. Yet he doesn’t get the straw for the bricks.
Irving, the man, remains a mystery. Any quantity of
detail about Irving the actor. Ellen Terry, the nearest
approach to a Boswell, palpably concludes that he was
not a great man —only a great histrionic executant.
You are not to find the Hamlet inside him.
John Henry Brodribb was always called John in his
youth. His mother was much distressed at John s
theatrical aspirations. His father lived to see him the
triumphant Lyceum manager. Hoskins wanted to take
him to Australia in 1856, and offered £5 a week (where
would Billy have got it ?). Irving’s mother sa,ved him from
this. Phelps offered him £2 a week, to begin in about a
year. Irving, however, resolved not to commence
in London. On Hoskins’s written recommenda-
tion he got the Sunderland engagement, where
he started by acting gratis for a month. His
first performance at the Edinburgh theatre was with
Barry Sullivan, who acted Richelieu. In 1859 he first;
appeared in London, with a small part in “ Ivy Hall.
Dissatisfied with this, he obtained the cancellation of his
engagement, and did not perforin in London again till
1866. His striking performance in the provinces of Raw-
don Scudamore, in Boucicault’s “ Hunted Down,” led
Boucy to obtain him an engagement to act the same part
at the London St. James’s. However, he made his debut
in “ The Belle’s Stratagem.” Lewis and George Eliot saw
it. Lewis said Irving would be at the head of the London
stage. George Eliot said, “He is there already.” There is
no evidence that Dickens ever saw him act Sikes, as
commonly stated. Dickens saw him as Redburn, in
“ The Lancashire Lass,” and said he would be a gr at
actor. When he acted first with Ellen Terry she had £l5
a week, and he onlv got £7. He married Florence
O’Callaghan in 1869. She was daughter of the ex-Surgeon-
General of the Indian army. When “ Fanchon ” failed at
the Lyceum (1871), manager Bateman wanted Irving to
go with him to America. Bateman had no faith before-
hand in “The Bells.” About 10 nights before Irving’s
appearance in it a version presented at another London
theatre was condemned. Irving was very much averse
to acting Richelieu, Bateman’s good idea later on. Mrs.
Bateman’s management fell through because Irving ob-
jected to the Misses Bateman as leading actresses.
“ The Profesh ” : There is sweating in other places than
clothing factories. A big Australian city owns a dra-
matic co. addicted to plays of the hair-raising class whose
supers rehearse once and sometimes twice a day, play at
night (in some cases having as many as three changes of
clothes in a show !) and provide their own disguises—all
for 12s. per week. About 48 hours’ toil for a dozen
roberts 1 Thrippence an hour ! If anyone knows of a
more economically managed show than this I should
admire to hear from him.
There is a theatrical person in Australia whom actresses
coo about with double-distilled bitterness all because hf
his distressing mania for climbing about the theatre to
peep through windows and cracks. The habit, it is
thought, must be an awful worry to the poor fellow,
apart altogether from the outside chance he runs of
breaking his neck.
The offensive London habit of smoking in theatres has
received a big impetus. Hitherto it has been the chief
mark of music hall (of however high-class), as opposed to
the dramatic show' (however low). But now the Lord
Chamberlain, who has control of these matters, has let
the managers of the “ regular ” theatres know that they
can do just as their patrons wish ; and apparently those
who wish to smoke are enough to carry the day in many
houses.
“ Vic.” : In a paragraph I wrote here on Vic. goldfields
theatres (21/1/’O9), a compositor, or somebody, altered
Charley Thatcher to Richmond Thatcher. I knew both
these Thatcher brothers. Charley was the star of the
Shamrock Music Hall, Bendigo—Billy Heffernan’s place.
He married Lydia Howarde, who appeared asprimadonna
in Melbourne with Lyster’s opera company. Charley’s
name was O. R. Thatcher. Was his second name Rich-
mond? I don’t know, but he was always called Charley.
He composed lots of songs, and you may yet pick up
Thatcher’s song-book, with his wistful-looking portrait
on the paper cover. One of his songs was a parody on
Bent’s favorite, “Ben Bolt”—not Horace Bent, but
Thomas Adolphus—
“ Oh, don’t you remember Green Gully, Jack Jaggs?
Green Gully of gullies so fair—
Where we buckled in and lined our old swags
With three hundred ounces a share ? ”
Then there was Charley’s younger brother, Richmond
Thatcher, universally known in Sydney as Dick, and
somewhat less familiarly in Melbourne. Didn’t he write
for The Bulletin ? He was a free-and-easy, clever sort of
journalist, and the first to write an account of the railway
trip from Sydney to Melbourne. He became a theatrical
agent, with Mrs'. Scott Siddons, etc.
THE GOMEDY MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No merry clown in cap and bells
Is he,
His merchandise, the jest he sells,
Is forged with pain, nor ever wells
Joyous and free ;
Others may laugh, but still for him it spells
Bread, butter, beef and tea.
Wamba and Touchstone and the rest
Were fools,
In jolthead mirth they found a zest,
Made their dull sallies with the best
By well-formed rules ;
Then motley did not wholly pass unblest
In camps, and courts, and schools.
But the slow centuries have set
A pace,
And the jokemaker’s brow is wet
With something more than Adam’s sweat;
Swift is the race
To overtake some mirth that lingers yet
With young and smiling face.
Weighted with care the statesman’s brow
Is Bent,
Weary the toilsome cult of Cow,
And sad the heart that chases now
Five quid it lent;
But laughter’s weight is heavier I trow
Where we our jests invent.
MAOEILAND.
D.M.W.
THOROUGHLY APPROPRIATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The eruption ot Stromboli will be a strong feature.
Stromboli will commence to throw lava on to the city
below. The tidal wave will be shown sweeping over the
city. The Newtown and Metropolitan Baud will render
aprropriate music. —Sydney Star re “ Destruction of
Messina " show. .
A grisly blackness falls upon the world ;
Across the sea a hot breeze shrills, then dies.
Out in the -waste of dark old Stromboli
Fitfully growls, and flashes spikes of flame.
Shudd’ring, the doomed folks cower their homes within,
Fearing they know not what. And now begins,
Slow yet inevitable that which chills
The stoutest heart to ice. Palaces sink
To their foundations, mighty domes and spires
Keel for a space, then hurtle down. The gale,
Long-threatened, is unleashed ; screaming it tears
Over the hissing waves. The mountain-top
Leaps of a sudden into scarlet flame.
Crash upon crash, as though close under foot
All Hell were warring, horrifies the ear.
And through it all, like to a long sea swell,
The earth rolls restlessly, and over it
The white hot lava wends its dreadful way.
A thousand death-screams rend the burning air ;
A thousand horrors start on ev’ry side ;
Death marches grinning through the anguished land
To rag-time from the Newtown-Metrop. Band.

A. Suit to Measure

Society
HAUNTED.
It is stated that the Government will give & tor every £
collected by councils at all the chief resorts of surf
bathers. Is the Government in earnest, and does it
expect councils to appoint persons to cadge collections
on the beaches ?—Letter in Sydney Paper.
Soon there will he no escape from the collection-
box, either ashore or afloat.
The luck of Ah Sin is out in Sydney.
About a fortnight ago the Customs got hold of a
lot of his opium, and now they seem to have got
hold of Sin himself. When the pilot steamer
Captain Cook found 400 tins of the dream
material floating off North Head attached to a
lifebuoy, it was plain that Sin and his friends
were not going without their smoke. The com-
plete absence of Sin from the seascape, where his
property was floating at large, might have been
due either to plain funk or to carelessness.
How many hysterical, wet Chows had
searched through the blind, black night for
that £2OOO packet until dawn made it un-
safe to be pottering about any longer in
full view of the cliffs, can only be guessed at.
Possibly the confederate on the steamer got
mixed, and heaved the dope over at the wrong
time; in which case the shore Chows were pro-
bably sleeping on the customary heap of bananas
when the untimely deed was done. Their re-
marks, when news of the capture reached them,
must have strained theresources of all known Chow
cursing. After the find, the Customs laid its ears
back; so when the Empire was leaving Sydney
last week an unobtrusive launch butted
along behind it to the Heads, and watched the
big ship clear away for Melbourne. When it was
a mere smudge on the horizon a strange launch
came up from nowhere in particular, and nosed
about as if looking for fish or the lost will men-
tioned in Anderson’s drama. For six weary,
blistering hours the Customs officers waited arid
watched; but at last, when the sun was putting on
his coat after his day’s work, the stranger was seen
to swing round and scoot for the entrance. That
was where the mistake came in; it should have
scooted for Brisbane. Then the Customs people
turned their craft towards home, and fled up the
harbor ahead of the suspected vessel. The final
swoop of the Law was delayed until the smugglers
were about to go ashore at an uptown wharf,
and then the Fowl of Justice made its dive, and
got its talons into six wet sacks containing 867
tins of opium, worth anything up to £3OOO. With
the North Head lot, that makes about £4OOO
worth of smoke arrested within one short fort-
night, and if Sin and Hang Fat and Ah Too Soon
aren’t dancing mad, then the impassivity of Far
Cathay is the real goods.
* * * *
A unique sort of strike has been in pro-
gress at North Prentice, in the Rutherglen (Vic.)
district, where the housefathers and mothers have
declined to send their youngsters to the school
until the Department replaces the dilapidated
educational shanty with something less resembling
a crushed cowshed. The Public Works Depart-
ment, which till recently was bossed by that jest
of an administrator, old Farmer Cameron, pro-
mised about two years ago to get an unused build-
ing humped from another site. Actually it got as
far as calling for tenders; but as it considered
these a trifle too high, it postponed the job, and
has gone on postponing it ever since. Now that
the school has come to a dead stop, the Depart-
ment intends to really do something But it isn’t
rushing matters. The “strike” had been in
progress ten days before Education Minister
Billson, who is a novice and unacquainted
with red tape, became officially aware of
it. Then he threatened to upset the placid,
gradual progress of affairs ; but the Department
looked at Billson as at some new beetle it had
never heard of, and assured him that that wasn’t
the way to do things. He must first of all send up
an officer to make a report on the matter ; then
the report must be compared with previous ones,
and with others that might come to hand from
other interested parties; then it must be properly
docketed, numbered, and initialled ; then it must
be passed on to the Public Works Department,
which would also report, examine, minute and
initial. And by the time Billson is an old man,
and the Prentice children are all either grown up
or dead, the matter might possibly be in train for
tenders to be called anew for shifting the vacant
building the necessary two miles or whatever
it is. Billson is at present in a highly explosive
state, and swears to be the death of red-tape.
And though bigger and stronger men than he
have often tackled that job, this paper wouldn’t
for worlds discourage Billson’s murderous inten-
tions.

Dear Bulletin. —Would it be possible for
the Sydney Cricket Ground people to fly the
Australian flag over their pavilions without hurt-
ing the feelings of any person or annoying the
S. M. Herald? During the last N.S.W.-S.A.
match I noticed about 17 flags
flying—viz., two white ensigns,
eight assorted Jacks (union and
dis-union, the latter being in a
very holey state), a German flag,
and sundry club and association
banners or oriflammes or some-
thing. Also (of course) the old
N.S.W. “colonial” flag. But I
looked in vain for our own
Australian flag. Noticed the
same thing during present
N.S.W.-Victoria match, only
some flags had been repaired.
Still no Australian flag. I sup-
pose, under the Dog Act, or
Wade’s lovely Police Offensive
Act, the Australian flag is interdicted, and the
committee would be fined £2 and costs if it flew
one.— Clarence O’Hagan.

THE HARD ANSWER THAT TURNETH
ON WRATH.
HE: “ I simply tell you, plainly, I won’t stand it!”
SHE: “ Well, I’ve never questioned your sim-

The amazing luck of T. Taifc and of the
people who trust themselves to his trains still
holds good. On January 25 the Melbourne-Sydney
express slithered up to Wangaratta (Vic.) sta-
tion at 50 miles an hour. It was supposed to stop
at Wangaratta; but the brakes failed to act,
so it kept on till it was pulled up by the hand-
brake half a mile further on. As the outfit
swerved from the main line to the station siding
and back again, it tossed the passengers about like
footballers in a ruck, and drove the guard head
first through a window. Fortunately, Sunshine
didn’t repeat itself—this time the line was clear,
and there was no call for the hospital train. But
when T. Tait heard of it, it is alleged that it set
him thinking so hard for three minutes that he
let his cigar go out.
» * * *
The Melbourne Morgue statistics for last
year make gruesome reading. The 958 deaths re-
corded include 11 murders, 47 manslaughters (the
Sunshine victims accounted for 44 of these), 69
suicides and 120 fatal accidents. Another 120
careless corpses were found dead, or found
drowned, or were discovered defunct otherwise ;
and the rest on the list died from natural causes.
Poisoning is still the most popular form of suicide
in Victoria, with 19 adherents; hanging and
shooting dead-heat for second place, with 13 each ;
while nine found Mr. Tait’s railways untidy but
useful as a means of exit. Of the murderers, four
adopted the late Othello’s method; three were
abortionists ; and drowning, shooting and poison-
ing give a little diversity to the record.

“ Star ” : The R. C. Church makes specific
announcement of the fact that for the future
mixed marriage will entail excommunication for
the Catholic bride or bridegroom, and will be re-
garded as wholly invalid. This means that in the
eye of the Church children born of such mar-
riages will be illegitimate so far as the Church is
concerned, and the parties to the marriage will be
“living in sin.” But while the Church may think
what it pleases on such matters, it roust not think
out loud. There are simple secular laws for deal-
ing with the man—layman, priest, or bishop—
who alleges that a decent married woman is inde-
cently single, or that her children are otherwise
than absolutely legal. The Church will have to
step lightly on the subject as if it were leaping on
eggs. It is difficult to see exactly how a man or
woman can be denounced for living in a state of
adultery, withou t uncivil and defamatory remarks
being made. And a denunciation which is so
polite as to run no risk of defamation can hardly
denounce hard enough to blow a fly off the altar.

THE GREAT WE ARE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We do not follow Lord ITortheote in his suggestion that
the Northern Territory maybe used as a starting point
for an army of Chinamen or Japanese intending to march
to the southern States. To do so would be to suppose
them capable of unparalleled powers of marching and of
commissariat organisation.—S. M. Herald, 2311f09.
Pooh !
Bah !
Pish ! also Bosh !
How exquisitely absurd—how entertainingly ridiculous !
Excuse us while we smile—
SMILE—
e large, free, expansive smile of Immeasurable
Superiority !
The calm, unupsettable smile of complete assurance and
all-containing knowledge !
How preposterous are these persons !
Quaint, well-meaning Individuals from whom Fate, or
Providence, or Circumstance has withheld the gigantic
intellect which shines in and from and about us !
For We are It !
Undoubtedly and incontrovertibly It !
The Sydney Scorning Herald.
While we do not deny these and similar Persons the rudi-
mentary intelligence, the glimmering of reason, that
marks the human infant—
While, indeed—with that amplitude and generositv of
thought by which WE are distinguished—WE allow
that they may possess a certain precociousness of
comprehension,
A precociousness which gives promise of eventual Under-
standing—
Still,
W e- Us-Ourselves
The Sydney Scorning Herald—
Must, and hereby do, politely but firmly remind you—
whoever you may be—
That WE are It!
What We think IS—
What We say GOES—
What We do is RIGHT.
Everything else thought, said, or done by anybody else,
which does not square, coincide arid comply with
what WE think say or do, is Wrong !
Utterly, absolutely and intransmuta,bly Wrong—
Ibis also rash, inadvisable and Socialistic ; not to mention
that the time has not arrived for it.
Furthermore, and in addition to all these disqualifications,
it is probably disloyal.
And finally and conclusively, no Right Thinking Person
tvould entertain it for a moment.
The idea 1
WE are astounded—
Vast astonishment mingles with vexed amusement in US.
That any person should presume to propound proposi-
tions with which WE cannot possibly agree !
And which, therefore, are palpably false, sham and bogus,
Unorthodox, undemonstrable, untenable, unparalleled,
unprecedented, unsettling and unrighteous 1
For the future, You Persons, know your place 1
Never again dare to advance your puerile jejunities !
WE will do your thinking for you—
WE- US-OURSELVES—
The Sydney Scorning Herald,
That is Our Job—that is what we are here for.
It was so ordained from the founding of Things—yea,
from the very Beginning was it decreed !
Fancy anyone else putting forward opinions 1
Independent, progressive and highly objectionable views—
Mere dreams—delusions—gossamers— chimeras 1
How ridiculously delightful 1
How absurdly exquisite 1
Bosh ! —also Pish !
Bah !
Pooh !
N.S.W.
VAN TROMP.
THE OLD STORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A returned Australian declares that society in Melbourne
has merged itself into a number of sets, each of which
considers itself the most superior, according to its own
particular idea of superiority ; and these, according to
the lately-arrived critic, turn up their noses one at the
other.— Melbourne Paunch (lh/l/’O9).
The matron who is sternly mild, a pattern of urbanity,
A pearl for never getting wild, and dead against profanity ;
The dame who doesn’t dash or damn, nor walk beyond a
well-bred crawl,
Why, let her murmur, “ Yes, I am by far the naicest of
them all ! ”
There is a limit, she forgets, to blends of “form” and
piety,
We know that ours, of all the “ sets,” is really high
society.
Oh, all our nicest families, we find—our aristocracy,
You know—are prone to use the wheeze of “ Yes—the
poor snobocracy ! ”
What though the butcher-nobles bounce, and shove good
birth against the wall,
Though splendid, soap-made kings denounce the well-
born, haughty crew who call
Kings down ; though proud beer-lords express their bored,
august satiety,
And scoff at better men—still, yes, you know you’re “ in
society.”

Sydney.
The colored brother is on velvet in a whole
heap of quaint ways in , the Gold Land. Says a
J London exchange of last month :
At Wimborne yesterday Prince Jitendra was summoned
for having driven a motor-car to the danger of the public
at Sturminster Marshall. The prosecution is a sequel to
a collision recently described in these columns.
Mr. Staple Firth, for the prince, said that as his High-
ness was a suzerain prince to the King, that Court had no
jurisdiction to try him or carry out any verdict which it
might give for or against him. If they made an order
against him, the order would not be effective. The King
was exempt from any Act, and also any ruling prince
under him.
The Bench desiring sworn information that the de-
fendant is the son of a reigning prince, the defendant
took the oath, and said he was the eldest son of the
Maharajah of Cooch Behar.
The Bench decided that they had no jurisdiction to
decide the case.
The United Kingdom teems with sable Mahara-
jahs, Jams, Princes and the like, especially during
the polo season. A goodly number of them live
most of their time there. It must be a painful
thing for the British hind to know that, if the
person who motor-hogs him happens to be an
Indian potentate or the relative thereof, the honor
of the experience must suffice to cancel any
damage done to his (the hind’s) person or property.
In fact, it is enough to make even an English
yokel start talking Alien Exclusion in a loud
whisper.

The latest murderous outbreak of negro
King, the unholy terror of Pentridge (Melb.) gaol,
seems to have been the indirect outcome of the
Burns-Johnsing business. After the irrepressible
nigger had been overcome by a strong combina-
tion of warders, he-said “ When I fight, I fight to
win.” This was a few days subsequent to the
triumph of his colored brother Johnson. Some-
body must have acquainted Mr. King with the
fact that brother Johnson had
fought to win. Therefore he
burst into song and asserted
his pride of race.

NEAR-SIGGTED
OLD GENT
(irascibly):
“ Damn
these spires !”
Two years ago N.S.W.’s Carruthers Minis-
try conceived a scheme for supplying water to
Blackheath and Medlow, on the Blue Mountains.
A large dam holding 70,000,000 gallons of water
was built. This dam is now slopping over,: and
the water going to waste, for when the authorities
began to lay the water pipes, someone discovered
that you can’t lay water pipes except to a munici-
pality. Blackheath is only part of a shire, and so,
until it is gazetted a municipality, the water will
continue to slop over, and the inhabitants con-
tinue to depend for drinking fluid on the occa-
sional thunderstorm. It seems a pity that the
legal point was discovered before.the pipes were
laid. The Government would then have Bad a
fine chance to furiously proceed against itself in
the courts and recover Gosts.
“ Troides ” : A glimpse of a
great Melbourne detective at work.
The scene was a dingy, uncared-
for-looking boarding-house, in an
upstairs room of which a poor
little faded woman, the wife of
a wharf laborer, had been found
lying doubled up on the floor
in a pool of blood. The
police, several myrmidons
of the “ C. I. Branch,” and
this Grand High Sherlock
himself were investigat-
ing. That is, the others
were standing outside the
room of death, pale and
trembling, and he was cross-
examining the woman’s hus-
band, A reporter entered.
This reporter had, in the course
of his business, interviewed
Judges, actresses, and had even
spoken to volunteer colonels
without fear. Perhaps he
failed to treat the great detec-
tive with sufficient reverence.
Three trembling subordin-
ates crept down. ' “ Hush ! ”
they whispered, “The Mas-
ter is upstairs.” The in-
truder heard the rich, fruity
voice of the sleuth : “Now,
sir; be careful, sir. It’s no
use telling lies to me, sir.”
“ So help me Gott,” replied
the suspect, “I don’t tell
some lies already pretty
kviek.” The Master wrote
this statement down in a
notebook. “ Answer me, sir.
Have you been drinking to-
day, sir?” “ Perhabs I haf
some beers, perhabs I don’t.
Yot it madder?” “That is for
ME to say, sir,” thundered the
prodigy, taking down the answer,
and adding a note, “Prevarication!”
For an hour he asked similar porten-
tous questions. The reporter began to
get tired. One of the minor ferrets went
into the sanctuary, bowed, and said ‘‘Newspaper
feller outside, sir.” “Ha!” The Master, who
hadn’t got anything out of his suspect, saw an
opportunity of showing his greatness. He came
outs upon the landing, and so terrible was his
aspect that the unfortunate reporter fell at his
feet. The policemen-in-waiting also prostrated
themselves. “Now sir,” he said, “I cannot ’ave
you interferink with my work, sir. lam inter-
ror/atink a suspect, sir.” The terrified reporter
fell down stairs in his fright, and the pale sub-
ordinates pretended to look for bloodstained
bootmarks on the wall. At the bottom of the
stairs the reporter was dragged out into the street
by the inmates of the house. When he recovered
he got the : story from a small boy, who seemed to
know a good deal more about it than the Master,
and went away to write it up.
* * * *
The London jury which sat last week on
the bodies of the victims of Hefeld and Jacob,
Russian anarchists, urged the coroner, according
to Reuter—
to draw the attention of the Home Secretary to the
danger to which the country was subjected under
existing conditions governing the admission of aliens.
London and provincial juries have been making
this sort of request to coroners ever since the
writer can remember, and of late a little—but not
enough—has been done in the matter. Whenever
Europe or America gets too hot to hold a chronic
dynamitard, he comes to England, and there he is
usually at peace. Soho is full of revolutionary
aliens—long-haired, insanitary lunatics who
sleep with knives between their teeth
and melinite bombs under their 'pillows,
and in other respects behave like the half-
witted plotters of bellowdrama—and, by latest
advices, they are slopping over into the postal
districts on the “south side of the water.”
Rightly or wrongly, the Continental peoples hol'd
this up against Britain. It is a curious fact that,
for years and years, no attempt has been made on
the life of any member of the British Royal
family in the United Kingdom. Rulers,
objectionable and otherwise, and innocent rela-
tions of rulers, have been mown down in
considerable numbers over the Channel. But in
the United Kingdom the Anarchist has ever held
his hand. The Continental nations say that the
British police deliberately give harborage
to regicides on the understanding that there
shall be no regicide in the land wherein the Bull-
dog breeds. There is no doubt, according to
reputable French and German papers, that the
majority of Anarchist schemes that have come to
fruition in late years were hatched in London.

Thus the Oldest Inhabitant:—
A more dismal function than that of the N.S W Bar
bidding farewell to Chief Justice Darley on Thursday can
scarcely be conceived. A funeral would be a comedy to
it. J went because, in November, 1873, I witnessed the
farewell function at Alfred Stephen’s retirement and was
present when James Martin was sworn in. The Stephen
adieu was given in the old Banco Court, and there was
no restriction upon the public. People were admitted
without comment, and mingled with the wigs and “-owns
On Thursday, the janitor, civil but stern, said that his
instructions were to “ admit only a few barristers ”
Presently the jolly, rubicund, genial Moriarty came along
in wig and gown. He was promptly blocked, much to his
astonishment, and retired apparently very much annoyed
Soon there were several barristers, with the representa'-
tives of the press and a couple of camera fiends all
stopped in the same fashion. Mr. Darley’s extreme
ideas as to the government of “ his Courts ” were being
carried out. After some delay the janitor sent for
instructions, with the result that barristers only were to
be admitted. Then the sub-Sheriff came along and he
graciously ordered the gallery to be opened to the mere
public.
Apropos the new Banco Court, it was built on Mr
Darley’s suggestion, on the lines of the King’s Bench in
the Four Courts at Dublin, where Mr. Darley had fre-
quently carried an empty brief bag. Now, the Dublin
Four courts were erected in a time of trouble. There is
a great rotunda, and the courts have entrances therefrom
Kacb doorway is small, so that two armed men stand-
ing in the stuffy entrance could hold a mob in check
while the Judge Jeffreys of the time escaped by the back
way. Mr. Darley’s court, built on this suffocating Irish
principle, was simply as a Turkish bath on Thursday.
Within the court everything was sombre. Four
Supreme Court Judges attended, the pair of Simpsons
being prominent, not to say glaring, by their absence
Four minor Courts” were visible-Heydon, Gibson and
Backhouse being away. Of 14 King’s Counsel on the roll
only four—Wade, Reid, Ralston and Shand—put in an
appearance. Of the retired Judges not one turned up
•p h f7 c ou>d not forego their hohday even to bid fareweli
to the Chief.
HIS LAST
CARD.
“ Wake up, you’re talking in your sleep!"
“ Well, am I never to speak in the house at all ? ”
Adelaide is in the throes of a burglar
scare. Between January 1 and 23, 14 safe rob-
beries, etc., occurred. There is no clue extant in
the case of any one of them. Despite a £250
reward, the slayers of Constable Hyde are still at
large. In the Flynn inquiry last week McLach-
I vn, counsel for Flynn, assured Inspector Priest
that a “wanted ” might lunch with the policeand
never fear recognition. When Lawson, of Block
14 robbery fame, was questioned by the Commis-
sion regarding the police denial of the presence of
Sparks in S.A. at the time of the robbery, he
asserted that he knew a “wanted” who walked
the Holy City streets for 17 weeks, despite the
fact that the law was pining for him both in Vic.
and S.A. Adelaide possesses 179 cops, but a cop
that doesn’t cop anything seems as useless as a
John Hop who doesn’t hop after the malefactor
or a John Dunn who has never done anything.
They do amazing things at Broken Hill
these days. Lately a man elbowed or shoved a
constable. Later John Flynn was run in, and
one bobby identified him. Flynn’s reply was that
he was on picket duty at the time, and that it
was a case of mistaken identity. An officer of
the union swore that Flynn was on picket duty at
the tiriie. Eight witnesses swore that he was on
picket duty at the time. The town librarian
didn’t know whether Flynn was on picket duty
at the time or not, but he swore that he did
know that the man who shoved the policeman was
not Flynn, because he witnessed the incident.
Then the magistrate said he believed the one
policeman, and alleged that it would be £3 or a
month. Now, if Wade’s Law Department has a
penn’orth of faith in the wisdom of Wade’s P.M.
it should prosecute eleven different persons for
perjury, and then listen to the thud of itself fall-
ing in. Probably it hasn’t enough faith to prose-
cute even one person.

“ Hassan ” : Just now all the small boys
of Melbourne are busy losing kites on the tele-
graph wires. A month ago the little girls were
drawing fearsome propositions on the pavements,
and playing hopscotch therein. Still earlier both
sexes were spinning tops, and, at the end of the
football season, the game of marbles was in full
swing. And these games come in with the utmost
regularity. It is not as though one kite appeared
ifl the suburbs and others followed. One day the
sky is clear, and the next it is, as an Italian
friend in the police puts it, “stiff wid
the dahm t’ings.” Any little boy, however,
who flew a kite in October would be shunned
by his fellows. It would be un-British. Kite-
flying is not a sport which is confined by natural
conditions to any particular month. Marbles are
played during the cold weather, and must chill the
knuckles considerably. But probably because the
kids in the “Mother Country” play marbles in
August and fly kites in January, ours carry on the
habit. What puts the top, or hopscotch, or kite
virus into their blood at any particular season is a
mystery deeper than that of the birth of a hero of
melodrama. But the fact is another evidence of
the cyclic and automatic theory of human life.
ON THE SCARCITY OF SHOPWALKERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are complaints in Melbourne of a great scarcity
of shopwalkers. “We find it extremely difficult to secure
men with the needful good presence and nice manner,”
says the manager of one large concern, with houses in
several suburbs.
What’s coming to our splendid race ?'
What ails the men of British birth ?
This damning fact we have to face,
This ominous and galling case,
That, whereas once the men of worth,
So tall and graceful, wise and kind,
Were common, now we canno.t find
The soul of dignity and grace
To murmur : “ Forward to the lace ! ”
That man who walks the shop must be
Superior to the common herd,
A suave Adonis, not too free,
Bland pattern of gentilifee,
With always just the proper word
To fit the weather, and a smile
That never wearies mile by mile.
No stunted fop, no lumbering ox
Should guide a lady to “ the socks.”
The draper’s heart is filled with dread
The grim alternative he scorns,
A “walker” who is under-bred,
Or bandy, or has hair of red,
Or walks absurdly, racked with corns.
But all in vain the masters mourn.
Shopwalkers to the manner born,
The products of the present age,
Now air their talents on the stage !
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

entered the ports of the United Kingdom was British-
owned ;in 1906 only 63J per cent. In 1870, when the
figures were highest, 88i per cent, of the. steam shipping
that entered and left the ports of the United Kingdom
was British; in 1906 the proportion was down to
65 per cent. In 1870 1,731,273 tons of foreign
steam shipping entered and left British ports ;
in 1906 the figure was 40,374,078 tons Wang:

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
A RARA A VIS.
SWAGGIE (bewildered): "Say, is there anything
funny-looking ’bout me ? Them*fore's ’ve bin
starin' at me ever since I passed by that pub."
LOCAL RESIDENT : “ That's just it, mate. It's
'cos you passed it by."
“ Jeach ” : Yes, I met one of those “ Way-
farers,” though I did not know it till I read your
description of the new order of converted travel-
lers. He was bullock-driving, and after he’d
broken his whip handle, split the welkin, torn his
hair and burst his trouser-seams, he knelt down
and repeated the “Wayfarers’ prayer.” It’s a
powerful prayer, sir, and calculated to remove
sandhills. I camped with him one night, and
after I was rolled in I heard him sing the “ Way
farers’ Hymn.” It’s a real Australian bush hymn,
but I don’t think it was arranged for use in
churches. If The Bulletin is taking names for
our future capital I beg to nominate the follow-
ing : Gorsavia, Billyeanberra and Yassphodel.
“ Itan ” : What a poor sort of struggle
the aborigines are putting up against the un-
e mquerable movement of evolution, which fore-
d loins them to early extinction ! A decade or
tvo ago the moralists used to make reckless
charges against Rum and a couple of other gentle-
men of the same bottled family. But it is
quite clear that grog was, at the worst, only a
hastening influence. Recent experiences prove
this, and also prove that the tide of ex-
tinction cannot be stemmed. The north
coast of N.S.W. was the most thickly abo-
riginaled part of the State. Half-a-century ago
there were hundreds of petty tribes thereabouts.
Now it would be exceedingly difficult to muster
500 of Binghi all told between Port Macquarie
and the Tweed and back to the tableland. Of
late years the police hate been strenuous in their
supervision, so that the decadence cannot be
entirely attributed to drink. The festive shearer
or riotous timber-getter of the old school would
sink more liquor in a week’s spree than half-a-
dozen of the luckiest blacks would get in a year.
The missionaries have had their chance too. The
aboriginal’s bump of religion is an insignificant
mound. He’ll be anything, if there is an extra
feed or a stick of tobacco to be squeezed out of the
mere profession. Not long ago a Roman Catholic
missionary visited a small settlement on the
Clarence River and christened the whole
lot without any ado. Another brand of
religion had been in the habit of con-
ducting services amongst the remnant, and
it fumed with indignation. So next day it
counterblasted the R.C. ceremony by marrying
the couples in orthodox Protestant fashion,
although the niggers had all gone through their
own matrimonial ceremony, which included the
cutting off of the first joint of the gin’s little
finger. The wounded sect felt easier in its mind
after that. Then the niggers, who had got some
cake and turkey twill out of the business, sat and
gazed earnestly down the road in the hope that
some more religion might becoming.

“ G.S.K.” writes
As the writer of the par (B. 15/10/’08), re the use of the
bicycle by shearers as a means of transit, I would like
to reply to “Sr.” (B. 21/l/’O9), who alleges that my yarn
is grievously in error. First, let me draw his attention
to the all-important fact that my statements related to
shearers travelling Out-back, in places where there are
no parcels offices for the lame bike to go to, no welcome
trains for the traveller to fall back on when the hike jibs,
and no swampy roads to cause the bike to shy. The
sulky is little used in those parts mainly owing to the
circumstance that horse feed must be carried in the
sulky, and can only be obtained at places hundreds of
miles apart. Also water is too scarce, unless you can get
a non-drinking horse—the Government tanks are over 40
miles asunder. The exorbitant price of feed beyond the
Darling is a sufficient bar to the use of the horse and
sulky. “Sr.” should see the country referred to, and
then he would be convinced that it’s not a place to expect
water up to a sulky’s axle, except in exceptional seasons
that are about ten years from one another, and even after a
deluge this country dries up in a couple of days owing to
the absorbent propensities of the ground. No, “Sr.,’’the
horse and sulky, from the standpoint of practical utility,
are not in it with the bicycle, which carries all that a
shearer requires in the shape of swag, tools, a day’s
tucker, and a water-bag.
