“ Dandalup ” : lie speed of bush fires.
“Milky White's” whirler (B. 14/l/’O9), which
propelled itself along at the rate of one and a half
miles per hour, must be a very tired member of
the Blazing Snail family. In dry country a bush
fire will travel, almost invariably, at the same
velocity as the wind ; while, in green bush, the
flame will hurry along at from half to three-
quarters the rate of the wind. In support of my
first statement, allow me to produce the
great Gippsland fires in the latter
’nineties. Eye witnesses state
that the flames rushed along
as fast as the wind, licking
at and frizzling up every-
thing they touched. Many
a time, they say, the Fire
Demon flashed through the
bush, green and otherwise, at 30 or
40 miles an hour. Often his flight was
along the tops of the trees, and the under-
growth did not ignite till some time after he had
passed over. Out on the great plains of the West
I have seen the beast swishing along in the wake
of '‘Cock-eye Bob” at a tremendous rate, and
many a vain-glorious, fleet-footed emu has had his
tail-feathers singed by the rapid conflagration.
I’ll back an Australian Never-Never blaze against
the best outsiders the world can produce.
* * * m
The sudden, discordant yelp of “ O.” :
This is about the time of the year that the correspon-
dent with the vivid imagination and the long words and
the brilliant descriptive power writes about the bush fire
on the mountains and the ruin that it 1. aves in its wake.
Phrases like “ the devouring element,” “ scenes of deso’a-
tion,” “ lambent tongues of flame,” and such expressions
are working double shifts, and are nearly sweated to
death.
I’m on the mountains now. I don’t see any desolation.
I see the bush look better than ever it did in my experi-
ence before. The fact is that the trees and shrubs on the
mountains like to be burnt 10 a certain extent. It in-
terests them and gives them new life and courage.
During the last few years there were many fierce fires.
That’s why the mountains look so well now.
There are some beautiful gums in the grounds of a
house about half way between Sydney and Watson’s Bay.
An old gardener was burning off the grass one day a year
or two ago, and one of the trees caught fire as Twas
passing on a tram. I jumped off and told the gardener.
He said it was all right; he knew about it; it was good
for ihe tree. Then he threw some dry sticks and
withered grass on the flame. That tree is growing
splendidly at this moment.
Suppose there were no bush-fires for a few hundred
years, how would the Blue Mountains look? In a dry
season they would be one vast mass of sandstone, without
a vestige of vegetation, for grass and herbage can’t stand
the drought. Any good botanist—Mr. A. Gl. Hamilton,
for example—will tell you that the seeds of many of our
plants will lie dormant for years, and will germinate only
when subjected to fire. Indeed, when nurserymen wish
to grow these plants, they burn the seeds or fry' them.
When, therefore, in the course of time, the mountain
vegetation died of old age, in the absence of fires there
would he nothing to replace it.
Please don’t let me hear any libellous remarks about
the bush fire in future.

“Surcingle”: Tom Willis, in the new
annual volume of the N.S.W. Agricultural So-
ciety, alleges that Australian horses do not dete-
riorate. The advance in price may be somewhat
likened to that of English hunters. The finished
article there used to be worth from £7O to £BO at
the most. Now a first-rate hunter may run up to
£lOOO. He has advanced from a three-quarter
bred to a six-eighths thoroughbred. When the
Australian remount price went up from the same
cause, the shoe began to pinch the dealer. Aus-
tralia was the dealer’s paradise when large
holdings of land were the rule, and the
cost of producing horses was consequently light.
Charles Baldwin told Willis that in the early
days of his Durham Court the cost of feeding a
horse was about a penny a year. A good bush
brood mare was worth about £5 and a blood stal-
lion £5O. Their produce in good horses was sold
at from £5 to £l2 each, at five years old. The
dealer for India got £45 for remounts, and up to
£lOO for chargers and
private orders, out of
the superior bush-
breds. Low prices for
horses continued till
about 10 years ago.
The Boer war opened
upabigmarket. Then
came the Russo-. Tap
disagreement. Since
then there has been
a more or less steady
horse demand for
Japan, the Philip-
pines, the Straits
Settlements, the
Dutch East Indies
and China. In addi-
tion to the foreign
trade, a large local
demand has arisen.
Where there was one
man in Sydney 40
years ago able to give
£2O for a pleasure
horse, there are now
plenty willing to give
£lOO. The Indian Government buyers set an
arbitrary low price, with annoying red-tape con-
ditions, and then trumpet forth that the Aus-
tralian horse has deteriorated. Their price of £45
in Calcutta means from £2O to £25 for the breeder
at this end, and he will get £3O from the buyer
for Japan or G'hina. Willis is sarcastic on
Colonel Goad, who descended on Australia
from India with condemnation of our horses.
The display of horses at the last Sydney Agricul-
tural Show was a complete answer to the cavillers.
With all their measurements one would think the
Indian buyers knew more about the drapery trade
than about horses. In the Sydney show, of 1100
entries, every class was well represented except
the thoroughbred. The reason is not far to seek.
An ordinary thoroughbred stallion is worth from
£3OO to £6OO a year, while a top-notclier can earn
up to £2OOO. No necessity to worry with showing
them. They are judged by the racing records.
There are 50 stallions of unimpeachable pedigree
in N.S.W. alone. Their second-class sons become
sires of cavalry and saddle-horses, and cost from
£2OO to £5OO. The progeny of such cannot be ex-
pected at cat’s-meat prices.
Hobson’s Bay has been invaded by a
school of sharks, and the blue blunt nose of the
adult man-eater is now poking into all the favorite
bathing places along the shore. The other Sun-
day afternoon a motor picnic, composed of a few
conspicuous citizens, a few theatricals, and si
bunch of xnen who were merely people, spread
itself in the ti-tree just beyond Black Rock.
Later five ordinary men, one actor, and one
prominent citizen went in to get externally wet.
When he had emulated the giddy flounder out in
the ripple for a few minutes, the prominent
person was horrified to find himself nosed from
behind by something that seemed to swim quickly.
He went head under, bit a mouthful out of
the sea, and came up, gurgling and half-
blinded with brine, to discover a dark
object looming through the wave. Then he

saw his latter end right there, and open-
ing his mouth to yell was gagged by the Southern
Ocean, After that, the prominent one began to
drown in real earnest, but presently, finding his
feet on the bottom, ho started to canter ashore,
yelling like a fiend. “A shark ! a shark ! ” he
shouted, and blundered into the dry sand, allout.
“It bit me ! See, the brute bit me ! ” gasped the
prominent person, showing a wounded shoulder,
and the whole picnic laughed. “ Why, man, it
was your own dog !” said the comedian. Faithful
Gelert, the tripe-hound, hhd been trying to keep
his master’s demented head above water.
A RHYME FROM MULGSLAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In solitudes remote this place is,
Where nothing much save empty space is,
A mole that on Australia’s face is.
In deserts old ;
A wild auriferous oasis
Where men seek gold.
They’re men of might, these mites of men,
Who dare the drought fiend in his den,
And scratch like some assiduous hen
In desert dust,
And have hard battling, now and then,
To raise a crust.
Existence to a rough bush-whacker,
With money tight and times grown slacker,
Yields little more than long hard yakker,
From year to year,
With luxuries of beer and ’baccer
By way of cheer.
When some success our bonds has riven,
We seek fierce joys, for which we’ve striven,
The world’s harsh dealing unforgiven,
With fasting savage,
By man’s compulsive passions driven
To spoil and ravage.
In some saloon, with some fast filly,
We sing, “ shout,” fool, flirt, sane or silly,
And paint the town till, dazed and dilly,
With drinking, girling,
The world seems one vast willy-willy,
‘ About us whirling.
The memory of times we’ve tarried,
The chances missed, the schemes miscarried,
The golden girl we might have married
Ohe summer time,
Are dagger thrusts but badly parried
By jingling rhyme.
Let Fortune pout and prove uncivil,
And turn away, what need to snivel ?
She’s but a statue on a swivel,
She’ll come round smiling.
Meanwhile with rhyme I fool and frivol,
Dull hours beguiling.
Australia’s sun is in my veins,
Her spirit in my being reigns.
Whatever penalties or pains
Fate for me nurses,
The love of her a joy remains
Through all reverses.
I picture her, superb yet slender,
A Queen in all the radiant splendor
Of womanhood, grave, gracious, tender—
Myself a wooer
Whose strong desire is yet to render
Some service to her.

“Vigilans”: Apart from birds and fish,
Australian bush game is poor eating. In my
wanderings I have eaten nearly everything—
kangaroo, opossum, bandicoot, porcupine, emu,
iguana, snake, crocodile and grubs. Kangaroo
rat and bandicoot make the best dishes, whether
baked in the oven or stewed. Porcupine tastes
like chicken. Iguana’s eggs, if taken from the
animal, are decidedly good. Carpet snake put
me in mind of an insipid fresh-water fish. Croco-
dile is horrible, and so are emu and opossum. In
Kimberley and the Northern Territory there is a
large white grub from three to eight inches long,
with a pair of sharp nippers in its head. It is found
in dead and rotten trees. These grubs, cooked on
the ashes, are excellent; they taste like rich
cream. The best meal I have had in the wilder-
ness was off a plain turkey, and the worst off an
iguana cooked on cowdung in the centre of the
tableland of the Territory, where these was not
enough wood to boil the billy. Amongst herbs,
fat-hen, when boiled, makes a good substitute for
spinach ; pig weed, either cooked or raw, is palat-
able;-and a diminutive cucumber grows thickly
on the plains of the Territory and is not bad
eating. The wild fruits are all very insipid and
mostly stone.
UNPROFESSIONAL.
J. BROKER STONE : “ Will you help a poor devil
out of a hole, sir ? ”
MR. COFFIN OAKE : “ Preposterous, my man.
I’m an undertaker! I put people into holes.”
“Oby ” : There is no great sense in firing
junks of rough English at Tom Austin, Chirnside
and other sports for introducing the rabbit to
Australia when, as a matter of fact, official tables
show that bunny came out with the first fleet and
figured on the first live-stock return compiled in
Australia. The “ Account of Live Stock in the
Settlement ” (1/5/1788), compiled by Andrew
Miller, Commissary, by direction of Governor
Phillip, gave:—Horses 7, head of cattle 7, sheep
29, swine 74, rabbits 5. Of the five rabbits three
were entered as the property of Governor Phillip
and two as belonging to officers.

“ Boswell ” : With all due respect to
“Agricola,” I fancy that the slaughter of the
laughing Johnny by the magpie during the breed-
ing season is by no means general. Certainly it
may happen that while an old patriarch jackass is
sitting absorbed in thought on his favorite branch,
the sharp mandibles of an irate mag may be
suddenly driven into his chest with fatal results,
but that would be an exceptional case. Also the
old chap was pro-
bably deaf, for an
attacking mag makes
a furious, slithering
noise with his wings.
No doubt the mag
is a raving lunatic
during breeding
time. He then im-
agines that every
living thing has de-
signs of burglary on
his premises, and
attacks alland sundry
without respect to
persons. Indeed, I
have often known
cases where it be-
came necessary to
shoot an exceptionally
demented mag. beside a
bush track or road in order
that children going to school
might escape his unfriendly
intentions. The spur-winged
plover is similarly constituted.
But apart from nesting time,
I am convinced, from ocular
experience, that Johnny is the
boss bird of the Australian
bush. Observe a number of
magpies picking up the scraps
round a bush camp. An old
crow swoops down, and they
abandon their food with cries
of terror. Just as the crow
is securing it Johnny takes
a hand. He charges the
crow full steam ahead, and
implants his strong beak
under the dusky one’s wing.
The feathers fly and so does
the crow, with a hoarse
croak of despair, leaving
Johnny master of the field.
Then John quietly picks up
the disputed chop bone, flies
up to his vantage point, thumps the trophy
several times to make sure that it is dead, and
gets whatever sustenance the wood-cutter has
left clinging to it.
* * * *
Bathurst is starting early to keep up the
centenary of the crossing of the Blue Mountains
by Blaxland, Lawson, and Wentworth, in 1813.
At a recent meeting of the Progress Association
it was resolved that all Western towns be invited
to join in the celebration. It was on Tuesday,
May 11, 1813, that the three explorers, with four
servants, four horses, and a heap of dogs (five, to
be strictly accurate), also with provisions, ammu-
nition, and other necessaries, left Blaxland’s farm,
at South Creek, to attempt a passage over the
Blue Mountains, between the Western River and
the River Grose. They crossed the ford on to
Emu Island at 4 p.m. On the first day they were
accompanied by two gentlemen, but history does
not name them. On Sunday; June 6, they re-
crossed the river, after breakfast, and dined at
Blaxland’s farm, having travelled to the foot of
Mount York. To-day it is still a question whether
Blaxland or Lawson was the leader.

Who folks said was incurably ill;
But he’s now well and sound,
Here’s the cure that he found—
Laxo-Tonic — a wonderful pill.

They are telling a story of Paddy
McNathan, the sporting publican, that when a
certain Milesian bruiser came to his town on a
mission to mop up the Australian middles and
heavies, and make a pile in the process, Paddy
took to him like a brother. Slanigan, the
bruiser, was given the run of the house. He had
small cash, but his credit was unlimited. Paddy’s
faith was so great that he stood to him for a grand
supper, to come off in the hotel after Slanigan’s
great fight. McNathan believed there was
nothing Australian in it with his protege, and
for a month only Slanigan was talked in
his bar. After the fight, Paddy McNathan
arrived home sooner than was anticipated, and
in a heated condition. “Clear up them
things,” he yelled. “There’s goin’ to be no
supper here. Get out o’ me house ! ” He barked
at a few early guests and they fled. It was an
hour later when a faint knocking was heard on
the outer door. “To the fiend wid you ! ” roared
Paddy. The knocking was repeated. “ It’s me,
Mr. McNathan,” said a feeble voice, “Mike
Slanigan. I’m stoppin’ here.” “Be hill, you are,
then,” raved Paddy. “ Ye’re stoppin’ there till
yeh pay me bill.” Slanigan had been donkey-
licked in two rounds.
“ W.G-.H.” from the Westralian way-
back
Fully 80 per cent, of the laundry work in Kalgoorlie
and Boulder City is in the hands of Monkey, Ape and Co.
Taking into consideration the professedly democratic
spirit prevailing among the workers on the fields, this is
a staggerer. I have heard the man who complacently
hands his “ biled rag” to the Jap for treatment discourse
blatantly on the necessity for a White Australia, and
enthuse over the Alien Immigration Act. Still he gives
underclothes to the baboon washerman to launder rather
than to his own countrywomen, probably because he saves
2d. on the transaction, or, more probably, because he really
hasn’t brains enough to see that he is encouraging the
evil he denounces. Curse No. 2is Ali Gunny Bhag, who
competes with carriers, enjoys almost a monopoly in the
gardening business, and in general makes himself a most
smellful and sinful pest. His quarters are generally close
to the town, and his filthy and insanitary hovels are the
best possible argument for a White Australia. In a good
many instances he has taken unto himself a white woman
(and probably torn the marriage-tie to pieces in the pro-
cess), and the result is often to be seen in a motley collec-
tion of brats playing outside the hovels. If the miner
were told that a scheme was on foot to dispense with him
and transfer his job to cheap Asiatic labor, there w'ould
be Gehenna to pay. Yet he sees nothing inconsistent in
bestowing his custom on an Asiatic, who is ousting White
Australian competitors. It is surely up to political
leaders and heads of unions to impress these facts on the
rank and file, and after a time self-respect will do the
rest.
Pimples, eruptions, blotches, scales, ulcers,
and eczema are all caused by bad blood. Chamber-
lain’s Stomach and Liver Tablets are wonder
workers in the cure of all disorders caused by bad
or impure blood. They eliminate all poisonous
matter by enriching the blood and enabling it to
make new and healthy tissues. Pure blood means
perfect health, and if you will use Chamberlain’s
Tablets they will give you good health, and a pure
clean skin free from pimples and blotches.*
When the Victorian country kid hasn’t
cow on his soul, there is orchard produce to weigh
it down. Thus at Mildura (Vic.), whioh is a
warm spot m summer time, the local youngster
devotes himself to fruit-picking. His pa is very
often a fruit-grower, and, as the fruit ripens just
on or a little after the nipper’s Christmas vacation,
the parent thinks it is proper, as well as economical,
to be the sombre gentleman who always finds
something for idle hands to do. Therefore he
sets Willie grafting in the orchard, in a tempera-
ture that often overworks the meroury. But pa
doesn’t mind, as Willie is almost equal to a man,
and hasn’t the effrontery to request a man’s wages.
Moreover, in order to make sure that Willie’s
holidays do clash with the fruit-ripening season,
Willie’s pa connives with the Government at
Melbourne, and also with the local Board of
Advice, which comprises quite a number of pas
who strongly sympathise with the hard and
dreadful lot of Willie’s pa. Then the crowd—
Willie’s pa, the Board of Advice, and the Mel-
bourne Government —conspire to arrange Willie’s
recreation in the orchard. A lot of the fruit
doesn’t ripen until February, therefore Willie’s
furlough is prolonged until February. If it were
not so prolonged his poor father would have to
find a job for a man. So there is something to be
said for Willie, though little can be said for his
father, except that after this season he stands in
great danger of having to hire labor.
“ Oby ” : Re a vexed question which has
lately cropped up again, there is no historical
doubt about the Rev. Richard Johnson having
fully recovered what he expended in the building
of his wattle and dab church at the intersection of
Hunter and Elizabeth streets, Sydney. G. B.
Barton, Henniker Heaton and others present the
case as though the chaplain, finding that Phillip
would not build a church, conferred the blessing
of a tabernacle on the community out of his own
official salary of 10s. a day. It is true that in
July, 1793, he started men to build, but the
structure was not only the first Australian church,
it also constituted the first ecclesiastical scandal.
When the reverend architect sent in his account to
the Government the items were objected to on
the direct allegation that he had doctored the
accounts and falsified the bill. Here was the in-
voice presented by the chaplain for the erection
of Australia’s first church :
Paid 20£ gallons of spirits, 116 cwt. flour, 18Jlb. pork,
35Jlb. beef, 51b. tobacco, 31b. tea. Total value, £l4 9s. 9d.
Paid in dollars, £59 18s. Grand total, £74 7s. 9d.
Lieutenant-Governor Grose valued the spirits
and other commodities at £7 145., and said that
the original chaplain was trying to take the
young colony down for £6 15s. 9d. He emphati-
cally refused to pay. Johnson, however, appealed
to the Colonial Office in England and pulled
wires there till he collected his bill in full.

“ Cict ” : Where lies the true cause of
that depraved taste so frequently displayed by the
current foal? Not in the sex, the breed, nor yet
in the age of the brute doth it lie, for I have
descried the mother or the mate of the youthful
equine of all ages, sexes and breeds practically
destitute of tail-hair, the result of insane chawings
by the baby horse. Moreover, the youngster’s
absurd mastication of mother’s hair is not con-
fined to any particular month or season of the
year, but breaks out at all times. The victim of
the senseless attack is not always the severest
sufferer, for Moke, junior, quite often succumbs
himself after a prolonged bout on stolen plumes.
And, further, what maniacal instinct urges any
staid old hack to Submit to such indignity as this,
as neither his appearance nor his fly-flail’s handi-
ness are improved a whit ? The reasons handed in

THE WOODOARTER’S DOG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He knows the deeply-rutted roads that end in a splay of
tracks
He knows the twists in the winding oaths that lead to
the new-made stacks
And he loves the sound of the grumbling wheels, and the
clip of his master’s axe.
He also knows, as the old horse knows, the points of the
highway’s code,
As he lopes along in the heat and dust in the shadow
cast by the load—
For he wouldn’t stay at home, not he, when the cart is on
the road.
Though it’s thirty miles to the town and back he does it
all with a will,
And jumps to hearten the toiling horse when they come
to the long, steep hill ;
Or he hikes through a fence for scent of a hare, for lie
cannot bear to he still.
When the curs rush out from the roadside homes and
yelp at the passing dray,
He shows the set of his ivory fangs, and they’ve nothing
more to say—
He sinks his head with a savage snarl, and the mongrels
slink away.
For, being always a business dog, he hasn’t the time to
fool;
He may hail the tyke with a neighbor’s cart, but he’s
quite reserved, as a rule —
Somewhat distant to larger dogs, and to small ones
rather cool.
But his soul unbends in the great free bush when he
follows the twisting tracks,
For he loves the crash of the falling trees and the smell
of the new-cut stacks,
The grind and groan of the rumbling wheels, and the
clip of his master’s axe.
VIC.
C. G. A. COLLES.
OUR PROHIBITION TOWN.
[For The Bulletin.]
In a cosy nook by the bay it lies—
The dreamiest spot on all God’s earth -,
No more the shouts of men arise,
No jovial laughter speaks of mirth.
Slowest of all that is slow and dull,
Ringed by the grey old wasted scrub—
Only the scream of the white-winged gull
Sounds o’er our township without a pub.
And this you will find when they put down beer,
That joy goes forth and is seen no more ;
All is solemn and cold and drear,
And the thistles grow by each cottage door.
The swaggies even refuse to stay,
And the drovers, with stockwhip’s great hubbub,
Pass thro’ the bog-lands, a mile away,
Sooner than ride by our shuttered pub.
But some day, mayhap, shall the Devil come—
And he’s bound to come with a fiery thirst—
And he may, in his search for a good, strong ruin,
Upon our dead little township burst;
And may I be there when blood is shed,
And, casting mercy to winds afar,
May I assist to pound the head
Of the parson crowd that closed our bar !

BEST OF QUALITY.
PERFECT PURITY.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
Andrew Turnbull, who died lately at
Braemar, George-street, Windsor (N.S.W.), in his
75th year, was a pioneer worker on the Hawkes-
bury and Nepean Rivers. In the middle or centre
or stomach of the ’sixties of last century he super-
intended the fixing of the piers of the old bridge
over the Nepean at Emuford. He and his partner,
the late William Dixon, built the bridge over the
Hawkesbury at Windsor. Many other bridges
and public works in the State were constructed by
Andrew Turnbull. He started the Emu metal and
gravel industry, of which concern he was managing
director up to the time of his death. Turnbulls of
one kind and another were settled on the Hawkes-
bury in the early ’thirties of the old dead century,
and probably at an even earlier date.

the law came forward, one of whom—a lady—said that if

If the Premier is at all the man we think he
is, the mere fact of his being tarred and feathered
will not interfere with the ordinary performance
of his duties.
In the last big Broken Hill strike, when
the leaders were arrested for conspiracy, they
sought the late C. C. Kingston’s help as counsel;
but Kingston couldn’t act —he was only a member
of the S. Acs. Bar. In this present trouble it was
Paris Nesbit, K.C., to whom the unions first
turned after Tom Mann and the others had been
arrested ; but Paris Nesbit had to shake his head
for the same reason. Then “ Jim ” Gannon was
called for from Sydney.
Mr. J. Hume, of Collingwood, Gunning
(N.S.W.), underwent an operation in Sydney and
died on Wednesday, January 27, aged 53 years.
The daily press said that he was a descendant of
Hamilton Hume, the explorer, which is scarcely
correct, as Hamilton H. died without issue. The
deceased was the eldest son of James Kennedy
and Annie Hume, sister of Hamilton the ex-
plorer. He adopted the name of Hume when he
inherited the property of Kennedy Hume, who
was murdered by bushrangers at Gunning in 1840.
When Mr. F. M. Darley accepted a seat
in the N.S.W. Legislative Council (September,
1868), he was asked by a relative if he had no idea
of trying to enter the Assembly. And the awful
imported person replied that he could not think
of mixing with the class of men which was there.
In the Legislative Assembly in 1868 were Arnold,
Stephen Brown, Wm. Forster, Isaacs, S. A.
Joseph, Dr. Lang, William McLeay, Parkes,
Robertson, Saul Samuel, T. W. Smart, the two
Suttors (the elder ones), Windeyer, Wisdom,
James Martin, etc. And all these men shocked
Darley’s finer feelings, and gave him the cold and
elevated shudders down the back.
“ J.,” about a peer who is now dust:—
The Earl of Leicester, whose death was cabled last
week, had other claims to fame than his descent from Sir
Edward Coke and his Lord-Lieutenancy of Norfolk. He
was the greatest expert in the science of covert-shooting
in tiie United Kingdom. When he got too old to shoot
himself he continued to take a delight in directing bird-
massacres on his Norfolk estate for the benefit of his
friends. At organising these affairs he was unsurpassed. A
friend took me once to look on at a big shoot at Holk-
ham. The doomed fowl were pheasants. All the morning
the keepers and their under-strappers, in their white
smocks witli colored collars and wristlets, had been tap-
tapping on the trees with sticks. When the “guns”
took their stand outside a certain high-placed clump of
trees every pheasant on the estate had been shooed into
the undergrowth, nearby, ready to fly over and be shot.
This was the triumph of the old Earl’s manoeuvring.
Ranged in line in the hollow below the trees were half-a-
dozen of the best shots in England. Behind each man
stood loaders, with ammunition and extra guns. At a
given signal, a loud tap was heard, and the gorgeous
birds began to whirl madly over. There is an idea
abroad in this country that pheasants are easy to kill.
They aren’t. I have tried it, and I have seen others —
crack local pigeon-shots—try and fail miserably. It is a
special art, like the spot stroke at billiards, or Brother
Johnson’s way of being a Methodist. The way these
crack shots performed at Holkham was marvellous. It
was not so much that they practical]! 7 never missed, but
that they killed, grabbed fresh guns from their loaders,
and killed again with such astounding quickness. For a
brief time the air rained dead pheasants; they crashed
to earth behind the shooters in scores, in hundreds, in
thousands. The rare days on which these affairs came
off were the golden ones in the life of the old peer who
passed over last week. The only thing that really
maddened him was when a guest missed or merely
wounded one of his fowl. It is said that a
new-comer was shooting at Holkham once when
he observed a keeper jotting down notes in a
pocket-book. On cross-examination the servitor owned
that he was keeping tally of the guest’s misses, also of
the number of times he smote his bird on its tail or other
non-lethalpart. The total was to be submitted to “his
lordship” in the evening, so that the latter might judge
whether or no he would ever suffer the culprit to shoot
on his property again.
Westoalia’s queerest old cuss dropped out
of Parliament recently. He was very small
potatoes in the House, but he is a big
man up in his district, of which he owns large
patches. His few contributions to Hansard
were brief and to the point. “ All yous can
go to ’ell,” he informed a ribald Opposition
on one occasion. Whereupon a rude mem-
ber asked —if that was the destination of the
ewes, what would become of the wethers ?
The old gentleman’s most celebrated speech
was.in advocacy of the .Circuit Court sys-
tem, not then in vogue in the West. Like
most great speeches, it was a model of
brevity. “I reckon we oughter ’ave some
circus courts up our way,” he said ; and
dropped into hisseat again. Though he was
a shrewd business man, malicious old inhabi-
tants say that he used to keep his books by
drawing diagrams on the wall.
Tommy Bent assisted in celebrating the
jubilee of Brighton (Vic.) the other evening. He
pointed out that in 50 years Brighton had pro-
gressed from bullock drays to electric trams.
Brighton has also progressed from a horseback
postal service to wireless telegraphy. But it has
clung to Bent. It couldn’t well help the electric
trams and the wireless system, but it could help
Bent—and it didn’t.
“ B.” : Frank Anstey, the Victorian Labor
member, who broke down and wept copiously in
public because the railway men had promised to
vote for Watt, is a very emotional man—a mere
bundle of nerves. Were it not for this he would
probably be leader of the party, for his ability
and sincerity are recognised by everyone who
knows him. Anstey has had a very hard life
of it. He ran away to sea as a boy, and
endured the hardships of life before the mast for
many years. In his nomination paper he described
himself as a laborer, but as a matter of fact he was
caretaker at the Working Men’s College before he
got a much harder job in Parliament. Anstey is
a convinced Socialist, and one of the most extreme
men in the House.
From “ Melb.,” who has a habit of
bedecking his screed with capital letters :
Our Hangman, Mr. Smith, has been sent by the magis-
trates to the local Bedlam. He is a Mister, not a crimi-
nal. Said the Police : “He suffers from delusions, your
Worships ; People following him about.” Bench: “Oh,
Ah, yes! Men he has hanged.” Police: “No, Your
Worships, not at all. He imagines that Young Girls are
following him about and teasing him. He threatens he
will make an example of one of them. It is pure delu-
sion.” In short, our Scragger has fancied himself in a
continual state of St. Anthony. It was feared he would
take some innocent girl, and string her to a beam. Smith
kept a little Tuck Shop in Carlton. Good ladies would
say, “You must find it hard to make ends meet.’’ He
would say, “ I gits a little bit of a job now and agin.”
Nobody had the faintest suspicion what the job was. A
little girl thought she saw him going into the gaol. This
caused a rumor that he was in the Government employ,
and won him extra respect. Mrs. Boltrope has a shud-
dery remembrance of having once talked to him about
“The Pore Man as was’Ung this marnin’.” Said the genial
Smith, “ Oh, Mum, it’s nothink like so crool as you’d
imagine. All depends on the official who officiates. If he
has a nice soft hand, and a soft cord, it’s no more than a
ship gently sliding off the stocks.” The morning news-
papers mention that Smith was a Spiritualist, and
attended stances, where he was ever welcome, being an
excellent medium. This, and not scragging, led his
mind astray. When the hands were all laid on the table,
at Madame Empyrean’s, everyone used to remark on
what peculiar hands Smith had. His was a full hand,
large, massive and clammy-white, the most spiritual that
anybody ever saw. He would glance up at the ceiling,
and sigh out: “ I wonder where we goes to when we’re
gone.” Then a fishy eye would wander round the necks
of the ladies and gents, present. The first Melbourne
hangman on record was one-eyed Bamford. Then came
a Caliban named Gately. Next, Elijah Upjohn—a sug-
gestive Heavenly-Chariot name. After this the authori-
ties decided that the Hangman should not be known.
Several have thus passed away without figuring in the
chronicles of glory.

COBDEN PRESS: "He don't want them things
now—he's quite recovered. He moved his
right eyelid; at least I think he did!"
Though Chief Justice Darley had resided
in New South Wales for 47 years, yet the sale of
his effects drew attention to the fact that Aus-
tralian books and pictures were conspicuous by
their absence. One view of Sydney Harbor
adorned the walls, and Australian "literature
was represented by “Convict Once” and
one book by “Rolf Boldrewood,” who, by the
way, is the Chief’s brother-in-law. Darley’s suc-
cess in avoiding anything Australian approached
the marvellous.
Of late years Victoria has had frightfully
bad luck with its hangmen. First there was
Jones—steady-going, sober-sided .Tones—who
figured on the Government’s pay-sheet as “a
messenger.” He cut his throat in a bath at the
Melbourne gaol, rather than put the late Mrs.
Ivnorr through the drop. His successor collided
with the Law, of which he was an unobtrusive
ornament, through an undue fondness for short-
skirted damsels of flighty habits. And now Smith,
the last of the line, has been relegated to a lunatic
asylum.
Mr. W. Cox, Humffray Street, Ballarat,
Vic., writes : “I have been a great sufferer from
rheumatism, and having obtained the greatest
relief by the use of Chamberlain’s Pain Balm I
cannot speak too highly of it. Whenever I feel
the rheumatic pains coming on, I give the affected
parts a few applications and it always arrests the
attack. I know several people who have used
Chamberlain’s Pain Balm, and they all speak in
the highest praises of it.”*
The CRESSBROOK ESTATE. —In our advertising columns
will be found a notice with reference to the sale of the
third section of the famous Oressbrook Estate, which
will take place at Toogoolawah on Saturday, 6th March
next. The section for sale will include 8000 acres of the
best land on this well-known station, notably the great
plain paddock on the eastern bank of the Brisbane River,
access to which is being provided for by the erection of a
substantial bridge. The plan shows 52 farms, varying in
areas from 50 acres to 705 acres, most of the choicest
portion being subdivided into small blocks, so as to give
all persons a chance of securing a homestead. The
character of the lucerne land on the Upper Brisbane
River is too well known to need comment, and the
exceptional advantages offered by the Oressbrook Estate
should attract buyers from all parts of the States. The
Nestle and Anjrlo-Swiss Condensed Milk Company are in
full swing on the estate, and are paying at the present
time upwards of £ll,OOO per annum to suppliers, at the
rate of from sjd. to 6d. per gallon. The railway station is
right on the land, and the terms are up to 20 years with
interest at 5 per cent. An illustrated pamphlet and
plan is obtainable from the auctioneers, Isles, Love & Co.,
Queen Street, Brisbane, or from the local agents, Hugh
Duff & Co., F. E. Pulsford, Wilson Anderson & Co., 88
Pitt Street, Sydney.*
Melbourne people are remarking of the
recent police “raid” on the clairvoyant tribe
that Mrs. Rising, the high priestess of the cult, has
been carefully omitted from the bunch of cases.
Mrs. Turner, who comes next to Mrs. Rising in the
estimation of Believers, was prosecuted and fined
£lO and Costs for revealing to a bachelor police-
man the whereabouts of his non-
existent wife—but how about Mrs.
Rising ? She’s the lady who can reveal
all mysteries at a price. David Syine
used to consult her about his business
enterprises, until her predictions had a
run of bad luck and David’s faith
weakened. As a medical clairvoyant,
she enjoys a splendid practice, and
her power of peering into the interior of
a patient who has forwarded her a lock
of hair by a third party is a matter
of common knowledge—in spiritual-
istic circles. Outside of those circles
the stories of Mrs. Rising’s failures
are many and humorous. Her
methods of fishing for information
from her clients are no different
from those of the 15 men and
women whom the Melbourne
police brought to court. She’s
an impudent, abusive old char-
acter when a disgusted client gives
the lie to her occult babble, and if
the police want evidence against her they can
get miles of it without laying any new traps.
But if she were prosecuted, she would call a
number of well-known men for the defence,
and they don’t want to be well-known in this
connection ; therefore Mrs. Rising will be able
to pose as the old original genuine clairvoyante
to whom the Lawtook off its hat.
“ Singemoine ” : The Steinheil affair now
agitating France in general and Paris in particu-
lar, has resulted in the assassination of a Name.
Madame Steinheil, who is accused of murdering
her husband and her mother, is one of those dear,
irresponsible matrons whom anyone with 1 franc
50 centimes to spend may meet between the
covers of a Gallic novel of passion. She appears
to have had a whole academy of lovers, and they
all combined, with the full sanction and approval
of M. Steinheil, to keep the domestic pot simmer-
ing. Occasionally, as a matter of form, Steinheil,
who was an artist, painted one or other of the
lovers, and got a big price for the work. Evi-
dence given at the trial last month went to show
that Felix Faure, the last President of the French
Republic, was one of the binned and loved. Said
Steinheil to a friend a while before his death :
I will tell you something, but keep it to yourself.
Felix Faure was Mme. Steinheil’s lover, and she was with
him when he was struck down. I beg you do not repeat
what I tell you. lam already worried sufficiently about
the letters (f.e., Faure’s letters). I don’t know what my
wife has done with them. Mv mother-in-law said to me only
the other day that the best thing would be to send them
back to the proper quarter. X don’t know what has been
done. Anyhow, it is all over between my wife and myself
since this affair. She may have two or three friends, or
more, if she likes. As you become old you become
reasonable.
Faure was to all appearances the typically
righteous bourgeois. He had no discernible vices
barring a love of food, and a still greater love of
crowned heads. The disclosure of bis lapse has
occasioned the Parisians much delight and sur-
prise. They dance merrily at the wake of their
old idol’s reputation, and refuse to be uncomfort-
able.
DAVID STORRER, M.H.R., OF TASMANIA.
“S.” : I saw Frank Anstey do his grand
breakdown and bathe the platform with tears at
Newport (Vie.) the other day, and it was the
most effeminate and hysterical display I have seen
in 30 years’ experience of politics. The speaker
was explaining that his inquiries convinced him
that a great number of the railway men were
going to vote for Willie Watt and against Labor
candidate Fenton, and the floodgates suddenly
opened and Anstey wept. With his face buried
in his hands he sobbed aloud. The gathering of
brawny iron workers and carriage builders re-
spected the orator’s great sorrow, but it is sur-
prising that the tearful fit did not provoke a storm
of derision. I remember when “Mint-Sauce'’
Jones wept at Ballarat. He never got another
hearing throughout the campaign. But “Mint
Sauce” was weeping over his own political funeral;
it was Fenton’s funeral that made Anstey break
down.
COLD FEET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pekin, January s.—Yuanshikai was dismissed on the
plea that he is suffering from an affection of the foot.
Grey matter of the crinkled brain beneath the states-
man’s honored hat,
Not there, not there the fault and blattae
When wrathful citizens exclaim,
“ Behold he hatli a rat! ”
Yea, rat, and rats, and all is rat, exceeding ratty is the
time
Forgive us, Lord, for that we said
Of statesman with disordered iiead,
In prose and eke in rime.
Behold, Imperial China drags the mighty from his lordly
seat —
Where John o’er cabbage verdure grins.
I ask what were the Viceroy’s sins.
“ Too muehee no good feet.”
Oh, word of knowledge from afar, Celestial wisdom up-
to-date !
When statesmen falter, flinch and fail,
No more the fatted brain bewail,
Nor the beratted pate.
Australian heads are stout and good, Australian brains
are hard to beat,
What ails our statesmen of a day
Who come so fast yet never stay ?
Oh, can it be their feet ?
-•hy shrinks from war the man of might? Why faints and
falls the hero bold ?
Why has Australia failed to find
A statesman of a stalwart mind ?
Alas, their feet are cold !
JvIAORILAND.
D.M.W.
A SONG OF CHAINS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1.-isten, amigos, camerados and brethren !
listen, while I, Mann, descant on your miseries!
List, while I belch out a proud defiance in the face of the
“ Fat Fifty ” !
Gaze while I hurl a hissing curse, red-hot, in the paunch
of the Proud and Portly,
And laugh, my friends, with a wild, demoniac laugh,
while I grind out a grim anathema and gird grue-
somely at the crawling myrmidons of beleaguering
Capital.
For I am the Breaker of Chains, the proud Champer of
Bits, the Eternal Rebel who yelleth, “Arise and
Rend ! ”
A born jaw-warrior am I ; jaw-potent, jaw-valiant—a
wild wielder of the weapon of Samson i
Unto you I say, “Awake, O slaves, smite them hip and
thigh ! ”
Unto Paunch I say, with fiery iteration, “Away!
Avaunt! Absquatulate and aroint thee ! ”
And if in due time he do neither “ avaunt ” nor “ aroint ”
himself,
Then sink I down distraught in the pub of a sordid son
of Pelf
And drink with flashing eye the foaming ginger beer of a
proud and haughty freeman 1
Am I not, now as ever, prepared to fight—even to the
death—with a dire and deadly jaw ?
Prison I fear not; troops I defy ;
Cannon, powder, bayonets, bombs—l say “Let’em all
come 1 ”
I, Mann, erst of London, erst of the beer-trade, erst of
many trades,
Erect I stand ; tweak the nose of the law ; jeer at the
sacred name of J.P. ; laugh at police ; gibe at the
military ;
And cast, at times, a pint of mud right in the Eye of
Authority !
Believe and believe me, O camerados—
There is but one Brotherhood and I, even I, am its per-
manent Prophet!
I am he who will assuredly save you,
But ask me not, camerados, for troublesome details.
It lies, after all, entirely with yourselves
And below is the modus operandi
Lift up the chain that hinds you, O bondmen,
Take it, rend it—break it, send it
(Shattered into a thousand shivering fragments),
Send it hurling, send it whirling
All in a shower of myriad deadly missiles
Deep in the paunch of your foe, the craven and Crapu-
lous Fat One !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

The directors of this co. evidently believe that, if share-
holders get al5 per cent, div., they shouldn’t want any
information. Certainly they don’t get any to speak of
even if they do want it. The profit is shown “ after
deducting repairs and renewals, depreciation of plant,
and all other charges,” and there is no hint as to what
depreciation is thus provided for. Whatever provision is
made—that is to say, whatever revenue is put aside
ostensibly as a set-off against wear and tear and natural
decay—is no doubt put into plant, mains, etc. Ten years
ago the plant, mains, land, buildings, etc., which had
been charged up to capital account stood at
£1,036,257. The sales of gas, etc., brought in £174,475,
or at the rate of £33 13s. 6d. per cent, per annum on the
investment. (That, of course, is gross revenue.) In the
past six months £271,778 came in from a fixed investment
of £1,240,979 ; so the rate now is £43 16s. per cent. Put
in another way, the co. is supposed to have spent £204,722
of capital in 10 years ; and the increased revenue is at the
rate of £194,606 per annum. It is possible, of course, that
£204,722 of works may bringin£l94,6o6 of annual revenue ;
but somehow it doesn’t seem at all probable, particularly
as the extensions of mains, etc., would naturally be in
the parts which would yield the least revenue. No doubt
the old mains have yielded more revenue as population
has become more congested ; still, it seems highly prob-
able that, in order to produce the results mentioned, a
large amount of unmentioned profit has been stuffed away
into new revenue-producing works. Of course it isn’t
all profit that has been thus stuffed away; some of it is
a fair set-off against depreciation. On the other hand,
with a co. as old as this, when renewals are charged up
to profit and loss the amount required to be set aside for
depreciation isn’t very great; because the renewals that
are being called for to-day are as great in proportion to
revenue as they are likely to be 20 years hence.
Besides the reserves shown above, the Austra-
lian (Sydney) Gaslight Co. has—Premiums on new shares,
£232,227 ; reserve for replacement of meters, £19,133.
The paid share capital is £799,998 ; so, adding the pre-
miums to that, the total capital put into the co. is
£1,032,225. This brings down the 15 per cent. div. to
an average of 11J per cent. As to the future,
it is impossible on the published figures to be very
definite. On the fixed investment (that is, capital put
into mains, plant, etc.) it is clear that the gross revenue
is very much greater than it was 10 years ago ; but it is
impossible to say how much of that gross revenue is being
absorbed by extra costs, and how much is being stuffed
away into new plant, etc., in view, probably, of keener
competition from municipal electric lighting and power
by-and-bye. There may be very good reasons why the
management doesn’t want either consumers or the Labor
party to know its real position ; and it is to be heartily
congratulated upon the skill with which it succeeds in
telling nothing that really matters. Also it is to be just
as heartily congratulated upon being in a community
which allows itself to be kept thus in utter darkness.

This shows a remarkable spring in profits; and inquiry
will show that it must be due to much-increased sales at
the same old high prices :

Possibly the co. sold an extra quantity of gas while Mel-
bourne was showing Brother Jonathan’s Fleet around;
but making some allowances on that account, the growth
of the output is notable, and all the more notable when
the competition of the municipal electric-lighting and
power works is considered. Yet it is the sort of thing
that is to be expected. In 1907 there toed the mark in
Victoria 4210 bridegrooms whose usual place of residence
is the metropolis ; and, though here and there one house
is shut up when a new bridegroom goes put into the
world, in most cases marriage means an extra house to
demand a gas meter and call for a stove. Between 1891
and 1901—10 years—the population of greater Melbourne
increased only from 490,896 to 496,079; in the next six
years there was a spring to 538,000. No wonder the in-
vestor who thinks over these things is prepared to pay
£lO Ss. for shares which, 10 years ago, were valued at
only £7 2s.
The Melbourne Gas Co.’s reserves are made up
like this Premiums on shares, £466,535 ; formal re-
serves, £79,864 ; meter renewal fund, £21,454 ; undivided
profit, £2988. But there is probably a large reserve not
shown as such, and represented by works paid for out of
profit. Under the rotten N.S.W. Companies Act, which
in many respects seems designed to assist the person who
is out to fleece or fool the public, it is not necessary for a
gas co. to give any particulars of any consequence ; in
Victoria, however, the cos. are required to tell a lot, and
having to tell so much, most of them reckon that they
might as well tell everything. This co., therefore, shows
exactly what revenue it does spend on new works ; in the
past six years £152,847 has gone that way. With so much
new works paid for out of profit and not charged up to
capital, the profit on the works at the figure at which
they stand in the books moves up, of course :
Total charged to Return on
Capital Account. Capital.
December, 1902 ..£2,152,788 £4 13 9 per cent.
December, 1908 .. 2,149,842 615 4 ~
Certainly all the £152,847 spent in six years may not be
true profit; some of it may be a set-off against wear and
tear and decay of other plant, mains, etc. But repairs,
maintenance and renewals are charged direct to profit
and loss. An exception is made with regard to meters :
the cost of renewing these alleged perjurers is provided
out of a special reserve fund, and shown above as written
off. Also the amounts carried to “ gas stoves account ”
are shown as written off. (This last is an item represent-
ing practically a subsidy to the users of gas stoves, which
are let out at a charge which isn’t in itself quite profit-
able.) The co.’s share capital is £857,210in 171,442 shares
fully paid to £5, and there are 28,558 shares authorised
but unissued. There is also a debenture debt of £798,000,
of which £460,000, carrying 5 per cent., will be repayable
1910-12 ; and over that transaction the co. can no doubt
see an annual saving of £4500 sticking out in the near
future.

Something is alleged to have been written-off to cover de-
preciation ; but there is no telling what it is. “Manufacture
and distribution of gas, rents, rates and taxes, renewals,
lamp-lighting, bad debts, depreciation, management,
etc.,” are all lumped into one item. If he likes to take it,
this gives the man who is inside a fine advantage over the
man who is outside. The co. may be either making a
great deal more profit than it is showing, and stuffing it
away under the cloak of “renewals, depreciation, etc.”—
putting it, in short, into new plant, mains, etc. —or it
may be barely earning its dividend, and making, no ade-
quate provision for the big renewals that will have to be
faced later on. If the management is adopting the
former policy, the insider will know that, presuming the
village doesn’t slump, an increased dividend or a distribu-
tion of shares at par is likely to come along presently;
if it is adopting the other policy, the insider knows fairly
well that a reduced div. is highly probable in the future.
In most cases when a gas co. so carefully hides its proceed-
ings it is assumed that things are better than they are
alleged to be ; the hiding is put down to the directors’
dread of telling the truth and awakening the municipal
devil. The assumption has panned out all right in a good
many cases ; bub there is an obvious danger in it, and
some day some group of investors will wake up to find
themselves the victims of the confidence trick. Under
these circumstances, before one plunges into any gas
co.’s shares, it is just as well for him to make friends with
some director or other who is allowed behind the
counter. With this co. a distribution of shares
at par was made during the year; and the capi-
tal is now £22,500 instead of £20,000, as it was 12
months ago. The gas-sales, etc., for the y ear totalled £8624;
that is, 34 per cent, on capital and reserves. In 1903 the
capital was £15,000; and the sales, etc., £4532, equal to
29 per cent, on capital and reserves. One assumption
from this is that Manly nowadays stays up later at night
to put oil on its sunburn ; but a more feasible one is
that the co. has piled up reserves which it doesn’t show-
in other words, has made undisclosed profits which it has
put into plant, more or less as a set-off against the de-
preciation of the other assets. The div. for the year is
shown as 9£ per cent.; that means 4£ per cent, for the
first half-year and 5 per cent, for the second half, and the
market is evidently assuming that the 5 per cent, per
half-year is going to last. The co.’s debts total £2197 ;
its liquid assets (before paying the half-year’s div. =£1060)
were £1778. In view of the rapid extension of the suburb
and the evident need for more capital, further calls on
the last issue of 10,000 shares, now paid to ss. (£2500), are
probable ; and as that capital will no doubt also bring in 10
per cent, (for a time, anyhow), these are calls that in-
vestors will welcome. The market has, however, dis-
counted all that, for the ss. paid shares are actually
worth 235., so that until the calls are made they will
bring in only £2 3s. 6d. per cent. Even after they have
been fully paid the shares will at that price bring in only
£5 55., working on the basis of a 10 per cent, dividend.

This little bank, which keeps itself to Ballarat, and is the
only institution of that English type in Australasia,
works away very largely on its own money. For every
£1 of its own, it holds in deposits, etc., only just 30s. of
the public’s. Its liquid assets are £88,636 ; the debt to
the public is £240,743. Deducting the liquid assets from
the debt, there remains £152,108 of the public’s money ;
and, apart from bank premises, there are £312,122 of non-
liquid assets. That is a very comfortable position
for depositors to be in; assets would have
to be very rotten if, on the average, they
didn’t pan out at 9s. 6d. in the £. Of course
profits couldn’t be high in a business of this
nature unless it went in for loans at high rates on poor
security. In that case, though the profits would be high
sometimes, they would be liable to be cut down below
zero at others. As a matter of fact, even if this bank’s
profits are low, they are very uniform (of course, there
may be internal reserves to regulate things). In propor-
tion to capital (£85,000) and reserves, recent results have
been: —

has still 37 p.c. of its debts in cash or Govt, stock.

In issuing its present accounts this co. does for itself what
an attempt is made in the current number of The Lone
Hand to do in a general way—to show, that is, what an
estate has to pay for having its trustee work done by a
professional trustee co. which has put up a money guaran-
tee that it'will go straight, and won’t either die or run
away. This co. takes an estate of £lO,OOO which will be
20 years in winding up, and shows that the total commission
will amount to £25 a year, assuming (1) that the income
averages 5 per cent, on the investment, and (2) that the
income isn’t rent (a higher charge is made for collecting
rents). The Lone Hand showed that a £25,000 estate,
yielding 4 per cent., and taking eight years to wind up,
would pay £825 in all, or Ss. 3d. per cent, per annum ; or
if it was a 40-year trust the payment would work out at
3s. 3d. per cent, per annum—a very small payment for a
great service. Of the £825 the corpus commission (that
is, the single charge on the capital value) would be £625.
This amount various trustee cos. deal with in various
ways. This co. apparently passes it over to a “com-
mission reserved acceunt,” and draws upon that account
gradually as the estate is wound up. The next table
shows the net results of the operations on that account: —

vaguely described as “ loans on mortgage and other in-

There is history in those three lines
1903 .. Property written up ..£lOOO
1906 .. Property written up .. 2000
1908 .. Property written down.. 1700
While a country is borrowing 4 , and money is flowing in
from outside, a fictitious affluence is produced ; and
prices boom. The immediate effect is just the same as if
the country discovered two or three Mount Morgans, or
had a succession of phenomenal harvests, along with
phenomenal prices. Later on, however, the effect is
different. If the inflow of borrowed money simply
stopped it would be bad enough ; but in addition to that
there is an outflow of interest, and in many cases the
lender wants his principal back, and these facts make things
far worse. The boom prices then start to shrivel; and the
pendulum is apt to swing just as far back as it swung for-
ward. Already this co. has to write off £l7OO “on free-
hold property which has fallen into the co.’s hands dur-
ing the year.” Evidently with a view to setting its house
in order, the co. has been busy reducing its advances and
strengthening its cash reserve, which latter was referred to
12 months ago as “ appallingly small.” this comparisoh
shows the position at Dec. 31: —
1902; 1907. 1993;
Capital and reserves .. .. £131,492 £149,634 £150,406
Liabilities .. .. -- 256,484 321,259 296,267
Mortgages and other securities 364,987 451,583 409,801
Freeholds 2,500 8,478 17,430
Book debts 4,691 5,816 5,420
Liquid assets .; .. .. 15,798 5,015 14,022
The liabilities, therefore, are a shade under £2 for every
£1 of shareholders’ funds ; in other words, the depositors
and other creditors are protected against a slump to
the extent of a shade over 6s. Bd. in the £. And
on top of that protection there is the shareholders’
uncalled capital of £5 per share. (There are only
250 fully-paid £lO shares out of a total of 20,000.)
The principal asset is described as “ mortgages and other
securities,” which isn’t a satisfactory way of lumping
things ; still it may be taken for granted that the “ other
securities” don’t include anything liquid. The propor-
tion of liquid assets to total liabilities is still much too
small, and if there is any demand for the deposits it is
at least possible that further amounts will have to be
written off on account of forced realisations; Nevertheless;
there seems to be no reason why therfe should be ally
demand for deposits; because the protection offered tb
depositors by the shareholders’ funds, paid up and un-
called, is very substantial.

40,000 shares got £2500 for the Use of £40,000 for an
average of 4J months, which is a nice little return of 16 2-3
per cent. Of the very fine profits shown for the half-
year, £3550 has been added to the formal reserves—for
special repairs and insurance. But besides those appro-
priations, probably something has been wiped off revenue
direct, and used to reduce the book value of snipping. Two
new vessels are on the stocks ; a big one is almost com-
pleted, and a second smaller one has been started.
Also others have been improved. Yet the book value of
snips has only moved up in the half-year from £62,051 to
£69,829 —which, by the way, works out at the ridiculous
average of about £2lOO per vessel. This indicates how
severely the asset has been written down ; in other
words, to what an extent profits have been put into
steamers without being shown as profits.

“ In view of the authorised capital being now fully paid
up, your directors propose,” they say, “ at an early date,
to submit a special resolution for increasing the same to
£400,000 ; but the present subscribed capital will not be
increased till actually required’’—which is to say, until
the directors can’t conveniently get rid of the profits
otherwise. And the investing public takes the hint and
pays 635. (ex div.) for the shares; so, in expectation of
favors to come, the investor is willing to pay a price which
yields him an immediate return of only £3 3s. percent.,
assuming that the div. is kept at 10 per cent. The extra
for the past half-year is distributed by way of a bonus on
account of “ specially favorable figures, chiefly due to the
visit of the American Fleet last August.”
It is worth while working out what return an
investor will get by the time the whole of the new shares
have been issued. Suppose they are issued at par—share
for share. The in vestor’s two shares will then have cost
him 83s. If the divs. are kept at 10 per cent., he will get
that much on 40s. =4B. per share=£4 16s. 4d. percent.
Of course, other things may happen. Some of the shares
may, for instance, be issued as bonus shares; of the pre-
sent 200,000 shares, 80,000 were issued that way (60,000
when the present co. was formed, and 20,000 subse-
quently). But assuming they go out at par, the present
price won’t return anything Very dazzling in. the way of
interest, even after what maybe a long wait. What it
may return to the man who buys shares to sell later on in
a favorable market is, of course, another matter.

if that reserve f und isn’t made of profit, what is it made
of? Not premiums on new shares, for no new shares
have been issued. And it isn’t a repairs and renewal
reserve—in other words, a provision for the six months’
wear and tear —because special provision has been made
for that; the “plant renewal account” has been in-
creased during the half-year from £5500 to £9OOO,
which alone is nearly 12J per cent. per annum on the entire
book-value of steamers (except the Burrabra), freehold
and leasehold properties, works, plant, machinery, etc.
To the end of the half-year £23,134 had been paid on
account of the Burrabra ; at least, that much is admitted.
So the Burrabra has apparently cost £23,134, at least;
and all the other steamers stand in the books at £59,156.

The Callow Revolving Band Screen gives
promise of revolutionising the system of ore classification
in Australia, as it has already done in America, for all
classes and conditions of concentrating ores, with vastly
improved extraction in the mills. At Broken Hill it has
been especially successful. It is an unqualified success as a
fine wet screen, and this is what all the mining world has
been seeking; it is also equally effective on the coarse rafts
from Cornish Rolls. With it there is no excessive wear, no
clogging of the mesh ; it has an enormous capacity and a
small water consumption. The fundamental principle of
this machine is a travelling band or belt of screen cloth
stretched on a drum over which the ore and its carrying
water is spread by means of a distributing apron of
special design. The drum passes over rollers, and the
belt is caused to revolve continuously' at a speed adapted
to the nature and quantity' of the material to be screened.
The machine is fully protected by patent, rights. For
further particulars apply to the Austral Otis Engineering
Co., of Hanna Street, South Melbourne. This firm are the
manufacturers and sole agents for Australasia.* [2
