This co. stood out of the “honorable understanding” in
the coal trade when it was first fixed up ; but the co. and
the “understanding” are very good friends now. It
may be on that account that, though the coal trade in
general hasn’t improved, this co.’s sales of coal have in-
creased, and better profits are shown than for some time
past, barring one half-year. Nevertheless, there isn’t the
rush for the shares that there was : 12 months ago they
were put up close to £3O ; recently they got below £26.
Evidently the public found time at last to run its eye over
past records, and discovered how erratic they were.
Nevertheless the average div. for the past 10 years has
been 19f per cent., though if you go back only five
years you will find the average comes down for that
period to 14 per cent. The profits are only shown
net, after providing for depreciation ; and what allow-
ance is made for that isn’t mentioned, though in
connection with such a wasting security as a coal mine
few things are better worth mentioning. The co.
keeps in a very good position to buy other properties as
they offer; its surplus of liquid assets (including book
debts) over liabilities was £62,450 on December 31. The
other assets are lumped—“ Mining property, including
mineral lands, buildings, railway, rolling stock, pits,
tunnel, etc., £102,964.” If this stack were divided up, it
would be found that the co.’s surface or township pro-
perty is worth considerable ; and in that respect this coal
co. differs from most others in the same list.
* * * -Jr
Justice Higgins has gone to Broken Hill to hold
a Federal Arbitration Court over the Broken Hill Prop,
dispute. Without prejudging the case, it is hard to-see
how an Arbitration Court can award worse wages and
conditions than those which have been accepted by two-
thirds of the cos. working the same lode. What will hap-
pen then? Only the management knows for certain;
but the chances are that the ii. H. Prop, will make a very
leisurely start, and will then proceed to work the
mine on a limited scale, picking out the best
men offering and waiting for the metal mar-
kets to improve a lot before it bustles again.
The difference between what a first-class miner and
a second- or third-class miner can do is so great that if
it only finds places for the first-class men, the result will
be exactly the same as if it made a cut of 15 or 20 per
cent, in wages. The men will get the higher wages, but
the standard of efficiency will be raised several points.
In other words, the co. will get around the award (sup-
posing it goes this way) by making it apply to the best
men, and not to the average or under-average men.
* * *
There apparently wasn’t enough luck to divide
between Lobb’s Hole and Kyloe, the two copper shows in
the Monaro (N.S.W.), which cos. have lately taken in
hand ; and Kyloe managed to get the whole stock. As
fast as Kyloe has improved, Lobb’s has slid the other
way. Apart from its excessive capital its trouble is that
it has only a narrow vein in hard country ; and the country
has got harder and the vein narrower in the No. 2 level,
where something decent was expected. Therefore,
though the little smelter was only started on January
15, the campaign will last not later than the middle of
this month ; then the pot will have to shutdown and wait
for ore. Lobb’s was a very profitable copper show for the
small party which worked it for many years ; and that is
the sort of enterprise for which it is best suited.
* * ■*
The Barrier, Wyalong (N.S.W.), the best of a
number of properties controlled by a Sydney syndicate,
promises to yield several crushings during the year. The
shaft is down some 42ft. from the 400 ft. level-on high-
class mineral ore, while a winze 50ft. south of the shaft is
showing a mineral reef all the way running from 8 to 12
inches wide. It is intended to sink the shaft so as to
provide a lift of 100 ft., and then run a level to meet the
winze. The syndicate would do a wise thing when the
shaft has been sunk the required depth to drive north to
the Welcome Stranger boundary, as the shoot of stone
in the Stranger which yielded £19,000 worth of gold
down to the 180 ft. must be somewhere thereabout.
* * * a
“Straight Go”: The Bulletin, I see, still
regards the Queen Bee, Cobar district (N.S.W.), as a good
little mine, troubled by bad equipment. I have been
mining for 30 years, have worked at the Bee, and my
idea is that its real trouble now rests with the value and
quantity of ore. Manager Fox, a large shareholder, took
charge 15 month- ago. He promised big things, dividends
among them. The dividends haven’t come, and I think
the other promises are in the same basket. On the mine
are four reverb, furnaces and one small water jacket,
capable of treating 1120 tons of ore per month. Given
8 per cent, ore, this should produce 90 tons of copper per
month, or 45 tons of copper with 4 per cent. ore. Judging
by the smelting returns published, the plant has been run
at about one-half its capacity. Why? The only reason
a sane person can arrive at is that the ore is not avail-
able. No doubt it has been a rich little mine, but below
No. 3 level the ore decreases in copper value very
rapidly till at No. 4 level it is about 4 per cent. :
average width of lode, 4ft. 6in. At No. 5 level very little
ore is showing, and the winze between No. 4 and No. 5
levels shows a still further shrinkage in ore and values.
* * • ■*
“E.H.” writes: “Re Boppy copper mine
(N.S.W.). One month’s experimental work with differ-
ent kinds of concentrating machinery proved the com-
plete amenability of the ore to concentration with Wil-
fleys or similar tables, 2 per cent, to 4 per cent, ore
concentrating- up to 18 per cent., with o‘s per
cent, to 0’75 per cent, loss in tails. If this isn’t successful
concentration then tell me. The mine shut down be-
cause theco. hadn’t money to put in necessary machinery
for concentration purposes, not because experiments
failed. The ldwt. gold is in concentrates, not in ore. The
mine is still there, as sound a little copper proposition as
will be found in the State, and some one will make money
out of it.” Which last is the conclusion at which The
Bulletin had already arrived. But the shut-dowm of the
mine wasn’t caused exactly in that way. The trouble
was that the bank was dissatisfied regarding a certain
guarantee, as to which it said it had been misled. As to
gold contents, the mines inspector reported for 1907
“ The mine raised and sold 380 tons ore, containing 37
tons copper and 18oz. gold,” and the difference between
ldwt. per ton and 18oz. in 380 tons is too small to matter.
Still it admittedly was picked ore.
The Wellington district (N.S.W.) hasn’t bfeeh
feeling very well lately owing to the practical shut-up of
the old and once very profitable Mitchell’s Creek, and the
metallurgical troubles at the Commonwealth ; but the
Welcome Jack, near Wuuluman Creek, has tried its best
to keep up its end of the old field. The co. started in
January, 1908, with a paid capital of £l2OO ; and since
then £1475 has been paid away for plant, £750 has been
distributed in divs., and besides a reserve of £2OO there was
£202 in the bank when things were balanced the other
day. The co. issues along with its accounts a statement
of assets and liabilities. The assets include 3150 tons
tailings at 16s. (£2520) and 2000 tons ore in sight at 395.
(£3900); but whether that is gross recoverable value or
estimated profit isn’t obvious. If it is gross value then,
of course, there ought to appear on the other side the
cost of turning the assets into coin.
* * * *
Westralian Minister for Mines Gregory held
forth on the greatness of Kalgoorlie in the Perth Parlia-
ment : “ There is no area in the world of the same dimen-
sions that is producing so much gold as the Golden Mile.
To the end of 1908 Kalgoorlie has produced
10,586,3240 z., valued at £44,967,846. . . . . Six of the
Kalgoorlie mines have to date paid in dividends over
£12,500,000 sterling.” Yes, but you can’t turn the mill
with the water that has passed. Some of the six mines
mentioned have now dropped out, and with the exception,
perhaps, of the Kalgurli no dividend-earners have shot
out to take their places. If Gregory was merely out to
boom things with a view to tickling some more money
out of Cohenstein, his yarn is understandable ; otherwise
he must have told it with his tongue in his cheek. When
Brown has lived to the remarkable age of 92, it doesn’t
signify that Brown has a long life ahead of him ; it only
signifies that of the total years of his life no fewer than
92 have gone ; and the more that have gone the fewer
there remain.
* * * *
Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) is now estimated to
have 1,065,409 tons in sight, of a gross value of about
£2,700,000. Setting aside 275. per ton for cost of produc-
tion, and allowing a fair thing for losses in residues,
this estimate gives a “ profit in sight ” of somewhere
about 70s. per share. Previous year’s estimate was
1,062,332 tons, containing £2,765,084 gross.
* » • •
For the year ended September last Central and
West Boulder (W.A.) put up another remarkable per-
formance. The local expenditure—almost wholly salaries
and travelling expenses—was £638; and London got
away with £l4OO, three-fourths of it for directors’ fees
and secretarial services. Having farmed out one lease on
profit-sharing terms to the Oroya-Brown Hill, and let the
others on tribute, the officials have apparently decided to
enjoy a good time while there is money and opportunity.
Yet no one at the annual meeting seems to have raised
the slightest objection to the balance-sheet; and it was
unanimously adopted. It is amazing what J. Bull will
put up with. But when the co. is irretrievably on the
rocks, it will be Australia that those shareholders will
blame ; and in a few more minds this country will have a
black mark put against it.
* * * *
Central and West Boulder had £lOl6 in hand
when the year closed ; but, on the other side, the co.
owed £2045. The most promising feature of the year’s
operations was reckoned to be the discovery of a new
make of ore near the Associated boundary. An examina-
tion of this section indicates that “in all probability
something approximating 4000 tons of good grade ore will
be recovered from these workings ” ; but as the co. has a
bad record as a tipster, the public had better not place
too much reliance on this estimate. Dealing with returns,
the report mentions that since crushing was resumed
about six months ago 7398 tons have been extracted and
treated at the Oroya-Brown Hill mill for a recoverable
average of 18s. 6d. per ton. This left nothing for the
Central Boulder ; but how the Oroya-Brown Hill fared is
not stated. It may be taken for granted, however, that
the latter co. didn’t lose anything, as it is not the sort of
concern to work other cos’, leases for the benefit of its
health. And it is not likely to get into debt over them
either.
* * * *
The strike of woodcutters, and the consequent
shutting down of the mill, prevented the Great Fingall
(W. A.) from earning the shilling dividend just paid within
the quarter, but the Board made up the balance out of
accumulated profits. After providing for this distribu-
tion the co.’s cash resources, including bullion in transit,
amounted to a trifle over £90,000.
* • * *
Tindals (W.A.) still fails to inspire confidence.
When the new mill started and free drinks were handed
round on Nov. 21, good returns and early dividends were
predicted by the chairman ; but why has not the result
of the first clean-up been given out ? Is anybody ashamed
of the figures ? The silence, along with the report that
the property is deep in pawn to a well-known machinery
firm, and the fact that several ugly stories are now in
circulation about the stock, suggest that some inquisitive
shareholder should begin to ask questions.
* * * *
The Gold Finance Co. of London evidently
belongs to the Hope-and-Trust persuasion. Report for
year ended June last shows a loss of £5962, which is
mainly due to the slumping of large share interests in the
Cosmopolitan Proprietary and Bellevue. Both these pro-
perties are said to have “ good prospects,” but that is
what thev sav about every mining derelict.
* •" * * *
Mt. Yagahong (W.A.) inflicted another annual
report on its shareholders last month. Assets are alleged
to be practically unchanged ; but as these mostly consist
of shares in two played-out shows in the West and an
apparently unmarketable interest in a S’African diamond
mine, which doesn’t seem to be yielding diamonds, the
fact that they haven’t changed isn’t in their
favor. The co. still has a bit of money, and the
Board advises shareholders to “consider a scheme for
writing down the capital to an amount which may earn
dividends.” Shareholders had much better go the whole
hog, and have a division of assets right away. The his-
tory of this co. has been one of failure and ineptitude,
and there are no better prospects now visible.
* •* » *
The value of stone from the New Moon, Ben-
digo, got down last half-year to 4dwt. 10gr., making an
average of 4dwt. 20gr. for the year; and yet from the
£39,388 won the co. knocked out £9600 in divs. Most of
the stone comes from the big formation in the shallow
ground, where the direct labor cost is only ss. BJd. per
ton, including timbering. Total working costs for the
last half-year were £16,401; total revenue was £20,341,
leaving a profit of £3940. The co.’s revenue included
£1546 for public crushing, which is one of the mysteries
of Bendigo management, seeing that the co. has big
reserves of its own low-grade ore, which isn’t earning
anything as long as it is left in the mine.
* * * *
Porepunkah Dredging Co., Bright (Vic.), set
out on a capital of £5OOO, intending to make the mine
pay for its own development. The purchase of the land
was arranged on time-payment, and a dredge was got on
terms. Probably it wasn’t economical, and it isn’t a
system that could be worked on anything but a dredging
claim ; but in this case it is panning out all right. For
the land £lOOO a year has to be paid. The total debt has
now been reduced to £3908 ; and, besides land and plant,
the co. had £402 in hand on Dec. 31. In the first half of
last year the net profit was £1006; in the second half,
£1337. The dredge is eating up about 20 acres a year.
* * * *
Williams’ Fancy and Birthday Tunnel, Ber-
ringa (Vic.), fight for the lead in cheap work. The
Tunnel is now ahead with a record of 11s. Id. per ton,
against 11s. lid. by the Fancy. Its work is at 800 ft.,
against 600 ft. in the Fancy ; and 3s. 9d. per ton was spent
on development; against Jd. per ton by its neighbor.
The recoras in both instances are the best in Victoria to-
day. On 4dwt. quartz the Tunnel is paying 25 per cent;
of the gold won as profits, and is taking ldwt. for de-
velopment, other expenses being more than covered
out of the balance. What makes such costs possible are
big formations, with the gold easily recoverable.
■ft * * *
Portuguese Flat, Creswick (Vie.), is one of the
few good things “Ha, ha” Peacock has in his list of
mining cos. The co. is sluicing a Creswick old-time
rush, and on £B4OO spent has put up a plant, paid
for the lease, and dragged 52080 z. out of two acres of
ground. The face is deep, but the gold is all right. The
co. has got on to the div. list, and looks like sticking
there till the last bit of dirt is washed out of the flat.
•» * * •
The importunate South Star, Ballarat, is
wheedling the Victorian State Treasurer again. A bit of
slobber led Thomas Adolphus Bent to lend £6OOO to
enable the co. to go down to 3000 ft. on a lode that for
hundreds of feet had been as barren of result as a score
of Bent’s promises. The Bulletin measured up the
chances of that bit of work, and its estimate came out
right. It now predicts that if Jack Murray sanctions
another grant for driving a crosscut to the Albion lode
he w’ill help the co. to drown itself. Central Plateau,
next door, sees the danger, and is out with a hot protest.
Not alone that, but the Central is setting the Star the
right example by going out west into sound country to
sink a new shaft, with the object of picking up the lode
below' the old gutter. Such work will cost less relatively
when everything is debited up. There will be no water-
spout ahead to fear, and the facilities for opening up the
lode will be what a miner wants.
■*■***
Crown Lyell, having got its lease back, puts out
a report w'hich shows how sadly Tasmania wants decent
labor conditions for mines. No active work was done
during the past half-year. Yet the co. had money in
hand, because directors avow’ that the financial position
is such that mining operations can be begun. Work in
North Lyell, near by, has drained the country, so the last
impediment to sinking has gone. On this draining the
directors build up castles in the air by surmising that the
North Lyell ore body extends into the mine at depth.
Such a deduction is very pretty. By a parity of reason-
ing, the fact that the Tasmania mine at Beaconsfield has
drained the country for miles round should indicate that
the dozens of surrounding blocks contain the Tasmania
lode. But nobody is foolish enough to even suggest that.
Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) lias got its surface workings
and milling plant into better order, and the new manage-
ment can be congratulated on improved output, recovery,
and economy. During the half year 90,872 tons were
treated for 480 tons concentrates, giving 67£ per
cent, metallic tin. Thus it took 189 tons of
dirt to make one of concentrates, and still
there was a profit. Mining and milling costs came
down with a rattle from ss. 9d. to 4s. 3d., and the
smelting cost, calculated on dirt, is only a few' pence.
Total divs. for the half-year (£18,000) are equivalent to
nearly 4s. per ton of dirt. Considering the low average
yield and the weak and falling market, the ss. a month is
not too secure. Recent returns, however, indicate an
improvement, and any extra tin in the dirt will be all
“bunce.” The mine produced an average of 80 tons of
concentrates per month, so a profit of £37 10s. per ton
was required to equal a ss. div. (£3000). Such a big profit
on concentrates is enough to make every tin miner’s
mouth water, and to get such results out of some of the
poorest tin dirt in the Commonwealth is a notable
achievement.
» * * *
Bonnie Dundee, Charters Towers, crushed
523 tons for £2195, which isn’t up to expectations. The
returns from a small mine, worked for all it is w r orth,
are likely to see-saw'. Development in the two deepest
levels has also been in-and-out. Brilliant Deeps put
through 102 tons for an average of £2 Bs. lOd. The
foolish excitement caused by a few rich assays from a
diamond drill hole has now fizzled out, hut the Deeps
keep their solid backing because of the good develop-
ments next door in the Brilliant Extended. Charters
Towers is gradually entering an era of deep-level mining,
and the sinking of several deep shafts, such as the Queen
Cross, Mills United, Freeholds, Brilliant Block, and B. St.
George, also the proposed deepening of the Brilliant Ex-
tended underlie below 2800 ft., denote a wide range of
activity. The Brilliant Deeps are prospecting a reef split
into hanging-wall and footw'all sections at a vertical
depth of 2413 ft. The footwall branch is being driven
south-west on a make of mineral stone about 2ft. wide.
A call of 2d. is due on the 100,000 contributing shares.
New Brilliant Freehold’s fortnightly return was
£2401 from 447 tons, so the average is well maintained.
The December lot was £3846 from 709 tons, and the total
for last year, including tributors’ gold, was £17,514 from
3473 tons. Mills’ United, next door, got out £179,695 for
the year, and the Freehold will have to do a lot of de-
velopment before it can climb up to its big neighbor’s
output. Yet the market, with 20 years’experience of
the Day Dawn, values the Freehold at about £20,000
more. Sinking is not so rapid as was expected, but in a
few weeks the shaft should be deep enough, and No. 7
main level far enough advanced, to start crosscutting.
With so much development in hand the directors are
careful of the money, or probably a dividend would have
come before this. Mining and haulage are being done
under expensive conditions, so there is every inducement
to push on with the main shaft, which fortunately is one
of the best on the field. E. Mexican has better hopes in
consequence of another make of mineral stone 64ft. up
the rise from No. 6 level, and the fact, that some rich
stone was got from No. 6is not forgotten. Although the ■
past exploration of the Mexican has been disappointing,
there is still much unexplored country near the N. B.
Freehold’s boundary which may provide a pleasant sur-
prise.
The Herberton district continues to hold first
place among the producers of minerals other than gold
in Queensland. Mt. Morgan begins to forge ahead as far
as copper is concerned, but the lead so far does not
amount to much; for the last three months of 1908
Mt. Morgan produced 1263 tons of copper, against
Herberton’s 1206 tons. These two gave nearly
three-quarters of the output. Mt. Perry (434
tons), Oloncurry (406), and Rockhampton (239) were
the other important contributors to the total, 3695 tons,
which is 920 ton 3 more than in the last quarter of 1907.
The output of tin has gone back by about 300 tons ; but
silver, lead, and wolfram show big increases by the same
comparison. Three-fifths of the silver is from Herberton
district, one-sixth from Stanthorpe, one-tenth from
Charters Towers, and Mount Perry is responsible for
20,0000 z. of the total of 356,0000 z. for the three months.
This total is nearly 100,000 oz. more than in 1907. In tin
again Herberton has 643 tons out of 879, and
139 tons of the balance comes from Stanthorpe.
The lead output increased by 500 tons, but decreased in
value by £l6OO. Counting in the Thornborough district,
138 tons of wolfram out of a total of 141 tons were turned
out from the Cairns back country. The value of these
minerals for the quarter is given at £388,997, or £52,000
more than the last quarter of the preceding year. The
steady advance in production does not appear to have any
good effect on share-market business ; brokers all over
the State are finding business on the decline, and the
speculative element is badly in need of a pick-me-up.
* • * ■*
As they sink on it the Tommy Burns (N.Q.)
gets no better ; but some sort of an output is being main-
tained from the surface stone, though even there a good
deal of hand-picking is being called for. It seems as
though Dalziel’s were going to repeat itself—a wonderful
surface show, with nothing worth having underneath.
Shares, which were skilfully boomed up to £2O within a
day or two of the floating of the co., can be got now for
£6—and you don’t have to regard the business at that
price as a favor either.

BUCHANAN WHISKY—
“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 2, 1909.
My Dear Artni
The steamer Empire—the same one which
dropped that amazing quantity of opium over-
board just outside Sydney Harbor—brought over
the two sons of Melbourne’s lion-headed Dr.
Bevan. Fortunately, it didn’t drop them over-
board also. Professor Louis Bevan—a pale-pink,
swollen edition of his father—is Professor of
International Law or Marine Agriculture or some-
thing at the Imperial University at Tai-Yuan-Fu,
or some such curried place, and the alphabet runs
after his name like Pharaoh running after an
Israelite. Another son is a churchman, and
teaches the young Chow idea how to shoot at
Medhurst College, Shanghai. They are accom-
panied by a wife and a small son apiece.
One of the fascinating youth of Potts Point
leaves his perch on the higher branches of the
tree of eligibles. He is Alfred Phillips, a young
widower, and he is to marry Constance, one of the
gilded and titled Benjamin tribe of Melbourne.
The event is booked for February 10, at Canally,
East Melbourne.
Captain Clive Dangar arrived from India via
London on the Mooltan. He has, in the artless
phrase of the military man, “ chucked the service
for good,” and proposes henceforth to devote him-
self to hard labor on the ancestral station. He is
of the Abbey Dangar clan, which is a clan of con-
siderable wealth. The Captain has abandoned a
cavalry regiment to take on the arduous job of
watching sheep grow wool; and the only solace he
proposes to allow himself is a motor car or two.
Matrimony is said to be at the back of this
tremendous resolve, and the lady, who is known
to Sydneyites, journeys Australia-wards later on.
There was an exodus of prominent folk on
Saturday. The Darleys, Admiral Moore and
Mrs. “Bob” Blackwood—one of Melbourne’s
smart dames —left in the Morea. By the Pericles
went the Batthyany nobleman, Mrs Innes,
two pretty Ryrie sisters (the family has sheep
stations up Monaro way, and labors under the
shame of having a member in the State Parlia-
ment), and the Walter Allens.
The Darleys put up at Mrs. Orr’s, in Macleay-
street —this is the one hash turret in N.S.W. at
which the haughtiest may stay and not dread loss
of social prestige—for the period between yielding
up Quarnbi to the Sep. Levys and clambering
with glad cries aboard the Morea. If anything
were needed to put the cachet of importance on
“52,” the Darleys’ action and appreciation of the
place would do it. Mrs. Darley is reported to
have said on leaving for the P. and O. wharf that
she “had no idea hash-towers could be made so
comfortable, and so like the houses of gentle-
folk.”
That pretty little danseuse. Miss Evans, who is
to marry her wealthy N.S.W. Adonis Binney next
month, is a wonderful result of how a “ given
name ” sometimes (but not often) fits. Consider-
ing the early age at which births and christenings
occur, few people make a success of the business.
Daisy usually grows into a globular angel of
14 stone, while Rose comes out yellow. But Miss
Fairy Zephyr Evans is as fair as a once-upon-a-
time princess, and her locks are of the Titian
breed, and her dancing is as light as the feet of
her second name when they waltz upon a hare-
bell.
The Macleod sisters are frockily back in Sydney
• —lounging in their city flat after astrenuous copper-
tinting at a surf-beaten beach. Toorak is to miss
their cafe and theatre parties for quite a while.
They depart for the Thames-side city in March,
with an interlude of two boats at Colombo.
Andrew Black’s pals from Paling’s, and other
intimate male friends, collected on Friday night
to tear a bit of food with Andrew at Paris House.
It was a Bohemian spree and limited to a round
dozen of guests. Andrew warbled some moonlit
songs, but took no alcoholic part in the proceed-
ings. The remaining eleven upheld that feature
of the programme. When the hours grew small
they chased the bridegroom off with cheers.
Mrs. Forsyth, who knows, according to horsey
folk, more about horses than any other half-dozen
women in N. S. Wales, has come Sydneywards
from Newcastle, where she abides, with a curious
contraption. It is an enormous caravan—the sort
the stage gipsy who kidnaps the missing heir
affects. That is to say. it is the gipsy sort in
general conformation. In all other respects it
differs from that humble conveyance. The For-
syth vehicle is of a most gorgeous appearance,
being mostly shining brasswork and yellow paint.
Four horses drag it about the country side, and
the lady herself drives them as only she knows how.
Somewhere stowed away in the recesses of the
machine lurk a butler and maid. Also there are all
the modern conveniences, from baths downward.
The owner avers that a caravan is a pleasanter
and healthier way of taking the air and viewing
the scenery than any motor that was ever
fashioned.
Mrs. T. H. Kelly left the Wentworth Falls
establishment, where she was vegetating with her
family in the absence abroad of her hubby, and
visited town last week. Her object was to per-
sonally superintend the slaying of a fatted calf or
two of welcome for consumption by the wanderer
when he returns. He will be back in ten days’
time, and already Glenyarrah is taking on a fresh
and burnished aspect.
One of the local residents who won’t be over-
joyed at the resumption of the status quo at Glen-
yarrah is Julius Knight. He had a loan of two
of the excellent Kelly servants, and now
that the family is to be once more in residence, he
is to lose them. Others like them are difficult to
find. The average cook now offering either
drinks heavily or is unable to cook, and the
normal house-parlormaid of the day is black,
answers to the name of Sambo or Ram Chunder
Ali, is blasphemous when annoyed, and has a
rooted aversion to soap, water, work, and the
truth.
Lord Northland, son and heir of the Ranfurly
Earl who was Governor of Maoriland between
1897 and 1904, has made his initial bow in the
Divorce Court. It is a bore in the way of loss of
time and money disbursed in lawyers’ fees, but if
you are a young man in the Cold Land’s Smart Set,
and mean to preserve a reputation for spirited con-
duct, you have to do it. The future Earl conies
of a family which has not hitherto occupied the
time of the Probate, Divorce and Admiralty
Division much. Knox is the Ranfurly family
name, and the present holders of it are de-
scended from William Penn, who founded Penn-
sylvania, and left an odor of piety and burnt
witches behind him. The case promises to be of
more than usual interest. On the one hand,
Mr. John Alex. Stirling, of Perthshire, late lieu-
tenant in the 3rd Battalion Scots Guards, accuses
his wife, Clara Elizabeth Tiivlor, or Stirling, for-
merly an actress in the musical comedy company,
“The Earl and the Girl,” of frivolling with Lord
Northland, and on the other, Clara Elizabeth
Stirling alleges that John Alex, has car-
ried on with the famous Mrs. Ather-
ton. Mrs. Atherton was divorced from her
hubby last year, the Hon. John Yarde-Buller,
son and heir of Lord Churston, playing the
rdle of juvenile-lead on that occasion. When the
decree was made absolute the Hon. John refused
to toe the matrimonial mark with the lady, and,
instead, wedded Miss Denise Orme, of musical
comedy fame. Mrs. Atherton sued him for breach
and got judgment and costs. The jilted dame
was in S’Africa with her spouse (he com-
manded the 9th Lancers) during the Boer-British
disturbance. Those who met her describe her
charm as amazing. Ninety per cent, of the com-
missioned officers of the 10th Hussars (your 10th
Hussar is an even haughtier person than a
Guardsman) adored her blitheringly, and so did a
heap of other important males, from dukes down-
wards.
Mr. “ Tommy ” Burns is going to be responsible
for the arrival of three small immigrants to this
country within the next few weeks. His widowed
sister died in Canada the other day, leaving the
infants as almost her sole assets, and the Brussos,
who have no youngsters of their own, are going to
adopt the motherless ones and bring them up.
In the evolution of dress it is not the Frock
Fact that adapts itself to the environment; it
is the etceteras, the outside members, that
are forced to rigidly suit themselves to the
dress. Now happens the sudden change as if
done by a contortionist mirror. Everything is
contracting with horrible insistence. Alas for
the buxom blooms ! A note of interrogation, or a
Mrs. Eichorn, is the key-note or the pin of
fashion. The huge hats have diminished
to brimless busbies, elongated with an
upstanding brush. Everything horizontal is
lopped off, like a severely-pruned tree, and added
to the perpendicularity like a juggler’s cane. The
safest thing is to discard the present body and
build up a substitute of reinforced concrete. Soon
a law will be necessary to limit the height of any
human.
Even flowers and bouquets are rigidly nar-
rowed. The bride may not hide her blushes be-
hind half a ton of scrambled vegetable and tulle.
Fluffiness is doomed. Rigidity and narrowness—
narrow as the right road—are the insanely en-
forced law. Ascension lilies, or gladiolas, or
Moses bulrushes tied with an ultra severe bow
across their middle are just about the “cutest
thing out,” in present fashion’s language.
Irene Marks, that fair Amazonian slayer of
hearts, has opened a hat shop in Bond-street,
London. It is doubtful if the petted society
butterfly knows how to concoct one solitary lid of
feather and fur or flower, but it is hoped that
London sassiety will fall at her beautiful feet,
take whatever her assistants hand over, and say in a
faint voice : “Search me, and take all the wealth
I have about me, but do not—do not ask me to
give up the hat ! ” Society often does well when
it dabbles in trade—though London usually de-
mands a countess, at least, with her coronet over
the door. A name is the necessity ; the assistants
do the rest. Just as it is quite possible to run a
laundry without “washing yourself.”
Wellington, in Joseph Ward’s fair country, has
been tumbling over its cliffs in its furious wel-
come of Ada Crossley, and Ada has wreathed
herself in smiles and her most adorable sheath
frocks, and sung divinely in response. She is a
member of the Girls’ Realm Guild in Maoriland,
and the Guild raised a cordial reception at the
Hotel Windsor, which is one of the gorgeous
palaces of the unsteady city. That same
windy metropolis has more bloated and elaborate
hotels than all the rest of Maoriland put
together. About everybody of importance in the
Island turned up to the reception (the wind was
not invited and stayed away in a huff, to the extra
glory of the frocking). The absence of the
Mayoress, Mrs. Hyslop, left a gap on the plat-
form that it took two of her daughters to fill.
Joseph Ward came in his ultra-shining plug-hat,
and brought Mrs. Ward. The Most Excellent
Plunkets were away, but they receptioned and
dined the songstress later. Mrs. Seddon sat at
Miss Crossley’s right hand at the tea ceremony,
and the Walter Nathans, of racehorse wealth,
came from Auckland for the spree. Mr.
and Mrs. Ted Tourist Donne were im-
portant stars, and a niece, Miss Lorna Donne
(I nearly wrote Doone), presented rare
exotic vegetables to her godmamma Crossley.
Tittell Brune drooped languidly in the few inches
of space allowed for poseurs. The whole affair
was engineered by Miss Dimant, the secretary of
the Guild. When Miss Crossley was presented
with the official badge, a rosette of mauve and
white lilac, she found a tiki dangling from it.
Mr. Tourist Donne had the tiki, which
was dug up from some aged and decayed chief’s
grave.
Violet Vanbrugh is coming out with husband
Bourchier early next year, and her coming frocks
cast shadows ahead. The Vanbrugh, quite apart
from her mummer tricks, is a wondrous, snaky,
supple poseuse, with a sense for color and silky
clinging effects that will drive female theatre-
goers pale mauve with envy. Then they’ll roll up
night after night arid make surreptitious notes on
the backs of their powder puffs about giving the
3s. 6d.-a-day sewing girl “ hints” to copy. The
majority will plunge too luscious curves or angu-
lar bones into the “creations” done by the maid
at 3s. 6d., and fondly believe themselves on the
fringe of a cult that is evolved out of her color
consciousness by One Woman for Herself. Any-
how Violet will be a zest—an elusively dainty
morsel for playgoers.
Melba is so much within cooee that she left
Naples last week for Australia. The Orontes
brings her to Melbourne by March 3. Melba has
written at great length to Mr. Lemmone about
her tour. She wishes to make an exhaustive
backblocks journey, and visit the small, out-of-
the-way places the people whereof rarely go to
the capitals, and never hear a full-fledged
songstress except in the tree-chorus. She is
following the lead of Jenny Lind, who in her
Swedish tour visited far-back ice pinnacles and
sang to the penguins. Arrangements have already
been made to carry Melba on her road by waggon
or bicycle. Quirindi, by the way, has heard of the
disturbance, and has written to the management
imploring that it be included in the tour.
starts off with a hustled first guarantee of quite 60
guineas either in coin or in pigs and produce.
Miss Florence Gleeson’s sister, who was very
seriously ill, has fortunately taken a turn on the
right side of the crisis, and is recovering. Miss
Florence had to leave her part at the Royal to her
understudy, and hasten to her afflicted relative in
Melbourne. Greatly to her distress, a report
gained ground that her sister had died, which
report is hereby contradicted as useless, inaccurate,
and superfluous.
The redoubtable and musical Sykes is back in
Sydney. He has been sojourning in the Island of
Apples that sleeps at the foot of this continent.
Saturday saw a whole tableful of the folk who
foregather at Paling’s fortifying themselves at a
Cafe Francaise lunch before going along to say
“ Hurroo ” outside the church and watch the
wedding of Tenor Andrew Black with the charm-
ing widow of Pfahlert’s. The wedding was as quiet
as possible, only very4ntimatefriends being warned
of the hour. The widow wore a grey garb for the
sacrifice; then shed that dream of a gown for
smart blue travelling gear of the tailored descnp-
tion.
News comes of Ola of the divinely-devilish
locks. The Humphrey-Titheradge-Kolker com-
bination has doubled on its tracks, and gone to
Auckland to play “The Silver King” ; and Ola s
tresses are now waving in the breeze that blows
up the Hauraki Gulf.
Carlyle Smythe and his fascinating spouse send
sudden cards—P.P. C. “ Just off to spend a week-
end or so in the Vaterland, with a halt at the
Great Smoke,” write the sprightly Smythess.
The death of Mr. Rossbach —one of the two Local
Government engineers—was a most regrettable
occurrence. He dropped dead of heart disease on
Wednesday night. It is not an obituary plati-
tude to say that he was the best-liked man in the
Public Works Department. Only a few days
before Mr. Rossbach was elected as a member of
the Public Service Council. He not only got more
votes than any other candidate, but he got every
vote going. He was a man of about 48 years of
age, and was within touch of the highest offices
in his department.
The London correspondent of one of the Syd-
ney dailies said this last week :
You do not need a coat, unless the weather is Arctic,
when you have furs cut as I saw to-day on a handsome
ex-Australian woman.
The ex-Australian strikes me as a ritew develop-
ment. She is probably unique in her way. One
does not hear of ex-American women. On the
contrary, even Lady Essex and the Duchess
of Roxburgh, of the Cold Land—women who
have married British husbands and registered
vows, in a manner of speaking, to abide in
their spouses’ native country—don’t dream
of abandoning their nationality. They re-
main Americans, and are proud to do so.
London is dotted with American societies that,,
have been organised by U.S. A. natives resident
in the Great Fog; and the organisers would be
very angry indeed if you referred to them as ex-
Americans. Their money comes from Hamland,
and they consider, and rightly so, that the country
which finds them in diamonds and meals at Clar-
idge’s is good enough to own up to. French tourists
don’t become ex-Frenchmen and women because
they have brought a wad of money across the
Channel and are employed spending sovereigns
instead of louis, and shillings instead of francs.
Australia appears to be the only Country on earth
whose absentee citizens disown it as soon as they
get abroad. The ex-Australian woman, with
furs paid for with Australian money, and an un-
dying dread lest her friends shall know the source
of her income, is one of the unpleasantest of social
phenomena.
Some persons seem to have a genius for partly
humanising animals. The boys and girls of a
certain family of my acquaintance used to tell me
of the unusual behavior of their dogs, cats and
birds, but as I had frequently boasted that my
collie had learned to read I didn’t take much stock
of other persons’ brag. I thought some of it was
brag. But lately I was ambling about my friend’s
garden, and saw things that will make me hold
my tongue about my furred and coated four-
footed dependents. First, a huge black and white
cat came around from the kitchen, licking his
whiskers, and lay down for a doze on the lawn.
A wild bird—so little bird and such a lot of
tail—that had been pecking amongst the rose
bushes, immediately flew to the cat and stood
on its luxuriously outstretched tummy. The
cat lifted its head a fraction to see what was
pressing on its lunch, then, evidently recognising
the wagtail as an old friend, went to sleep again.
The bird will wait about for hours for the cat to
come out and be stood upen.
Same family has a big, well-nourished blaek-
and-tan dog. After going on the jag somewhere
in Woolloomooloo, Rover came home with a wiry-
haired, insanitary-looking, very starved mongrel
about ten inches high. This stray found good
food and drink in Rover’s quarters, and promptly
adopted the big dog and his owners. His devotion
to his rescuer is uncanny. He saves him the trouble
of washing his face and paws after a meal, for
he rushes in and does the service for him. Should
Rover start scratching, the small dog jumps for-
ward and takes up the struggle at the right spot.
Rover no longer barks at the “bottle-oh” men, for
the mongrel saves him that trouble. The maternal
house cat now evades the washing and combing
of her two young kittens. The mongrel does it
and keeps them amused so they shan’t worry Ma
Meow. Some humble, grateful, helpful and
homeless human soul has blundered into that
dog.
At Shepperton-on-Thames (England), there
exists what describes itself as “A School of
Colonial Instruction.” Here a British army cap-
tain named Morgan teaches a band of embryo
pioneers how to calm the billy when it boils, how
to identify a land agent in a Johnsonian railway
carriage, where to search for goburra spoor when
hungry, and a score of other matters invaluable to
one who has decided to make track-blazing his
life’s work. A while back the ca,ptain resolved
to marry a widow-woman of his acquaintance.
The London Daily Mail (11/12/’08) thus de-
scribes the typically “colonial” marriage cere-
mony that occurred :
Sixteen red-shirted cowboys upon sturdy ponies gal-
loped in front of the motor-car which held the bride,
waving- wide-brimmed hats, firing revolvers into the air,
and giving full lung-power to far-reaching “whoops.”
The noisy, picturesque cavalcade consisted of the cap-
tain’s jackeroo pupils. Bowie knives protruded from
their belts, their shirt-sleeves were rolled up to the
elbows, their cowboy trousers, boots and other equipment
were complete to the smallest detail.
How it must have brought the smell of the gum-
trees, and the long, low wail of the State Premier,
back to such Australians as were in the neighbor-
hood of Shepperton-on-Thames that day!
A paper “Save the Birds,” read by A. H.
Chisholm, of Maryborough, before a local society,
and published in two parts by the Maryborough
ani) Dunoluy Advertiser (Vic)., is a powerful
and pitiful appeal to women to forego the wearing
of what the writer calls “murderous millinery.”
With facts and figures he tries to awaken the con-
science of plumage-wearing women to a sense of
their crime. For cruelty is criminal, and the
woman who wears plumage that she knows has
been torn from the living bird ought to be treated
as a criminal. I would suggest three years’hard,
and when she comes out the compulsory wearing
for the term of her natural life of a poke bonnet,
trimmed with drab ribbon. Says Mr. Chis-
holm :
Some idea of the extent of the plume-plunderer’s
ravages may be gained from the fact that at the plume
auctions, held in London during the last six months of
1907, the catalogue included no fewer than 19,742 skins of
birds of paradise, while 1386 crowned pigeons’ heads were
offered at one sale alone, and 30,000 kingfishers, and 200
lyre birds’tails at another. There were also catalogued
1411 packages of the nesting plumes of the white egret,
representing the feathers of nearly 115,000 birds. This
beautiful bird—the white egret, or heron—is one of the
chief victims of murderous millinery, and the persecu-
tions of the plume-hunters have reduced its numbers so
greatly that at the present time it is within measurable
distance of becoming extinct. The breeding haunts of
the egrets are invaded every season by bands of ruthless
and ignorant men, whose methods of collecting the
coveted plumes, which are only donned by the birds in
nesting time, are so brutal that the mere recital of them
should effectively put a stop to the fashion of wearing
“aigrettes.”
In Australia the egrets nest largely amid the
great swamp lands of the upper Murray, and the
scene after a visitation by the jackals of fashion
unnerved the naturalist who looked upon it:
The dark waters of the swamp were strewn with blood-
stained bundles, of white feathers, floating silently, in
death. Above, in the big-stick nests, were perishing
families of young egrets, clinging feebly to their oomes
after many days without food or warmth. As the
observer passed along in his boat, those unfortunate
starvelings craned their skeleton heads over the nests
and uttered faint cries for the succor which would never
come to save them.
Nothing but legislation will save the fair birds
from the “fair sex.” the “gentler sex,” the
“ ministering angels.” And when it does come,
let us hope the police will be only half as keen to
spot evidences of bird murder for millinery as
they are to nose out Sunday drinking.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

The MOST DESIRABLE TYRE
Reliability, durability and efficiency may be considered
as going hand in hand as the first considerations in the
manufacture cf the most desirable Motor Tyres—and
in no tyres will this trinity of features be found more
pronounced than in the ever-popular Continental variety.
The Continental Co.’s interpretation of the word
“reliability” is “ability to get there,” and not only
to get there, but to get there well; to be certain at the
commencement of a journey that the end is in sight.
CONTINENTAL TYRES
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 1, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik, —
Governor Carmichael has been “ doing ” the fat
and ought-to-be-flourishing Western district. He
managed to swag it (by motor) in the comfort of a
disreputable-looking soft felt hat. Aide Victor
Hood went along just to indicate that there was a
Governor under the old brown hat. They have
been sharing the friendly damper with the Moles-
worth Greenes, the “Purrumbete” Manifolds, the
Everard Brownes, and lots more of the Western s
naicest people. The ten-days’ trip finished on
Saturday, when the Governor made a bee-line for
Macedon to spend a quiet and peaceful Sawborth
before returning to Melbourne to prize open the
A.N.A. Exhibition on Monday.
The Exhibition building is once more in a state
of upheaval, and the sound of the hammer pounds
the air. The A.N.A. Exhibition is to be opened
on Monday by the Carmichaels. Not a square
inch of floor is left to dump another exhibit on.
There is to be a complete dairy, also a model
poultry house with live chooky-hens and real eggs;
and a huge glass freezing chamber will show
how dead bunnies are packed for export. House-
wives have swamped the place with home-made
jams and preserves, and that two-legged animal
without feathers called man —the thing that the
fish described as an erect, dry object with prongs,
which goes horribly on land —has fashioned all
manner of iron things, from a torpedo down to a
thimble. The Women’s Choir, whose voice is a
power in the land, is busy oiling its top note for
use during the four weeks of the show, and if its
top-note is crowded out it will be an angry combi-
nation.
The old Earl of Leicester, who died last week,
was rather unduly honored by an obituary cable-
gram and quite a long notice in the Melbourne
morning papers. He was a matter of no Aus-
tralian interest, except in the fact that, some two
years ago, a Mr. Coke, of Melbourne, tried to
prove that he bad a prior claim to the title and
estates. The attempt was a dismal failure, like
the Druee claim to a dukedom, and everybody
had forgotten all about it until the earl’s decease
waS announced. He seems to have been the most
unremarkable octogenarian in the whole peerage,
so hopelessly dull and respectable that he reigned
as Keeper "of the Privy Seal to the Prince of
Wales for 30 years. But he married the daughter
of a brewer in his young days—and was doubtless
a charmingly wealthy person.
A real live peer, Earl Stanhope, has arrived in
Brisbane by the Makura, and at present ought to
be in Sydney. His obj ect of travel is ‘ ‘ to study the
problems of the Empire abroad.” As he is under
30 and unmarried, he will probably find the inter-
esting problem of the Eternal Feminine thrust
under his notice for consideration by the way.
The Summer School has finished its picnic at
Nepean Peninsula. Teachers and the much-
dreaded inspector have shaken the friendly paw
of good-fellowship and parted good friends. The
camp had a virulent outbreak of presentations,
and thrust the silver-backed brush and the auto-
graph album upon one another at the slightest
provocation.
Miss Mitchell, who is the drawing teacher at the
Technical Art School at Warrnambool, conducted
a class of admiring art students—mostly men —
each day for the four weeks. They worshipped
under the shade of the palm to the music of
the ant and the cricket, and rose up on the last
day and enthusiastically made a presentation to
the favorite.
There was an exodus of clergy last w'eek.
Archdeacon Hindley departed to the Bleak Land
of England with his wife and daughter and a
heavy purse of golden sovereigns, which the Very
Best People of the Cathedral laity had gathered for
him. Three hundred coins caused the heaviness.
Up in Collins-street the Baptists wept their fare-
well over little Pearce Carey, sometimes known as
Fierce Canary—and loaded him up with pursy
luggage. The golden-tongued little man was a
great Browning scholar, and his lectures on that
rather obscure and tangled poet will be missed.
Still Browning will keep. What Melbourne really
wants is a Josh Billings Society.
Re the recent arrival from China of two sons—
Louis and Willett—of our white-haired and
cheruby Dr. Bevan. The pastoral parent has a
pleasant summer residence at Upper Beacons-
field, some 25 miles from Melbourne, and there the
visitors will abide for a few weeks. Eaclj
of them brought a wife and offspring with him,
the spouse in the young parson’s case being Miss
Vale (as was), and the progeny a miraculous baby.
Somebody was remarking that one or other of the
Bevans from China must be the tall brother who
played “ heavy lead ” in the Trinity College per-
formance of “ Alcestis” years ago, at Melbourne
Town Hall. But somebody erred in that assump-
tion. The hero of the Greek tragedy (his pro-
nunciation of the dead language was declared to
be “ beautiful ” by all who couldn’t understand it)
was D. J. D. Bevan. His wife, on that occasion,
was Miss Florence Towl. She died early in the
drama. After which Hercules (Mr. Bullivant)
came and fought with Death, behind the scenes,
and restored the late lamented to her husband’s
care.
The case of a Prahran milkman convicted of
filling the domestic jugs with skim milk, which
(as he said in Court) had been really intended for
a “ sick horse ” and not for human consumption,
reminds me of how High Court Justice Isaacs
iately dismissed a member of the milk-oh brother-
hood with a severe caution. Mrs. Isaacs, the
Judge’s mother, lay sick at the house of son John,
the ex-M.L. A. Fresh, pure milk was essential to
her recavery, and because the man who had been
supplying the household was rather late in his
morning round, another and an earlier milkman
was engaged. The newcomer gave complete satis-
faction for a day or two, but on the third
day the milk toaS found to he curdling
just as it was poured into a glass
for Mrs. Isaacs. Had she taken Only a
little of it the result would probably have
been fatal. When the new milkman, came round
in the afternoon the jug containing the curdled
stuff was shown to him. He looked at it, sniffed
at it, and their, he put the pertinent question,
“Has there been another milkman supplying the
house ? ” Being asked the inner meaning of his
query, he explained that the rival merchant had
been guilty of a common trick of the trade. From
a small bottle which some of the brethren carry
in a vest pocket he had poured one drop
of vinegar into the vessel that stood on
the doorstep, with a view to blasting
the character of the other fellow’s milk.
The evidence was submitted to Justice Isaacs,
who called every morning at his brother’s house
to see his mother, and in due course the offending
milkman was brought before him. The man
thought he had been summoned to the house to
receive a renewal of the old order for milk, but
when the learned Judge called him from off the
mat into the dining-room, and glared at him with
a terrible judicial glare that seemed to penetrate
to his very soul, the culprit lost all presence of
mind. “You put vinegar into our milk jug, you
miscreant! ” thundered the august personage.
The trembling accused could think of nothing but
“Ye-e-s,”in reply. “You confess your guilt ? ”
roared the High Court, and the man answered
“Ye-e-es.” Then the Majesty of the Law ex-
plained that the prisoner at the dining-table
might have been convicted of manslaughter had
old Mrs. Isaacs taken the milk and vinegar, but
in consideration of his apparent penitence and
his undoubted funk he would be allowed to go free
for the present.
Hugo Wertheim, who suspends externally at
Queenscliff these times, signalised the arrival of the
German training ship Herzogin Sophie Charlotte
with much hospitality. He treated a number of
the cadets to a mild junketing at the Ozone Hotel.
The young men in tall collars and neat, brass-
buttoned uniforms twirled their rather commo-
dious feet in the dreamy waltz, and made them-
selves pleasant. Once or twice they upraised
their combined voices in song of a rather dreary
character. Mrs. Hugo Wertheim, in a neat frock
that was spotted with black and white like a
checkered career, and sporting some massive
battlements of pink roses in her corsage, hostessed
the multitude of cadets and (mostly) short-frocked
girls. As for Hugo, genial soul, he made himself
useful generally with a diabolical black German
cigar partially submerged in his countenance.
If sympathy and consolation were synonymous,
Jack Dane and his girl-wife would have a lighter
sorrow to bear. Everyone will regret to hear of
the birth and death of a child to them —a baby
that just saw the big world for an hour or so, and
then went home. The young matron is a magnifi-
cent specimen of Australian womanhood, meant
to be the mother of strong men, and this bereave-
ment will be the more trying as she is so far away
from people and friends, and this was her first
experience of motherhood.

Artist Dora Sommers follows artist brother
Jack’s example, and is taking a life partner.
About Easter she is to make the “ I will ” remark
to a young Hercules of the Commonwealth Home
Department, who distinguished him-
self some two years ago by diving into a hidden
river snag. He was carried from the holiday
camp on that occasion almost scalpless, but, thanks
to surgery and a good constitution, Dora’s adorer
lives to jest at his scar. He has lost his scalp
only in the figurative sense that it now dangles
from Dora’s girdle.
At last the artistic temperament is to have a
holiday resort all to itself. Painter Hal Waugh
has conceived the idea of converting his charming
Eltham home into a “paying guest” establish-
ment for artists and art students only, so no
longer need the recruiting genius have his peace
disturbed by the offending oleograph and the irre-
pressible musician. It is a home designed by
an artist for an artist, wherein the budding Corot
may dream his dreams exquisitely. Leslie
Wilkie, Norman Carter, and the cherubic Hal
himself also intend holding classes there for the
benefit of the more strenuous trippers. From
which it appears that to be intensely soulful is
not necessarily to lack business instinct. But as
regards those paying guests—are artists and art
students the sort of guests who pay, in the common
or Gothic sense of the term ?
Mrs. 11. W. Bryant, who with her brilliant bar-
rister husband set off for a pleasure trip around
Maoriland, struck bad luck at the first port. She
had to leave the boat at Dunedin to spend part of
her holiday in a private hospital. Her illness is
painful, though not serious, and it is hoped the
pair will be able to take the second course of the
journey in a fortnight or so.
News of Katherine Goodson tells of a successful
time at Honolulu, on her way to Vancouver.
There had to be a wild rush back to the boat by
motor car ; the party, in its best evening clothes,
trailed much unnecessary vegetable, and scrambled
breathlessly on board as the gangway was being
hauled up. Following the custom of the Island,
the pianist’s admirers had hung wreaths of laurel
and carnations round her neck, “ as a small token
of their esteem,” as they say in polite society.
Melbourne billiard players are using up all the
energy left them by the heat, enthusing over
young Gray, the Queensland boy player, who is
“cannoning off the red” for all he is worth. The
weather and he each made a record “break” on
Thursday afternoon. It was “ ladies’ ” day, and
a proud mother sat in pride whilst young hope-
ful did his record-smashage. Boy Gray takes his
“cue ” from his father, who is a bully player.
A war of worry and words has swirled in the
daily papers regarding tips for the driver of the
taxi. An insistent shout from managerial quarters
has split through the flurry, and the burden there-
of is, “There ain’t goin’ to be no tips.” Never-
theless, the imported art of tipping spreads like
the imported bunny, which entered as a small
luxury, and behold! has inherited the earth.
And the tips are high compared with those of
Londen and Paris, for what Australian menial
ivould accept tuppence ! Jtist consider the reflec-
tion Of tuppence on the face of an Australian free-
born porter or waiter !

A returned tripper whose name one seems to
remember in connection with, legal proceedings
dragged herself into Her Majesty’s Theatre on
Saturday night in the most approved and un-
gainly latest London manner. To be “correct,
it seems, you must hold your body well forward,
your shoulders must come up under your ears till
your low-dressed hair almost touches your high-
drawn waist, and your face must wear a loose ex-
pression of utter inanity and boredom. Ihe
returned gadder was a glad dream of shimmer
and sheen in a frock that looked like one large,
smudged rose, whilst her evening coat was of a
more brilliant yellow than the ripest orange that
grows.
The somewhat beauteous model of many adver-
tisements is once again out on the matrimonial
warpath. After a brief, bright period of widow-

Miss Helena Rubinstein, who directs the re-
juvenation of those who can afford to pay for it,
flits.through Australia for a brief month before
setting out to open a beautifying-bureau in India.
She is in Melbourne for one week only, like a
“star” stage-lady. Besides some wonderful
frocks, Miss Rubinstein has brought along with
her a new husband, so that her “ really-truly ”
name is now Mrs. Titus. Her husband, I regret
to say, has not yet replied to that letter which
Paul sent to him in the New Testament. Like
myself, he is a dilatory correspondent. Mrs.
Titus is a little brunette, and a charming denial of
the statement that women are lacking in the busi-
ness instinct. Between her dainty ruffled collar
and her smart satin hat lies a world of acumen
and originality. Melbourne, which was once the
headquarters of her business, is now a mere play-
thing and a side show. In London, the titled
dowager is an eager votary at her elaborately-
equipped altars, whilst a 500-guinea fee for send-
ing a wrinkle-remover across the Atlantic to wave
the magic hand over the features of a. New York
millionairess is a not impossible incident. Mrs.
Titus chats prettily of gadding Australians, and
mentioned that she saw our only Nell the day
before she left London—just the same Sweet Nell,
j very busy engaging her company for Australia,
managing everything herself (George Musgrove is
:convalescing in Sydney), very anxious to succeed
in her forthcoming tour, and very lonely without
her little girl, who is at boarding-school in Swit-
zerland.
A recent par. in “Melbourne Chatter” may

Rembrandt Studio, in the Block Arcade, passed

therefrom. They transferred the business to Mr.

Studio is not less open to consideration than of
yore.
On a recent P. & O. liner a returning Mel-

asked had she seen many beautiful women in
London. “Do you know,” remarked the cameo
beauty, “since I left Australia I have only seen
one woman whom I should be afraid outvie
me, and she was in a frame at the Louvre.”

The Glenelg amateur mermen had a carnival the otner
Saturday, and did their best to get chilled to the
bone. (I might mention incidentally that in Adelaide it
has mostly been a cold winter this summer.) Four tiers
of seats had been arranged along the north side of the
baths for the breakable sex, and they were all packed
tight with thick furs and winter-coats. Even then there
was some shivering and shaking and clattering of teeth.
For mermaids there was but one event, and only three
damsels ventured into the wet. The prize was won by a
small Farrelly child, who swam like a mer-damsel, and
had long red-gold hair to help sustain the character. As
a gentle relief from the serious racing business there was
a clownish person who emerged out of the atmosphere,
tore precariously round the roof, and fell into the sea.
Also there was a Burns-Johnson fight of hilarious
character, with sundry pugnacious-looking policemen to
be treated unkindly for stopping the fight. It was a
frilly assemblage that shivered on the tiers, including a
batch of Speaker Coles’girls, some of the Mayoral Varleys,*
a Talbot Smith or so, a Pile or two, and some Scammells
and others. . , „ , , , ■
The University of Geneva, which can’t help having its
three-hundred-and-somethingth anniversary in July,
has invited our ’Varsity to send someone along to show
them what we can produce. The Council has chosen Mr.
J. G. R. Murray, who is a K.C. nowadays, but was one of
the first graduates turned out from this seat of learning.
In fact, it was only an infant’s chair when J. G. R. Murray
won his South Australian scholarship.
The officers and boys of the German training ship were
at Glenelg on the holiday, and the H. W. Varleys enter-
tained them, and showed them the gum-tree under which
we were declared a province, and the train that runs up
and down the main street, and all the other local curiosi-
ties. The visitors brought their band along with them,
and it played all over the place and provided a very nice
noise though German. In the eve the host and hostess
took the boys in bulk to the Pierrots.
In a few months artist Will Ashton is going off again on
another painting jaunt. He intends to descend upon
Italy, and (earthquakes permitting) to paint there until
further notice. In March he is to have an exhibition in
this hamlet, which remark is intended as a warning to
the public that if it doesn’t save up a sovereign or two by
that time, it’ll be sorry ever after.
Lobethal, the little German township which makes
good tweed and blankets, and claims our Clara Klein-
schmidt for its special own, got itself into a state of
excitement on the 26th, when the talented maid had a
farewell concert there. The local hall nearly burst its
walls, it was so fiercely packed. After the programme,
there were speeches and rhetoric galore, and, finally, her
townsfellows gave the maiden a dressing-case and a rug,
manufactured in the local mills, which last, by the way,
are just opposite Miss Kleinschmidt’s home —another
illustration of the “debasing influence" of manufactures
upon Art.
Motorists have been getting their names into the papers
no end of times lately. The other day Bertie -Barr Smith
and H. M. Aunger (he who moted across Australia with
Harry Dutton erstwhile) shot off to try to leap from here
to Melbourne in record time. They took a 60 steed-
power Napier to do it with. Same day one of the Reed-
beds Whites stowed his 35-h.p. Talbot into the Melbourne
train with the purpose of tearing back to Adelaide before
the other party had got into Collins-street.

An Australian Favorites Splendid Triumph
HOW SHE CONQUERED THE WORLD’S METROPOLIS.
She Tells the Interesting Story in an Interview.
By DOROTHY C.........
I have heard from an old friend in London of
the marvellous success which has fallen to the
share of a woman whose name is known all over
Australasia. My friend has written me: “How
everything about the lady interests, attracts,
charms and rouses the most piquant curiosity.
How she has stirred, fascinated and compelled the
grandest of London’s grand dames into interest.
How one looked, marvelled and heard, and hear-
ing, marvelled Still more.”
Right into the heart and core of smart London,
right into the midst of Worrtan’s Mecca, this
enterprising woman has opened an establishment
of the most refined, cultured, and at the same
time of the most remarkable order ever known in
any capital of modern Europe.
A student of history, of literature and philo-
sophy, this woman has learnt in the annals of the
world, and in the indelible character of human
nature, the impression left by the power and scope
of woman’s beauty. Later study led to a know-
ledge of chemistry, and to pass on from that into
the realms of anatomy, and laboratory and hospi-
tal work, under the greatest authorities in Vienna,
Berlin, Paris, Russia, and elsewhere, was only a
logical step. This knowledge, experience, and
extensive travels, and astonishing capacity for un-
remitting work, combined with wonderful business
acumen, have resulted in the founding and de-
veloping of an enterprise which now covers the
whole world. Therefore, having heard of Mile.
Rubinstein’s arrival in Australia, I have made it
my business to call on her at the Grand Hotel,
Melbourne, to hear from her own lips the story of
her rare success, and how she has achieved it.
Giving it to my readers, I can do no better than
quote her own words, which, in their straightfor-
ward simplicity; are more eloquent than any
labored literary polish can possibly make them.
“ There is nothing more distasteful to me,
began Mile. Rubinstein, “ than speaking of my-
self. I would much rather tell you how glad lam
to re-visit Australia, the place of my first real
successes. I would much rather ”
I was obliged to interrupt what I considered to
be evasiveness, and to impress on Mile. Rubin-
stein that my call was professional, not social, nor
prompted by friendship only.
“Well, since you insist,” Mile, resumed, “ I
will tell you that I have always scrupulously
followed the adage—‘Be sure you are right, and
then go ahead.’ When, some years ago, I se-
cured the sole control of the Valaze Complexion
Treatments, which at the present time are
known to women of refinement the world over,
I knew I could offer specialties which, in their
own particular sphere, had no equal, certainly
no superior. I knew that they were not the
product of obscure origin, but the fruit of the
best thought, the best experience of scientists,
whose names are sacred to enlightened medical
practitioners. Having in my own heart of hearts
satisfied myself of this much, I made my start.
My first beginning, away from home, by which I
mean beautiful Vienna on the Danube, was in
Melbourne. Why in Australia? you may ask.
Because I wanted to put the marvellous proper-
ties of my specialties to the most difficult tests
imaginable—a tropical climate. The complexion
corrective or preserver or beautifier that will
prove its worth in a climate which is as ruinous to
the complexions as is the climate of some parts of
Australasia must be one of great worth indeed.
From Melbourne the little jars of Valaze have
gradually begun to go out to every nook and cor-
ner of this isolated continent, then to New
Zealand, then to India, to Africa, till orders were
coming from Italy and Turkey and Paris and
London and America. In this wise, Valaze
was stretching out its tentacles, soon embracing
every section of the civilised and uncivilised
world. In this wise has been paved the way
for me, which led to the hub of the world —
London. It finally became imperative to estab-
lish a depfit in that city. But the idea of a mere
dep6t was soon abandoned when I discovered that
London, with its swarms of Beauty Specialists in
every street, practising this method and that,
selling compounds of every description and
nostrums of every kind, none of them possessing
any particular virtue, some being productive
of positive injury—and being only but a few
hours removed from Paris, with myriads of Beauty
Doctors of its own, and with toilet and beauty
preparations as numerous as the sands of the sea,
I soon discovered that London presented the very
opportunity that I had been waiting for. I have
tested the merits of my preparations severely, by
letting them carry out their work of complexion
preserving and beautifying in climates that are
the most difficult on God’s earth. They came out
with flying colors. I was now determined to throw
my fortunes into the great yawning vortex of
London and Paris combined. I was determined
to place my Valaze preparation in this seething
arena, where it would be opposed by armies of
uncounted complexion specialties, the greater
number of which have been established for years
and years. Would victory be mine ? A battle be-
gan, with diminutive and valiant Yalaze on
the one side, and the safely-entrenched
hosts of charlatans on the other.
One month told the tale. I now
count the bearers of some of the noblest names of
Great Britain amongst my clients. I have letters
of appreciation and thanks from the most famous
of English actresses. Amongst them is the
famous London creator of the Merry Widow, Miss
Lily Elsie, and Miss Elizabeth Firth, who, in the
part of Nathalie, has gathered a goodly quantity
of laurel. And then there is that beautiful and
statuesque Australian girl, Alice Crawford, who
has won London by storm, and the inimitable
comedienne, Miss Ethel Irving. The great Ella-
line Terriss is here, and Cissy Loftus, who now
commands a salary of £5OO a week; and the
charming warbler, Ruth Vincent, and the never-
to-be-forgotten Edna May. My establishment at
24 Gra'fton-street, London, West, consisting of
twenty/tofa-Jqftyk lar 8 e rooms > known as the
Maiso|i,,d£ Beaute Valaze. .. . Oh, but I shall not
say another word, except this: Look, here is a
letter from Mrs. Maesmore Morris, only recently
one of the greatest of Australian stage favorites,
now living in England, whose classic beauty was
proverbial throughout the length and breadth of
Australia. This is what she says
“ ‘ I am so delighted to see that you have started
a depdt in London. For three years now I have
been sending to Australia for Valaze, and it’s joy-
ful to tbink I can obtain it here. I hope you will
have all the success your truly wonderful Creme
deserves. I wouldn’t be without it for the world,
and my skin is not a bad advertisement, as you
know.’
“I am showing this letter as an evidence of
what my Australian expatriated friends think of
my Valaze when away from Australia, with all of
Europe’s best to choose from. And there are
scores of similar letters each day.”
And not another word would Mile. Rubinstein
say. But most interesting indeed were the auto-
graph letters, for which presently Mile, was fish-
ing, letters from women in Christiania, Rio de
Janeiro, Constantinople, Paris, and every nook and
corner of Great Britain, from all over the world,
in fact; letters with coronets, betraying the Royal
origin of the writer, asking for appointments,
ordering preparations, and expressing thanks for
results, which have, till then, been impossible.
One is indeed compelled to exclaim with Celia,
“Oh, wonderful, most wonderful, and most
wonderful ! And yet again, wonderful!”
I asked Mile. Rubinstein to favor me with her
latest photograph. She replied she had none,
but kindly offered me the use of an interesting
sketchy portrait of her (reproduced on this page),
etched at the order of a grateful client by the
Swedish artist, Hagborg, who now enjoys such
vogue in London.
Mile. Rubinstein has now come over to Aus-
tralia on what she calls a flying trip to visit her
friends and business establishments. She must be
back in London for the next London season. She
will visit her New Zealand branches, and pro-
ceed from there to India, where she is
to complete arrangements for two branches.
While with us, every client of hers will
be privileged, as will others who have not
yet been her clients, to ask either personally
or by letter for any advice that may be desired to
be had on the subject of complexion treatments.
In fact, Mile. Rubinstein invites allto take the
fullest advantage of her presence here, and assures
me that she will always be happy to explain any-
thing within reason concerning the new special-
ties which she has brought over with her, either
in the way of treatment at her various Institutes,
or of home treatments. A Russian Balsam Im-
pregnation treatment, which lasts a full hour,
during which time the whole face is practically
saturated with health and beauty-bringing
balsams, has been one of Mile. Rubinstein’s suc-
cesses in London. Special machinery for this
treatment has been imported by her for her Aus-
tralian branches. The effect of the treatment is
the nearest thing to rejuvenation of the skin that
my imagination is capable of picturing.
Amongst other new treatments is one for the
relief of tired, strained eyes, that have become
lined, colorless and unattractive.
As to preparations, the most valuable novelty
is Valaze Speciale. An absolutely non-greasy
variant of the wonderful skin food, for day use.
When applied it leaves the skin without the
slightest trace of oiliness or unctuousness, but pre-
sents a lovely white surface, and when used in
conjunction with the Valaze Snow Lotion or one
of the two face powders that Mile, has the mono-
poly of, a most charming result is attained,
namely, that perfect ivory finish that has been the
rage of the Continent and so sought after. It is
a pure, white preparation, very smooth, easily
worked, with a texture light and airy. Now that
I have seen Valaze Speciale, I cannot understand
how it has ever been possible to get on without it
in this country. “ Valaze,” to build beauty while
you sleep. Valaze Speciale, to bestow it the live-
long day. That must now be the programme of
fashion.
Can you still wonder at Mile. Rubinstein’s
success ? The Valaze Snow Lotion, the most
refined of all Liquid Powders, which
caressingly clings to the skin’s surface,
cools it, and gives that precise tint that
one wishes ; delicate, refreshing, sweet-*
scented, a veritable luxury. Novena
Sun-proof Creme, a scientific protective
against the sun’s malice; the Novena
Cerate, for cleansing the skin when
soap and water should not be used
for it; and then the wonderful Marienbad Flesh
Reducing Tablets, that come from where King
Edward takes the cure year in and year out. But
unless I mean to turn this interview into a cata-
logue or into a sort of Beauty’s Shop Window, I
must pull myself up and only say, “ May she con-
tinue meeting with success which so deservedly is
hers —may this clever little lady.”
To show how different scientific beauty-culture
is from unscientific and slipshod methods, Mile.
Rubinstein’s book, “Beauty in the Making,” has
been translated ..and adapted for circulation in
English-speaking countries. As a contribution to
the literature of Beauty of Woman it has been
acknowledged to be a book of interest. It is a
handbook which sums up the very latest re-
searches of a science that should be dear to the
heart of every woman. “Beauty in the Making”
has been written with a view of making the care
of the complexion and the achievement of its
beauty a pleasant, easy task, an intelligent,
practical occupation, in the privacy of your own
home.
