POLITICAL
POINTS
Consider how a Labor Premier does things.
Some time last year, or the year before, Adolphus
Bent wasgrieved to hear about the prevalentadulte-
ration of chaff, hay, bran, etc. After a while he
actually introduced a Bill which he shoved gently
half way through the House; which point it
reached in a damaged condition. Then, however,
something happened, and he left off shoving!
Now Premier Murray promises to re-introduce the
measure next session. Yet Price, of South Oss,
though handicapped by a late start, modelled his
Bill on Bent’s measure, put it through both
Houses in a business-like way ; and the fear of the
Law has already entered the damaged soul of the
adulterator in that State.
If only the big landlord disease were
rooted out, Victoria would have sufficient reason
to “feel good ” just now, for its wheat crop is a
record. In the first two weeks of 1908 the rail-
ways carried only 457,000 bags of wheat; the first
fortnight of 1909 saw Tait given the job of humping
(strictly by proxy) 1,484,000 bags. But still cocky
can find something to growl about and be happy :
he can’t get trucks fast enough to carry off his
fatness —or the landlord’s fatness, as the case may
be- to market. And when there are not enough
trucks to carry about 75 per cent, more than he
usually wants carried, he heaves a brick at
“Socialism,” and the Deform apostle whispers
in his ear that Private Enterprise and Anti-Sosli
between them can always do the thing better.
Somehow, though, Private Enterprise and Anti!
Sosh never offer to carry cocky’s starving stock
for nothing when the drought year lopes along)
ROUSE YE, MY COMRADES, ROUSE YE!
Last year a camp was prevented, through the railways
not having sufficient rolling stock available. Mr. Ewing-
the late Minister, then, it is claimed, promised a double
camp this year, and the force is now looking to have this
arze ngement kept . The departmen tal answer is that
practicall all the money went in the Fleet demonstra-
tions. The volunteers also cannot have a camp, and the
Garrison Artillery can have only four days.
The Battle of North Sydney ” was the
heading which the Evening News affixed in large
black letters to its account of certain operations
on the anniversary of the Day We Were Lagged
and ot suggested that something really serious
was being done in the way of military training.
,ut a reading of the lengthy yarn revealed that
there were 70 kiddies on one side and 125 small
boys on the other, and a total of 195 offsprings
isn t quite an army—in fact, it isn’t quite a regi-
ment even. ”
The Wade Government Police Offences
legislation, under which Sunday traders, for-
tune-tellers, spiritists and idle persons are
being chased from pillar to post, is in some
respects a reproduction of the N.S.W. police
regulations issued by Governor Macquarie in 1811.
Macquarie’s regulations proved a failure in the
end, but for a year or so they had a terror-
ising effect. The potentate fixed 9 p.m.
as the hour to shut up pubs, wine shops,
and similar places in Sydney, and if
noise were heard on the premises later,
the constable had authority to break
in and carry all the inmates to the
lockup. A constable who suspected
a house to be of bad character was
authorised to break in and carry off the
inmates. Idle and disorderly persons
were to be whipped and confined. “All
poor persons not using proper means to
get employment ... or wilfully absenting
themselves from their work ’’ were to be
treated as idle and disorderly, and whipped.
The fact that this treatment was specially
reserved for poor persons is worth noting.
Two convictions qualified the “ poor person ”
to be classified as an “incorrigible rogue,”
and then he was liable to a long
term in gaol and frequent whip-
pings. Macquarie never did
things by halves. The police
grew fairly affluent under these
regulations ; when they weren’t paid
they had an insane desire to haul
almost anybody away to the whip-
ping emporium, so as a rule they
were paid.
The recently-passed Australian
Stowaways Act has seemingly set
steamship companies decisively on
the warpath against the surreptitious
pig-tailed passenger. Recently the
German steamer Prinz Waldemar
left Hongkong after a search which
didn’t unbunker a solitary illicit
Chow. At odd times during fine
weather at sea the officers had another
search, and discovered nothing in
particular. But it was at Simpson-
hafen, New Britain, that the ship-
ping people waded in properly—the
wading in, it is understood, is to
be a usual disturbance on the
co.’s steamers at Simpsonhafen
in future. Passengers and
Chinese crew were sent ashore,
and the vessel was handed
over to a posse of 20 native
police. After eight hours’
ferreting in the steamer’s in-
nards the triumphat nigs,
dragged into daylight seven verv
sick and smudgy Celestial tourists
whom they had found sojourning un-
obtrusively in the coal bunkers. As an
outpost for White Australia t.ho No™
Britain nigger distinctly scored that trip.
The feminine candidates for positions as
Labor nominees for next N.S.W. Senate election
failed to win a place. The successful three were
all men McDougrall, who was in last unsuccessful
bunch; Gardiner, Labor M.L.A. for Orange in
last State Parliament; and Arthur Rae, who was
in the Assembly years ago, and has put up a re-
cord as an unsuccessful candidate since then.
But a fine sturdy battler is Rae, whom it would
take more than a few unsuccesses to crush.
The Ward Government (M.L.) could do
with millions, but it looks m the till, and what it
doesn’t see there makes it go a bit slower. Both
Wellington and Auckland want new post offices.
Tenders were invited; then, having got them’
the Ministry further inspected the till, and de-
cided that it couldn’t accept any. Here and
there credit is being given to Ward and Co for
pigeon-holing tenders “till the finances are cleaned
up.” On the same principle, this writer is
entitled to public applause for not buying three
motor cars, a steam yacht, and £1,000,000 worth
of pictures, not to mention a few trifles which
wouldn’t look so well in print.
“ D.M.W.,” from Maoriland :
The constitution of Premier Ward’s new Ministry
shows that the potentate from Awarua has a catholic
mind in matters political. Scarcely apolitical opinion
that is current anywhere to-day lacks enthusiastic repre-
: sentation in his Cabinet. Fowlds, Single-taxer and Free-
trader works in amity with Miliar, enthusiastic
Protectionist. Tom Mackenzie, freeholder, is to run in
harness with Hogg, an ardent leaseholder. N°-ata a
staunch advocate of work, will assist Jimmy Carroll who
has long held the portfolio of Repose. Bu'ddo has ’been
brought in to keep George Laurenson out; it was found
necessary to give Canterbury a member, and little
Laurenson takes such a large size in brains that the
Cabinet couldn’t safely hold him. Roddy Mackenzie is
generally regarded as having secured hi's place by the
power of his breath and the urgency of his whisker but
those who know Roddy are of a different opinion.’ He
has on one or two occasions displayed so truculent an
attitude m Parliament that his inclusion in the Ministry
may be an act of discretion on the part of a great leader
One thing is clear. With such a collection of colleagues
Ward does not mean to legislate. He will just sit still
and borrow until the cup of wrath is full and the hoof of
doom kicks him into the outer darkness.
“Borrow all you can!” says John
Forrest. It is never too late to spend. Borrowed
money gravitates largely to the pockets of the
monopolists, and, since posterity has to pay why
hesitate to borrow? Posterity never did any-
thing for us, consequently it is our privilege to do
for posterity—also to do posterity. Every coun-
try reaps what it owes ; but John Forrest will not
be around at the harvest time, so his advice is
“ borrow all you can.”
Cadge what you can. With little ruth
Allow the people of to-morrow
To learn in sorrow dire the truth—
’Tis easier to earn than borrow !
The murder of Constable Hyde by three
mysterious miscreants lias r.t length turned Ade-
laide’s attention to the subject of its police pro-
tection, and Adelaide wears a worried look in
consequence. Sydney provides a Robert for about
every 569 inhabitants, and Melbourne a similar
official generally a very bad one—for about every
004 inhabitants. Adelaide, on the other hand
has to be content—though it isn’t—with one trap
to about every 1011 inhabitants. In the Adelaide
suburbs the area that a bobby has to supervise
often looks like a German principality. Adelaide
and its suburbs cover a bigger area than Sydney
and its suburbs do, and while an Adelaide bobby
has about twice as many people to protect as a
Sydney one he has also, in many cases, to walk
over nine times the ground to do his protecting.'

to-day than at the time it was consummated. If
it had not been done at leisure at the commence-
ment of the century, it would have to be done in
haste to-day, because it is more than ever neces-
sary for the existence of Australia in the Pacific.”
—John A. Cockbukn, chairman . at
the Australasian Chamber of, Com-
merce in London on December 28.
THE NEW FRIENDLY
ATTITUDE.
At a recent fish dinner, at Manly, Federal Minister
Mahon assured State Premier Wade of his profound re-
spect and admiration, and so forth. In reply Wade ex-
plained that so long; as the Australian Government
adopted his views on State Frights it would find him a
very easy man to get on with.
WADE (to Mahon): “ Yes, my little hoy, so long
as you keep your face nicely washed and do
everything I tell you, things’ll be all right
between us.”
Westralia is staggering in the beer-soaked
wake of Maoriland, as it were, and is wildly
waving a dead marine. Last week the irrepres-
sible Government of Newton Moore raised its
poor, sick head just to murmur “ Fill ’em up
agen.” This time it is a mere trifle, a new loan of
£1,445,000, which ought to keep the State in a
reasonable condition of hiccup for a while. Yet
Westralia will wake up some morning sobered.
Something happened in Melbourne the
other day which is calculated to make Maoriland
and other wild borrowers and boomsters feel as
though a glacier was crawling down their spine.
Just when the Melbourne boom was getting into
its stride the N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co. sold a grain and wool store with a 198 ft.
frontag-e to Collins-street, west end, for £210,000.
Shortly afterwards the property was passed on to
ex-Premier James Munro’s Real Estate Bank for
£265,000. Last week, after being carefully nursed
for 17 years, it was sold at what is reckoned the
very fair price of £50,000.
British War-Minister Haldane has been
scoffing at the slack individual who “amuses
himself, but does nothing for his country ” in the
way of defence; and, considering that London,
with 6,500,000 people—more than one-seventh of
the whole population of the British isles—has
only supplied about 15,500 men to the Territorial
Army, it looks as if Haldane had a right to jeer.
Yet, in reality, he has none. Young men don’t
join the Territorial Army, because, if they do so,
they get the sack. Employers don’t want men
who have to get a few hours’ leave every now and
then for drill. If training were compulsory the
employer would have to put up with the dis-
ability, because all young men would have the
same drawback ; but owing to persons like Hal-
dane it isn’t compulsory, therefore the young man
who volunteers loses his job and the one who
doesn’t volunteer gets it. Probably the voung
man would like compulsory training as a break in
the shop or office routine, but he can’t get it; he
only gets Haldane’s foolish scoff. Haldane ,says
in his bitterness that “a short, sharp Act off Par-
liament in war-time ” may compel the slack person
“to do his duty in the matter of home defence ”
—in other words, he will be rushed to the front
at the last moment, Untrained, and not knowing
one end of his gun from the other. A? brilliant
man is Haldane-
A 25-Year-Old Razor. — To have stood the
test of fierce competition for 25 years, and to have come
out on top, is no mean record. And this is what the
Plum Razor, for which Messrs. Craig & Aitken, of 654
George Street, Sydney, hold theagencv, has succeeded in
doing. When a reason is sought, the solution is found in-
the fact that the Plum is “ one of the good old sort ” of
razors that have never sacrificed qualitv for cheapness,
and that can be depended on at all times to maintain a
consistently keen edge under all climatic conditions.
Its blade is of pure Silver Steel—the finest metal known
to the trade, and the forging is done by the good old-
fashioned hand method that existed before the cheaper
processes came into being. The man who decides upon
the Plum for his next razor will experience the delights
of a cool, easy shave whenever he requires it. The price
is the Strictly reasonable one of 10/6, posted free any-
where, and the agents, Messrs. Craig & Aitken, offer to
purchasers a guarantee for two years, for which period
they will also keep the Plum in good shaving order.*
All who are interested in Australian
national polities should read “The Commonwealth
Crisis,” by C. H. Kirmess, the great serial now
running through The Lone Hand. Forecasting
a “peaceful” Japanese colonisation of the
Northern Territory, Kirmess traces the political
and war consequences with a masterly hand.
See that you do not miss “The Commonwealth
Crisis.”*
Josiah Thomas, P.M.G., billed as a Sun-
day Sydney Domain orator, gives political tone to
a much-decayed form of open-air mouth exercise.
The Domain was one time a recognised arena for
the Sunday afternoon discussion of the politics of
the week, and such men as George Dibbs,
Deniehy, Parkes apd Robertson did not think it
beneath them to occupy a stump. But when
noisy religionists invaded the place, politics, ex-
cept of the wilder type, retired, and for years
even the most unconventional members of Parlia-
ment kept off the grass.
SHACKLES!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Referring to the coming Imperial Press Conference,
London newspapers describe it as marking a fresh
strengthening of the ties between the Motherland and
the colonies. —News item.
It is not upon affection
That the London press relies
To continue the connection—
But Tins !
Or, in dealing with the question,
It (the L.P.) sometimes sinks
To an amiable suggestion—
Of links !
When a stranger, shown attention,
To the usual toast responds,
He is always sure to mention —
Th ' BONDS !
Ties and links and bonds, ye setters
Of the speech bereft of brains,
Are but other names for FETTERS—
And CHAINS !
If, alas, our early hist’ry
Must o’ershadow all our lives,
Let us speak with deeper myst’ry
Of GYVES !
n. s.\v.
W. T. GOODGE.

Mr. Mitchell, the Minister of Agriculture, said that'the
Government was seriously handicapped in developmental
works by want of funds. The only way out of the diffi-
cultv was to borrow.
Mr. John Forrest said he was delighted with the san-
guine tone of Mr. Mitchell’s speech. They should borrow
all they could.—Neivs item.
Hark ! What does the statesman say?
“ Borrow, brothers, borrow.”
In the good old spendthrift way,
Heedless of To-morrow.
Blow To-morrow ! Dash Next Week 1
In a manner mild and meek,
Let us Uncle Isaac seek,
He will help us borrow.
Uncle Isaac won’t refuse
Help to us -who borrow ;
We will sign his I O U’s
Dated for To-morrow—
Who’ll redeem ’em ? Let the Kids !
What we want is quids—bright quids—
We will do as Forrest bids ;
Brothers, let us borrow !
Brothers, let us to the Yids,
To the Yids to borrow ;
Leave the paying to the Kids,
Let theirs be the sorrow ;
Let them pay what has been lent
To their pa (and duly spent)
At the rate of shent per shent—
Borrow, brothers, borrow.
L’Envoi.
We must borrow all we can,
Damn our children’s sorrow ;
Let’s keep on as we began—
Borrow, borrow, borrow !

HARRISON OWEN.
PALE, LISTLESS
GROWING GIRLS
Young Sydney Woman tells how
she Overcame Anaemia
during her Girlhood.
Every mother of a growing girl should know
what anaemia is, and how to recognise it. It is
one of the commonest complaints of growing girls
and one of the most dangerous, because their de-
velopment makes extra demands on the blood, and
there is always the tendency if neglected, that it
may run into consumption.
Anaemia is simplya lack of blood.. Its approach
is so stealthy that it is often well developed be-
fore it is recognised. Paleness, listlessness, head-
aches airad sideaches, weakness in the limbs, breath-
lessness on the slightest exertion are amongst the
earliest symptoms. If steps be not taken to
renew the blood supply, pernicious antemia and
often a decline follow.
_ For renewing the blood supply there is nothing
like Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. The great Cana-
dian doctor whose experiments resulted in their
production, aimed at embodying in pill form,
something that would make new blood and
strengthen the nerves, recognising that, with
strong nerves and a healthy blood supply, people
could have very few complaints. How well he
succeeded the fame of the pills bears out.
In the case of Mrs. Edward Curran, 46 Lawson
Crescent, Alexandria, Sydney, after suffering
severely with anaemia during girlhood, she found a
complete cure in Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People.
4- I got very run down and was ailing off and on
for a long time,” said Mrs. Curran. “I lost my
appetite completely; the daintiest food didn’t
tempt me. I always had a sick bilious feeling
after eating. The food lay on my chest and gave
me a pain between my breast bone and shoulder
blades, and I’d feel thoroughly miserable. If I
started to read a book my sight would be blurred.
On a hot day I’d feel more upset than ever. I’d
be so utterly tired I could hardly drag along or
do a bit of housework. I rarely got a night’s good
sleep. I’d be so restless and toss about for hours.
My tongue was rough and furred and my mouth
had a vile metallic taste. As to color, that went
altogether, and my skin turned quite brown. I’d
nearly always wake up with a severe headache,
and be drowsy all day. At last I tried Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. They did me good from the
first. They started by improving my appetite
and taking away that heavy choking feeling in
my chest. I kept on taking them and bit by bit
my headaches left me. My color improved and
I quite lost the worn, tired feeling. I began to get
good sound sleep and wake up fresh and clear.
My health since has been splendid.”
When you ask for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
don’t be put off with anything else. Go where
you can get the genuine with Dr. Williams’ name
on the outside Wrapper; it’s the genuine that cures.
Price 2s. !)d. a box, or six boxes for 15s. 3d. To be
had of storekeepers and chemists, or sent direct
by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia,
Ltd., Sydney.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
N.S.W.-Victoria final match was an amazing
circumstance, but dreary withal. In the first inninos
N.S.W. smacked and slashed unmercifully, and, by
making 815, broke its own inter-State record of 805 for a
single innings. The previous record was made by the
N.S.W. XI. at Melbourne in 1905. In the smashin" - of the
recordNobledidgood work, tottingup 213, which, although
a gigantic score, was still far from a record. Consider-
ably higher scores have been made in club cricket, such
as the 1074 made by the Melbourne University Club in
March, 1898; the 922 put up by Carlton in 1896, and
others in both Britain and Australia. The Victorian
answer to the crushing 815 was 468 in the first innings
and 487 in the second innings, which left N.S.W.
only 141 to make to win; and it got them for
the loss of four wickets. Considering the fearsome
start of N.S.W. it was anything but a disgraceful defeat
and showed the solid grit of the Victorians. They
gave no chances, and played the game coolly and without
sign of funk or despair. They looked like men with a
heavy job on hand settling down to do it or burst. They
failed, but were not disgraced.
The previous aggregate record was the 1739
piled up by N.S.W. and Stoddart teams at Sydney Cricket
Ground in February, 1898. This time the contending
teams put up 1911 between them. The gate receipts
soared up to £1233, and the smile on the face of the
N.S.W.O.A. grew daily more expansive. It wanted the
money very badly.
Leaving the old ’uns out of it, the feature of the
match was the batting of the two left-handed colts Rans-
ford and Bardsley. Ransford’s double effort of 182 and 110
climbs amongst the dizzy heights of double-century re-
cords of Australia—the land of cricket giants ; while
Bardsley smacked together 192 and 24 in a manner that
can, without hysterics, be pronounced promising. Aus-
tralian XI. teams of later years have always been rich in
left-handers ; and now that Joseph Darling and Clem
Hill have about faded out, Bardsley and Ransford carry
on the tradition.
fhe match blew to pieces many bowling reputa®
tions. Right through the piece the bat beat the ball with
weary monotony. The only trundler who showed repre-
sentative form was J. C. Barnes, of N.S.W. ; he gathered
in six inter-State wickets for 59 runs and five for 147—11
wickets of the 29 for 206. He is a curly bowler who
heaves the leather with many strange twists and much
deceit, and slays his batsmen by deft trickery instead of
by brute force, as Cotter does. If Barnes maintains his
in the Australian XI. v. the Best of Australia
Sin certainly have a chance of lifting - one of the
i. 600 swag's of English money there has been so much
wrangling over.
The above prophecy should be almost a cer-
tainty; for if ever there was need for a bowler it is now.
Consider the efforts of some of our alleged “ bests ” in
» 6 S™ upheaval: Cotter, four for 175 ; Saunders, two
for 206 ; Armstrong, three for 128; Laver, one for 113;
Vernon, two for 202 ; Hazlitt, two for 151; Emery, two
for 140 ; Hopkins, 0 for 108. These be our best bowlers !
A pretty crowd to send abroad.
* * * *
Although he has announced his retirement from
cricket often and vigorously, Clementine Hill has always
returned snuling to the pitch, with his flannels drawn a
trifle tighter across his girth on each succeeding occasion.
Now that he has elevated his back and declined a job in
the Australian XI. he practically steps out. He started
in the international cricket trade in 1896, and this will be
the first time he has missed a trip to the Cold Country.
But it is like the case of the man who tries to shoot across
the front of the tram, and misses. He goes out for ever.
And nothing so ill-befitted Hill’s long cricket career as the
unseemly squabble for the cash that preceded his leaving
it. He commenced the cricket industry earl}' and leaves it
still a comparatively young man.
Grade cricket in Sydney on Saturday produced
a sudden burst of g*reat splendor. No less than five men
scored the century and over: A. C. Mackenzie, 165 ;
Norman Deane, 121; S. H. Emery, 120; E. L. Waddy,
115; and L. Blundell, 105. Among the bowlers the best
were Cotter’s seven for 38, A. J. Bowden’s three for 35,
J. C. Barnes’ three for 49, S. H. Emery’s four for 59, and
Bamford’s four for 72. After that, mediocrity and failure.
It was the concluding day of a round. Waverley
beat University by 207 runs on the first innings. Uni. had
accumulated a miserable 178 in the first attempt, and
Waverley smothered it with 385, of which Mackenzie’s 165
was a valuable portion. Mac. was in great form. The
trouble is that occasionally he just gets tired, and then
he s horrible. North Sydney downed Balmain by 110 runs
on the first innings, closing with seven wickets down for
292 against Balmain’s poor 182. Deane’s 121 was a useful
contribution to the North Sydney total. Burwood’s glorv
has indeed faded, and its tail is long in the dust, when
Middle Harbor beats it bj' 73 on the first innings.
The figures were 274 and 347 respectively. At Parramatta
Glebe fell on Central Cumberland and wiped it out with
a first-innings margin of 162 runs—266 to 104. The
struggle between Gordon and Petersham was closer;
Gordon made 206 to Petersham’s 294, leaving Petersham
top dog with 88 to the good. Sydney and Bedfern dunno
exactly where they are, owing to some of the scoring
books not balancing. However, there doesn’t seem to be
very much doubt that Bedfern won by five wickets. It
could hardly be otherwise, as Sydney never wallops any-
body.
The result of the upheaval was to leave Glebe
still cock of the competition with 16 points ; Waverley
has 10, Central Cumberland 8, and after Cumberland the
figures drop to too blushful a depth to be advertised.
* * * *
That stupendous water carnival, N.S.W. Anni-
versary Begatta, came off on Tuesday week last, and was
generally considered a howling, frantic success. Cer-
tainly it was a charming day, with a fine breeze from
E.N.E., coming along occasionally in little spiteful puffs
which quickly thinned the lubbers out and put even the
best of craftsmen on their mettle. As usual, the P. and
O. liner was flagship ; this time it was the new 11,000-ton
Morea that filled the bill, and it was crowded in every part.
Honors were fairly well divided among the representative
boats. Queensland craft Violet won the double event in
the 14ft. class, during the first race of which the
Westralian boat Elma turned turtle. The Sydney boat
Mvra carried off the double event in the 10ft. class, and
Saxton’s beautiful Awanui, the fastest yacht in
Sydney Harbor, easily romped home with the champion-
ship and the 10-metres-and-over section ; while Petrel,
champion for many 3 r ears, maintained the sovereignty in
the Below-10-metres section, beating J. O. Fairfax’s
imported model Magic. Charles Belph did well in
motor boat events, scoring one win and one second. In-
vincible, starting from scratch, beat Mr. Henry’s
Wegiheh, although conceding 21min. 56sec. in the eight-
mile run. Whatmore’s luck was out, and his Baby Im-
perial, the new hydroplane, unfortunately broke down.
It was a bad day for anything of the hydroplane class.
In the other division, Belph’s Magic, a boat whose engines
he designed and constructed prior to the creation of the
Invincible, was beaten by Mr. Sanderson’s G.E.S.S., which
had 9Jmin. start. G.E.S.S. won by Imin. 41see. The
distance was four miles.
N.S.W. Motor Boat Club still plods along its
uneventful way. The chief end of the body now is to
hold Venetian Night Entertainments, and they are fine
things—from a social point of view. The next will be
held in Gore Bay on Saturday, February 6, and during the
afternoon there will be a series of races for three classes.
Gipsy tea rages from 5.30 to 7.30 p.m., after which the
entertainment and the band will hold the fort. The club
looks forward to having an eventful day—or evening.
There were 35 boats at the last upheaval, and 28 were
decorated in a becoming manner with Chinese lanterns
and colored lights. This time it is hoped that 50 will be
present, and that all will be decorated.
After all, the aggressive Australian cannot get
very enthusiastic about the Motor Boat Club. Its mem-
bers own, roughly, £72,000 worth of boats and engines,
and some of the boats are beautiful craft; but of this
vast mass of industrial produce less than 6 per cent, are
engined with Australian-made motors. Of course, the
boats are Australian-built—and, as said before, some of
them are really beautiful craft.
* * * *
More than possible that big Ben Thoroughgood
will have to forfeit the sculling championship of N.S.W.
through inability to raise the cash to cover Pearce’s de-
posit. The challenger’s money has been up about a month
now, and Thoroughgood is still struggling to get his
stake together. The Baymond Terrace crowd of true
sports will not stand to Thoroughgood if he rows any-
where else but on the Hunter Biver, and as the race with
Pearce has been fixed for the Parramatta they have but-
toned up their pockets. No wonder sculling ’is decadent
in Australia when the champion of the State cannot find
backers to put up the modest sum now asked for.
“0.K.”: After what had been heard from
Sydney and Brisbane of “ boy ” Gray’s wonderful pre-
cocity at billiards, the display he gave against Lindrum in
their Melbourne game was disappointing to the critics.
True, he made a record red-hazard break of 500 odd, and
his Strength and accuracy of aim in hazard striking were
equally remarkable. But at every other class of stroke
he was an amateur by comparison with Lindrum, who is
not more than two years his senior, it is said. He fre-
quently failed in his “ forcing losers ” through thumping
the ball. Apparently he had no idea of running up a big
break by other means than the one mechanical stroke
which he has practised assiduously. He made 30 or 40
consecutive “losers” off the red (in one of his second-
best breaks) all the time that his opponent’s white was in
admirable position for bringing the two balls into top-of-
the-table play with a cannon ; and when he broke down
it was through playing at the losing hazard in a difficult
way instead of resorting to the easy cannon. The fact of
Gray being so tied to this particular system of scoring
(which is, next to the obsolete spot stroke, the easiest of
all the systems) suggests that he has no special talent for
shaping a policy. Lindrum may never be a champion of
champions—he strikes me as not having the necessary
physique or “character ” —but he should within the next
year or two widen the gap that separates him from the
young specialist.
■» * * *
Motor-racing, which has been a gold mine to the
cinematograph man, is fading out in Europe, all the lead-
ing firms of the continent having signed a bond of £4OOO
not to compete in this year’s Grand Prix. So probably
the Grand Prix will die suddenly. Future events of the
kind will be restricted to reliability trials. The roads
will be a lot safer in some parts of Europe henceforth.
The Dunlop Rubber Co. has now mapped out
the course of the Dunlop Adelaide-to-Sydney Relay Ride,
and divided the journey into 63 sections, the longest of
which is that across the Coorong Desert of 38J miles.
As there are two riders to each section, the total force
will consist of 126 cyclists. The ride will be opened by
Baulderstone and Richards, of Adelaide, and concluded
by long-distance amateur crack E. A. Pearson and T.
Larcombe, the road champion of the Ma State.
• * • *
A. Humrn, the well-known Maoriland road
rider, who has on several occasions visited Australia to
take part in the Dunlop Road Race from Warrnambool
to Melbourne, and in which he has registered some very
fine performances (including a third in 1906), has just
established a great performance by lowering Andy
Calder’s long-standing cycle record from Christchurch to
Dunedin. Hunun, who rode “ Dunlops,” covered the 247
miles of mountainous country in 18 hours 20 minutes,
some four hours 19 minutes faster than Calder’s record.*

The inter-State open boat competitions came to
an end in Sydney on Saturday last, when Myra, of Port
Jackson, won the 10ft. championship by 2min. 9sec. from
Queensland Irene. Myra led right round the course, and
at no time was it seriously challenged. The 14-ft. Inter-
State Handicap w'as also won by a Sydney boat, J. Smith’s
Rene, which beat Botany Bay flyer St. George by 15sec.
* ■* * *
Frank Gotch, who was accused by Hacken-
schmidt of foul and brutal tactics, has been getting him-
self disliked by the gentlemen who call themselves
“ the real sports.” Seems that lately an Orien-
tal party hight Yousiff Mahmout, or something
like that, calling himself “The Terrible Turk” (all
Turkish wrestlers appear to be Terribles), bobbed
up and requested the pleasure of putting Gotch’s
shoulders against the mat. Gotch assumed a haughty
attitude, and told the Turk to go out and get himself a
reputation before making requests like that. Mahmout
went out, and, catching another Yankee named Jenkins,
an alleged wrestler of credit and renown, fell upon him,
shifted two of his ribs, and finally crushed him into sub-
mission and insensibility. When thev hauled him off his
victim the Turk was amazed to find lie had hurt the man,
and explained that he had not squeezed hard because the
Amurkan didn’t struggle. Then Gotch sailed for England
with great suddenness, and failed to notice the purse for
10,000 dollars that someone had hung out as an induce-
ment. Gotch simply Got.
* * * *
The Metropolitan Rifle Association’s prize
meeting, at Rand wick (Sydney) last week, failed a trifle
so far as shooting at the fixed target was concerned ; and
the only “ possible ” was put up by VV. W. Smith, with 10
shots at 800yds. At the 900yds. range, N. Pettigrew’s
score of 48 was on top ; while at lOOOvds. F. Atkins and
W. C. Coulter came off with the best scores of 46 each.
The majority of the shootists evidently suffered from the
distance of the object to be fired at and the gauging of
the rifle. In the “ running man ’’—eight shots at 300yds.
nine shootists hit the inanimate object with every bul-
let; and at the “disappearing disc”—eight shots at
1 3 r, ? emen never failed. The meeting showed
that the shootists had a better chance at a disappearing'
target than at a stationary one, and that is a good sign
of practical rifle-shooting.

The bowls fever has caught on very seriously in
Victoria, and 147 entries have poured in for the single-
handed championship of the State. Everywhere there
are budding champions, and the return visit to N.S.W.
this year is set down as a time for upheaval and revenge,
•*■***
Victorian Lawn Tennis Association is in a state
of high glee, fondly caressing a credit balance of £BO3.
The Murray-State association reaped £350 from the
season’s inter-State tourney; and the windfall will
possibly be spent in fostering the game. There is room
for extension and for encouraging young Australians to
defend the recently-acquired Davis Cup.
* * * *
A shrewdly-planned coup resulted successfully
at the recent A.J.C. Anniversary meeting. Directly the
weights were known, Foley and McGrath, the astute
trainers of Neith and Plunder respectively, with a couple
of others, gathered in all double money going about
Neith for the Challenge Stakes and Plunder for the Anni-
versary Handicap, taking as little as hundreds to three
before shutting up. The excellence of the judgment was
evidenced by the triumph of both nags, though each had
little more than an eyelash, a nose, or something of the
kind to the good. Bred in N.S.W., Plunder has
done the greater part of his racing in Queensland,
where he appropriated the May Day Cup, King’s Plate,
Brisbane Cup and other remunerative things. His latest
win was similar to the Brisbane Cup success, when he led
from end to end of the trip ; but Yabba Gabba (14 to 1)
nearly upset the “pot,” and finished close enough to be
just beaten, and no more.
Jack Smith, winner of the Hobart Cup last
week, didn t score out of his turn. Four wins in four
seasons isn’t much for the nag’s owners (the Messrs.
Clarke) to jubilate over, though two of the previous suc-
cesses were the Bond Cup and the V.R.C. Autumn
Handicap.
* * * *
Cleverly-laid plans were completely upset in
the Park States at Canterbury, Sydney, on Saturday,
when the bottled-up 4-year-old Mozart-Mirabelle geld-
ing, Merei, was freed, and money was heaped on him till
evens was the best price. Hopelessly out of it in the
earlier part of the race through being badly placed,
Merei (who had only just been named) put in a meteoric
finish from the home turn, to be just beaten by 20-to-l-
-chance Plaiden. It is because of the frequency of such
happenings that Ikey Mo is sleek and his loot bag weighty.
Years ago Jack Murray was the recognised
leader of the Totalisator movement in Vic. Assembly.
He used to introduce a Tote Bill periodicals, and Frank
Madden, the present Mr. Speaker, used to denounce it on
behalf of the V.R.C. committee. Nowadays Madden
would vote for a Tote Bill on behalf of the racing club
but Premier Murray is not likely to father one. ’
* * * *
Merman, the Australian horse that Mrs
Langtry bought from the late W. R. Wilson, and won
some big races with in England, has gone to Germany to
beget high-class cavalry horses and hunters from half-
bred mares. Merman, in his old age, seems to have
acquired a great name for hardness. As a racehorse sire
he was not a brilliant success, but his stock have in-
herited his sound constitution and sturdy legs.
Australia is now rapidly solidifying into one
concrete nation. The latest move is to bring together
all the State governing horse racing clubs, and elect a
Federal governing body, which shall control the registra-
tion of horses and the future publication of the Australian
Stud Book. The idea is to have a strong, practically
omnipotent body, which will exercise a general control,
while leaving purely local matters for the State governing
clubs. One of the worries at present is the duplication
of names and an enormous mass of correspondence
between clubs, which the establishment of a central
governing body will do away with.

A line as to the feebleness of the local assault-
vendors by comparison with those who do business else-
where may be got from the doings (21/12/’08) of Sam
Langford in San Francisco. Samuel there engaged Jim
Flynn, who last year accounted for Bill Squires. Says an
exchange:—
“After two minutes of fighting, Langford feinted with
his right. Like a flash he brought his left flush to the
fireman’s jaw, with an impact that was heard all over the
house. Flynn dropped as if hit with a log, his face turned
purple, and he rolled on his back until the pain from the
blow caused him to turn. The count of 10 was tolled off,
but Flynn was too far gone to even hear the count. He
remained helpless on the floor of the ring with his seconds
working- over him for fully two minutes. He was then
carried to his corner, and it was five minutes before his
senses returned.
Langford is sft. 7£in., and weighs but lOst. 21b.
The Americans are marvellous people. Three
months ago, they were yelping at Tommy Burns with one
united yelp to drop his nonsense about the color line and
face Brother Johnson like a man. Jim Jeffries yelped in
this regard as loudly and as persistently as any of them.
When the U.S. heard that Thomas was really going" to
meet the Black Agony they were pleased. They started
forecasting the result of the event, and many of their
forecasts favored the white champion. Jim Jeffries for
example, went into details on the subject, and
Burns the decision six weeks before the fio-ht on"his
superior footwork, his infighting ability, and” his game-
ness. Now the fickle James says this, which is what 90
per cent, of the other fight fans in Hamland say
“ Tommy Burns’ mistake, the one great mistake of his
career, was in allowing Johnson the opportunity to fight
for the title. I refused time and again to meet Johnson
"’bile I was holding the title, even though I knew I could
beat him. I would never allow a negro a chance to fight
for the world’s championship, and I advise all other
champions to follow the same course. Tomm v Burns has
been vastly overrated as a fighter. This has always been
my belief.”
Of a verity, N. Brusso spoke sooth when he remarked
after the Johnson chastisement, that “ Americans have
no use for a beaten man.” They are the world’s worst
sportsmen.

“ Adolphus Attic ” writes :
I beg to advance my limited experience of the colored
person.
For my sins, I have been compelled by Providence to
dwell in a chaotic boarding-house that perches on the
nos.e of a suburban cliff, like a morbid seagull contemplat-
ing suicide. It is not on the brow of the cliff, where the
free winds of Heaven could blow in the back door and
blow out the front, taking - with them the pale-green
smell of cooking ; it is half-way up, so that the back of
the house is jambed against the solid rock like the latter
end of a desperado who has been brought to (Neutral)
bay, and is putting up a last stand against fearful odds
and worse cooking. Hanging precariously over my head
(I have a back room) is a cheap lodging'.house, crammed
with cheap lodgers, and pervaded by a landlady who wears
her hair in curl-papers in the morning and goes
out mysteriously to dine with thp local nobility
at night, clad in a red silk evening dress and a
look of cold scorn. It is one of my favorite
nightmares that the cheap lodging-house will be blown
down some night when the wind is high, and that I will
wake and find mv room filled with broken bricks and
cheap lodgers, and the landlady in the dress that looks
like the burning of London in the days of Charles Two.
However, this is getting away from my own lodging-den,
and my experience of the colored person, who is the oook
thereof, and a proud nigger to boot. (It is on record that
one lodger booted him in the fragrant long ago, but he
was a strong man, and is now dead. Peace to his feet!)
Well, this undesirable alien, who spoils the viands for us,
has an unreasonable prejudice against boiled eggs. 1
love a hard-boiled egg for breakfast, but I can’t obtain it
unless the maid-in-waiting cooks it with her own
fair hands - and a little assistance from the gas
stove. She informs me that, at the mention of- hard-
boiled eggs, the undesirable flies into a fearful passion
and rolls his eyes murderously at the meat-chopper.
Wherefore she finds it easier to do it for me herself.
Although I have been resident in the house now six
months, I have never sighted the cook ; he dwells apart
in the kitchen and is not visible to the undressed eye. If
ever I meet him, I will demand at the point of the carving
knife—which is an incredibly blunt instrument—on what
grounds he refuses to boil my morning egg. I will de-
mand to know if eggs are against his political principles
or if they clash with his religious convictions, and if he
doesn’t give me a satisfactory answer I will carve his tur-
ban into four pieces and throw the four fragments in the
range. I dislike having to take extreme measures, bur
the insolence of this Asiatic with regard to my egg is
driving me to desperation.
In a country of vast distances and scat
tered settlement, where doctors are few and far
between, a reliable medical work is most neces-
sary. It is not intended to take the place of the
doctor, but to tell people what to do till a doctor
can be obtained. Many a valuable life is lost
because people know nothing about the rudiments
of first aid, and by the time a doctor has been
brought 50 miles or so the patient is beyond hope.
To meet this necessity The Bulletin Co. is
issuing a complete set of Dr. P. E. Muskett’s
works, “The Australian Medical Guide,” in two
volumes; “The Attainment of Health,” and
“The Management of Infants,” one vol. each.
The whole set is retailed for the exceedingly
reasonable sum of £3. *
“Adelaide ” : T. Bent has gone now, but
the other day I seemed to find one reason for the
amazing (to outsiders) popularity that kept hint
on top so long. I had to visit Melbourne for an
official function, and I went to it in the regulation
frock coat and tall hat; and I met Bent face to
face outside the building. I had never seen hint
before, and was surprised to find how little The
Bulletin has exaggerated his figure. I will
cheerfully make oath that he didn’t know me
from a bar of soap. Yet I was gaudily clothed.
I might be (though I didn’t happen to be) Some-
one. And I got an unexpected beaming smile
and bow. As already remarked, a habit like that
might go part of the way to explain things.
A STOUSH PATRON SPEAKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill Squires and Bill Lang battle for the heavyweight
championship of Australia on Wednesday, February o.—
News item.
Bill, w’en these few,remarks appears in print
(And unnerstan’ by “ Bill” that William Lang
As well as “ Boshter ” Bill I mean), by dint
Of many a wallop, biff, punch, bash and bang
You—either Lang or Squires, that is to say—
Will have out-fought the other bloque, and won
The proudest title that can come the way
Of local pug—viz., that of Champion.
Willvum, I grant no words in this ere sheet
(Which goes to press on Toosday) can affect
The doings of two lads who’re billed ter meet
The We’n’sday follerin’; yet I reflect
On C. G. Wade, E. Simpson, Rev’rend Ash,
“Father o’ Twelve” and such, and on their pent,
Shocked grief regarding the Burns-Johnson bash ;
And then thoughts come, and will ’ave written vent.
Bill—and I speak alike to ’im who trains
At that ’istoric pub, where flarin’ ramps
(Concocted by sly bike-promoters’ brains)
Once raged as ’ot as cycle-racin’ lamps,
And to the Pride o’ Noocastle—Beware !
Watch ’ow you rouse the Reverend person’s ire !
Do nothin’ coarse on We’n’sday next. The pair
O’ you I urge, as one, not to aspire
To plug the other on the jore or nose—
Which spots, once tapped, are liable to run
With ’orrid gore—nor to direct crool blows
Where they may daze or incommode or stun.
Abandon right-arm jabs, and upper-cuts,
And swings and prods (except in self-defence) ;
Do not arouse the Churchman’s shamed “ Tut-tuts ”
By deeds o’ wild unseemly violence.
Be gentle each with each. Omit to jeer
If Bill smites Bill with such a fierce impact
That he (that’s Bill) falls to the earth. Don’t sneer
If Bill, by Bill, is pushed about and whacked.
The moment that yer spot a drop o’ blood
On self, floor, seconds, posts, or referee
Stop hittin’, Bill, and burst into a flood
Of tears for every pietist to see.
Don’t shove or hustle when you’re in the ring,
Or use rude terms like “ Blow it ail! ” and “ Dash ! ”
Such cuss-words horrify like anything
The looker-on of sanctity and cash.
Be mild and courteous as a little child.
Don’t injure Bill. Look on ’im as a friend.
’Andie ’im gently. Don’t get narked or wild
And knock rents in ’im that may never mend.
But ’old—l’m writin’ on the Toosday eve ;
Ther scrap’s ter-morrer, and these warnin’ lines
Will not appear till Thursday. So I grieve
Me advice is wasted. Futile are me whines.
One partin’ word : If yer both fight like “ beats,”
Clinch, bash, howl, curse, and shame yerselves ter
rights,
Don’t blame the writers in the weekly sheets,
But them that fixes scraps for We’n’sday nights.
N.S.W.
J.B.D.
BAD BREATH.
THE TONGUE IS THE INDEX.
Your breath is foul, but you do not perceive it
yourself. You are like a person who is always
amonp the oils and varnishes—you get used to what
is to many people a most obnoxious smell. It is most
disagreeable to those with whom you come in con-
tact. A slight headache, a bad breath, and a coated
tongue are the first symptoms of stomachic
disease. Take time by the forelock, and intercept
germination ; arrest the germs ere they develop
into disease. A pill in time may save days of
suffering. Look at your tongue in the looking
glass every night and morning, and if it is at all
discolored take a couple of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. They will remove the objectionable
matter from the stomach, regulate the system and
sweeten the breath. One or two of these pills
taken once or twice a week will ensure a well regu-
lated system, fortified against all disease.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a searching
remedy, and are a permanent and positive remedy
for biliouness, indigestion, constipation, bad
breath, headaches, sallow complexion, liver and
kidney troubles, piles, pimples and blotches, and
for all female ailments.

The German Emperor has been making
good resolutions at a great rate. Lately, lie has
resolved to sell five of his castles, to give up
liquor, to seek after peace, to leave off being inter-
viewed, and to discourage any allusions to his
movements in the papers. To put it shortly,
William has sworn off almost everything except
food and bed.
Professions, Etc.,

energy, and is essentially practical.
Well known to most Australian advertisers,

Can design ads. and write business-compelling

The Age, commenting on the report that
Kaiser William has become a teetotaler, says,
gravely, “The statement is prompted by the fact
that the Kaiser is known to be reducing his personal
expenses.” Which leaves us to infer that William
has been in the habit of drinking several breweries
daily.
Willie Watt, Victoria’s new Treasurer, has
been only 11 years in politics, but it has been a
battle all the time. He first arrived by defeating
Prendergast for North Melbourne, in ’97. Before
another election came round, McLean had taken
him in as P.M.G., and that killed him tem-
porarily. He resurrected in ’O2, for East Mel-
bourne ; but the constituency of Jews and “ pay-
ing guests” didn’t suit him, or he didn’t suit the
constituency, so at the ’O4 redistribution he
tackled Essendon, and has held the seat ever
since,,though with difficulty. Between times he
had a couple of unsuccessful shots at the Senate,
and also tried to be Fat’s nominee for the East
Melbourne seat on Findley’s expulsion. It will
be understood that Labor loves him, even as
Burns loves Johnson, and as long as it has a hope
of success will make him fight for his seat. The
present will be his tenth contest in 11 years. By
profession he is an accountant, though since the
McLean debacle he has been running a hay and
corn business in a north-western suburb.
That family of Smiths of which the just
dead Francis Villeneuve, ex-Chief Justice of Tas-
mania, was a member, simply rained lawyers.
The late Francis V. has two sons at the English
Bar. Francis V.’s younger brother was
William V., for many years Adelaide’s chief
criminal man ; and William Y. was uncle of
Francis Y. 11., who is now fast climbing up to
the place in the ladder that uncle used to occupy.
Also there is a younger William V., who pursues
Costs in -Westralia; and there are other Y.
Smiths who are pursuers of Costs in other places ;
but whose nephews these other V. Smiths are this
writer doesn’t know for certain.
The native-born Australian has not as yet
reached the N.S.W. Chief Justiceship. Matthew
Henry Stephen (born in Hobart) and G. B. Simp-
son (Parramatta) reached to the height of Acting
Chief, but the full office has so far gone to men
from abroad. Two of them, Dowling and Salo-
mons, were born in England ; Martin and Darley
came from Ireland ; Alfred Stephen was born a,t
St. Cristopher in the West Indies, and Francis
Forbes in Bermuda. Martin and Salomons both
arrived here when still young enough to become
good Australians, but the others were men in
years when they commenced their Australian
careers.

The Dropping of the Gentle Tram from
Heaven.
Launceston, in the slow-going isle of Tassy, is
still discussing the proposed establishment of a
tramway system. The question is mostly one as
between municipal and private ownership, and
long years of strugglement have left the matter
still in the air, so to speak. In 1906 a plebiscite
of citizens resulted in 493 votes being cast for
municipal ownership, and 2248 for private owner-
ship, and the majority was so overwhelming that
the matter seemed to be settled. But the first
private syndicate which undertook to do the work
fell through, after the manner of syndicates, and
time rolled on, and Launceston was left still
tramless. Now another syndicate is cruising
round the proposition, but a fear seems to have
arisen in the bowels of the Tory press that all this
delay and muddle niay have led to a revulsion of
feeling in favor of municipal control. Conse-
quently the local Examiner arose, on January 16,
to reassure the public in an enthusiastic article.
After supplying many figures about the proposed
syndicate, it made this remarkable statement: —
We have expressed a favorable opinion of the scheme
because it provides the citizens with what they want
under reasonable conditions. Should the company work
the trams to the end of the 30 years—and we are told it
has never yet defaulted—then the sinking fund will be
sufficient to redeem the debentures, and the installation
becomes the property of the citizens without any cost.
That is the position of the Melbourne corporation, which
in a few years will acquire its tram system, built on a
similar principle, without havingto part with a sixpence.
The last seven words suggest a really astonishing
attitude of mind. The statement, taken in a
large, general way, is a magnificent lie, though
viewed in a small, particular fashion there maybe
shreds of truth about it. The Melbourne trams
do revert to the municipality in 1916. And
then any Melbourne citizen who has never ridden
in the trains, if such a person exists, may own a
share in the property without having paid
a sixpence. Also the citizen who is an in-
veterate tram-rider, but who always goes alone,
and never by any chance takes two girls
with him and pays for the three—he won’t have
paid a sixpence either; at least not in one lot.
But an enormous number of Melbourne citizens
pay, and have paid most of their lives, 4d. a day as
the price of Private Enterprise—that is to say,
that twice a day they pay 3d. to the Melbourne
Private Enterprise tramway extortioners for a
ride which the Sydney State-owned trams supply
for a humble copper. It is so glaringly obvious
that something of this sort must occur that even a
blind asylum could see it, though the Launceston
Examiner apparently can’t. In the first place
the Sydney State-owned tramways are burdened
with a payment of about 3J per cent, on the cost of
construction, that being the interest on the loan
money with which they were built; the Mel-
bourne Private Enterprise trams have to pay about.
24 per cent, on the cost of construction, that being
the dividend on its actually paid-up capital to
which Private Enterprise kindly helps it-
self. Then Private Enterprise doesn’t in-
tend to lose its capital when it hands over
the tramways to the municipalities in 1916 ;
instead it is accumulating such reserves that, when
the trams are handed over and the company
winds up, the shareholders will probably get 30s.
for every £1 they put into the concern—this in
addition to the splendid dividends that have come
their way. Now, as the Melbourne Tramway Co.
has no power to levy a poll-tax on the people of
Jerusalem, or to charge customs duties in Turkey
—as it can’t, in fact, tax anybody save the citizens
of Melbourne—it is plain that the citizens of Mel-
bourne will pay for the trams to the last penny if
they don’t pay for them twice over, and that they
are also, while the company still owns the trams,
paying about 24 per cent, per annum on the
capital invested in the business, whereas under
State or municipal ownership they need never pay
more than 4 per cent. The Launceston Exami-
ner’s picture of a city getting a complete tram-
way system without paying a sixpence for it, all
through the benevolence of Private Enterprise—
having the affair dropped on it like the gentle '
tram from Heaven, so to speak—is very beautiful,
but it is also very thin. The sixpences which
Melbourne has been needlessly and foolishly done
out of through its Private Enterprise tramway
policy are so many that the company’s rolling
stock would hardly be strong enough to carry
them.
Tiie British Freetrader was wont, till quite re-
cently, when his attention was drawn to the
levanting manufacturing industries of his country,
to fall back on the aged gag about the United
Kingdom being “the world’s sea-carrier,” and to
say that the prosperity of the Cold Land’s
delivery-of-goods-by-ship trade was “amazing”
and “unparalleled,” if nothing else was. A
Reuter’s message (16/l/’O9) suggests powerfully
that, under the Cobden regime, even this avenue
of profit is getting moss-grown and weedy and
generally blocked up. Said Reuter :
Sir Walter Runciman, senior partner of the firm of
Walter Runciman and Co., shipowners, of Newcastle-
on-Tyne, who was chairman of the meeting, stated that
nearly 2,000,000 tons of shipping were idle, and that
dividends had dropped nearly £30,000,000.
Another speaker said that shipowners were short of a
living freight by £23,500,000.
It is not that they are building too many ships in
Britain. On the contrary, month by month the
tonnage under construction in the British Isles
grows less and less. Last year’s total was, accord-
ing to Lloyd’s, Register, 733,37B tons—the lowest
recorded for over a decade. The previous year’s
total was 1,080,378 tons, a difference of 347,000
tons. The main trouble is that Britain is manu-
facturing less goods for export, and that the
foreign protected countries are not only making
more goods, but are also building the ships to
carry them in. It is a state of affairs that is con-
trary to all the rules laid down by Cobden half-a-
century ago. In those golden days of skite Cob-
den assured the British public that it was as cer-
tain as next day’s sunrise that within ten years
all the civilised nations of the world would be
Freetrade. And there Cobby fell in horribly—in
fact, his ghost has never got out of the hole to this
day.
One reason why the Australian postal department
is short of money is its excessive desire to supply
the public with cheap services. It began this
system in a misguided effort to disarm criticism
at the time when it was the fashion among all the
State Fright papers to denounce at large unless
the Commonwealth gave twice the service that
the States used to do, and charged only half the
money. Now the State Fright papers are wailing
a different tune, hence it came to pass the other
day that Sydney Evening News published this
comparison of telephone charges per annum (for a
’phone not more than two miles from the ex-
change, one instrument, 2000 outward calls per
annum, and all inward calls free): —
Philadelphia .. .. .. £24 15 0
New York 21 16 0
Boston .. .. .. .. 21 15 0
Baltimore .. .. . _ 20 10 0
St. Louis .. .. .. 19 5 0
Chicago.. .. .. .. 16 16 0
London .. ~ .. .. 13 6 8
Germany .. .. .. 9 10 0
Australia .. .. .. 5 0 0
No wonder the, Australian Postal Department is
feeling at the bottom of its pocket for loose change
to build extensions with.
“Mate” : I hope The Bulletin is keeping its
eye on the appointment of officers to the Com-
monwealth Navy. One of the conditions is Aus-
tralian residence, but according to report this
condition is not being observed. We would be
hard put to it to get Australians, because so few
of them go to sea. This is a pity, seeing how
dependant we are on the coaster fleet, but it is
not to be wondered at when you consider the hard
conditions and poor prospects of sailorising. How-
ever, there are plenty of men on the coast ships,
good Australians at heart, and mostly married to
Australian girls, thoroughly up to date, and capa-
ble of taking a place in the Commonwealth Navy
right away. Yet the first appointments are going
to men who come out of deep-water ships from
England. These men may be efficient, and the
lines they belong to have more social glamor than
our coasting companies care about playing up to.
But they haven’t the Australian sentiment, nor the
close knowledge of Australian conditions that are
as necessary as a year’s training in the British
Navy and the latest accent. I think it very hard
that our coasting men—who haven’t time in the
rush of the Australian trade to cultivate the
powers that be—should be passed over for out-
siders whose fathers happened to be friendly with
authority in their youth, or whose brass buttons
caught the eye of Mrs. or Miss Authority when
they went “’Ome.”

Silver Medal for brass castings.

AUSTRALIAN
MEDICAL
HOUSEHOLD
LIBRARY.
There are people in this country who have no doctor within a
hundred miles of them . Even in the places that are reckoned
fairly settled the lack of medical assistance is most appalling .
THE BULLETIN places these volumes on the market with the special idea of meeting the needs of those who
are out of the reach of medical assistance.
M ldioal Needs of Port Stephens. In connection with the lack of facilities for obtaining medical aid at Port Stephens, Mr. W. Moore,
a resident of Nelson s Bay, writes : In the whole of this large area there is no doctor nearer than Stockton, about 30 miles
distant, and the fee charged for attending a patient is in addition to which the hire of a steam launch has to be added,
making it £l3 a visit. In one instance lately, the child of a fisherman was taken seriously ill. The doctor attended, and
next day had again to be summoned to visit the child’s mother, making the fees and travelling expenses for two short
visits, which seems a large sum for a fisherman to pay. Others in need of medical aid are quite unable to afford it, and it
certainly seems high time for tile Government to take steps in the matter.— Sydney Morning Herald, October 5, 1908.
THE AUSTRALIAN HOUSEHOLD MEDICAL LIBRARY
IS THE NEXT BEST THING TO HAVING A DOCTOR ON THE TELEPHONE.
READ THESE PARTICULARS.
THE ILLUSTRATED AUSTRALIAN MEDICAL GUIDE (2 vols.)

separate section for women, over 1000 pages, bound half morocco, stiff board, gilt lettered* Published at £3 3s*

THE FEEDING AND MANAGEMENT OF AUSTRALIAN INFANTS IN HEALTH AND DISEASE,

ADVANCE DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department offers most advantageous terms to settlers or intending settlers on the land, a few of
* the important features being :
(a) Advances made from £5O to £2OOO. ( d ) Loan instalments (interest and principal)
(b) Low AND FIXED rate of Interest during are payable half-yearly.
currency of loan. (e) Borrower has the right to repay the loan
(c) Money may be lent for any term up to on any instalment date ; interest to that
31 years. date on, y being charged.
The tenures upon which the Advance Department may lend are : — (l) Freeholds, (2) Certificated Con-
ditional Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (3) Uncertificated Conditional
Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (4) Homestead Selections (before or after
issue of the Homestead Crant), (5) Settlement Leases, (6) Settlement Purchases, (7) Conditional
Purchase Leases. SAVINGS BANK DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department makes advances up to £25,000 upon city and suburban freeholds and on country lands well
situated and of good quality at moderate rates of interest.

you. You are trying one medicine after the other with the same disgusting disappoint-

The dormant commission which, provided
there is no Lieutenant-Governor, enables the head
of N.S.W. judicature to act in a large vice-regal
manner during the Governor’s absence from the
State, was the cause of a notable shindy in the
days of Hercules Robinson. Hercules the horsey
went off with Joseph George Innes to annex Fiji,
leaving Parkes Premier and Martin Chief Justice.
Adso The left Mrs. Robinson at Government
House. The Governor had barely got outside
the Heads when Martin smilingly (and, though
a bit of a bully, Martin could smile a fasci-
nating smile on occasions) interviewed Parkes and
asked him to wake up the dormant commission,
as he and his wife had packed a few things to
take with them to Government House. Nothing
in life gave Parkes more joy than disappointing a
Society climber, so he made a slight delay and
took counsel with Mrs. Robinson. What passed
between them is unknown, but it is on record that
Parkes came out with his mane waving in the
breeze and a lot of joy in his wicked little
eyes. Then Mrs. Robinson declared that
if the Martins came she would go—out into
the night, over the sea, to England ; nay, to
Ireland. The Martins didn’t go to Government
House, and with all possible speed the Colonial
Office sent out a commission appointing ex-Chief
Justice Stephen Lieutenant-Governor, which office
he held all through the life of Martin.

Our Unfinished Commonwealth
(r
V=-- .- •
HE amount of aid which Australia can expect from the Motherland is necessarily limited, and Australia
must be largely dependent upon herself for defence.” —Lord Northcote, in London.
The popular impression that the Commonwealth owns the whole of the Continent of Australia is
quite wrong. One-sixth of it is really an empty land protected only by a flag, and, in the words of
S.A. Premier Price, “a fluttering flag does not constitute effective occupation.” For the safety of the
Australian Nation the whole Continent must be acquired, and the empty spaces garrisoned. In this
matter Australia stands alone, and the times are dangerous.
Read “Why” and “How,” by C. A. Jeffries, commencing in this February LONE HAND.


IN BANANA LAND
“Moorabinda”: Touching on the recent
pars, re alligators. The Fitzroy (Q.) is (or was
years ago) the most southern water in Australia
in which these brutes are (or were years ago)
found, but from there up to Cape York the rivers
and creeks are (or were years ago) full of them.
Why they are called alligators no one knows, for
the formation of their jaws and the shape of their
heads distinctly prove them to be crocodiles.
They have a great fancy for dogs in the way of
food when they can get them, but their diet ex-
tends over a varied range, from a full-grown
cow to a paving stone. On one of the
plantations on the Pioneer an alligator was
seen to perform a feat which gives some
idea of the enormous strength which these brutes
possess. The milking cows belonging to the
plantation used to go down every morning to the
river to drink. The bank was rather steep, and
the water just there deepened very quickly. As
one of the cows was standing drinking, with her
forelegs in the water, an alligator came up and
caught her by the nose, and, in spite of the
animal’s struggles, held firmly on, and succeeded
m dragging her down into the damp, dark
depths. The cow was never seen again.
With regard to the paving stones, no one
seems to know whether they are taken
in for ballast or to assist digestion, or
to fill a vacufim caused by hunger ; but it is a very
common thing to find half-a-dozen stones, each
double the size of a man’s fist, in the stomach of
an alligator. The ’gators on the Pioneer generally
grow to an enormous size. One killed there not
long after I arrived, measured 19ft. long, but even
this was eclipsed by “ Big Ben,” of the Fitzroy •
he measured 23£ft. When last 1 heard of him he
was in the possession of Mr. Jamrach, of London.
These alligators do not seem to increase much in
numbers, and they hang about the same pools for
years. From October to March, during the hot
season, they do not show themselves at all but
during the rest of the year they lie about on
the sandbanks all day, sunning themselves.
“ Myrtle,” from Queensland ;
When Bananaland Treasurer Hawthorne put through the
last bunch of estimates, there was omitted the usual vote
of £lO,OOO as a reward for the person who discovers a per-
manent eradicator of prickly-pear. Though there are
plenty of advertised remedies, all declared fit and willing
to Johnsonise the spiny curse in less than 14 rounds,
no one has yet claimed that £lO,OOO, and things are just
where they used to be—excepting the pear, which is in
many places now where it used not to be.
Can any one tell me a place where the wretched thing
won’t grow? I was recently camped for a month on
North Keppel Island, and one day a sudden spasm of
energy, due to oysters and ozone, induced me to climb to
the culminating point of the mountains there. The ulti-
mate roof of things was a high peak, exposed to the direct
force of a full Pacific wind and storm, and (barring one
thing) it was as bald on top as a Tory legislator. The bald-
ness (barring one thing) was about half a mile square.
Centuries of gales had even dragged out the grass roots.
But there, in all its spreading glory, the sole specimen of
plant life on the lofty premises, grew one big clump of
prickly pear. And this island is 13 miles from the main-
land.
In Central Queensland the cactus curse is getting a
hefty grip on the land. I back North Rockhampton
against all-comers as a curse-infested spot, but the porcu-
pine vegetable is spreading all over the fertile valley of
the Fitzroy, and holding on to the ground like an
octopus. Yet 1 know several places where the pear is
cultivated (some people would cultivate the Devil) as a
substitute for a fence.
Of course, every year the various shire councils issue
circulars to landowners, ordering them to remove the
herbal beast, or it will be removed by the council at the
owners’ expense. The owners do nothing. And the
council sits back in its chair and does nothing. Only the
pear does something ; it grows.
The Government will have to Do Things or people will
shortly have to wear india-rubber pants and walk on stilts
even to buy their beer. It is a big job, but if any alleged
strong man wishes to fling his name into posterity, there
isn’t any need for him to wait till the job gets bigger—it’s
big enough.
Meanwhile, the £lO,OOO reward is withdrawn.
I am going- to form an Anti-Cactus Society, and get
myself appointed secretary at £4OO a year.

RAFFERTY : “ No, Hogan, I will NOT foight yez. As it is, Oi know meself to be th' better
