Sheep King Jowett, of Longreach (Q.),
will this year shear 500,000 sheep, and as he has
just acquired some new properties, it is expected
this number will be doubled in two or three years.
As Sam McCaughey has eased off somewhat in
flock accumulation during recent years, Jowett
looks like heading the poll for Australia. During
the past few years Longreach has been adding
considerably to its flocks of woolly balls, and it
reckons it now has some claims to the honor and
dignity of being Australia’s biggest wool centre.
“Itan”: Kidston always was autocratic, but
since his trip to Fogland he is more so than ever.
Now he practically rules every Department. To
maintain his post he will be obliged this year to
do something more than shout for State Frights ;
so he intends bidding tremendously for popularity
by making himself the central buzzing figure in
the Jubilee Celebrations—the chief effort of which
will be a glorified Cow and Pumpkin Show, under
the moulting wing of the National Ass. at Bris-
bane. Kidston’s strength now lies in his capacity
to manoeuvre, and to create issues, and to invent
political kites; and he sees in this big-pumpkin
show a chance to pull various nuts out of the fire.
That eminent grass-and-bullock pioneer,
William Collins, who lately passed out, was asso-
ciated with the local beef-producing animal almost
ever since Bananaland possessed such an animal.
As early as ’63 Collins, then a young man of
helped to found Westgrove station, on the Daw-
son. In ’7B he became a partner with the late
Thomas Mcll wraith and with W. Forrest, under
the banner of the North Australian Pastoral Co.,
which acquired broad miles of country in the
Northern Territory and in Queensland. Ten
years later he was associated with Mcllwraith,
McEacharn and Co. in the despatch of the first
successful shipment of frozen beef and mutton
sent from Australia to England. And having
thus got fairly started he said that the business
was good, and he pursued the sheep and the ox all
tiie rest of his days.
Pay Day on the Mine.
(Being a cry from Cloncurry or thereabouts.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OH the bottles get a rubbing,
And the shelves get dusted down ;
The counter gets a scrubbing,
And the boss forgets to frown ;
Oh the barrel in the cellar,
And the whisky and the wine
And the jug are all replenished
When it’s pay day on the mine.
The barmaid is embellished
In extra special style,
And the housemaid does her darnedest
The piano to beguile ;
The missus lets the fire out
No one has time to dine—
For the beer pump keeps ’em busy
When it’s pay day on the mine. '
In the parlor there is dancing,
And there’s fighting in the rear ;
There is skiting and romancing,
And there’s murder in the beer.
There’s a solemn Jimmy Woodser
In the corner by the door
And a bloke who’s playing patience
With the fantods on the floor.
A stranger’s in the parlor
Who is silent and aloof,
The boss makes observations
Re “ a shingle off the roof.”
And that shingle off the ceiling
Causes bitter grief and pain
Till the stranger asks the missus
If she'll “fill ’em up again.”
Then the skiters and the fighters
Take the stranger by the hand,
And solemnly assure him
He’s “the whitest in the land !”
And the barmaid, and the housemaid,
And the missus and the rest,
Confidingly assure him
That he is “ the very best.”
Then the stranger grabs the missus,
And the housemaid grabs the boss,
And they dance away like blazes
Till the housemaid goes to doss ;
Then a little more refreshment,
And the stranger goes to bed,
More in sorrow than in anger,
With a towel round his head.
When the shift boss comes in later,
With the bobby by his side,
You will see the missus cater
For their custom at the slide.
And the John Hop’s bland expression
Is a pleasant thing to see,
When the missus shoves the coin back
For “You’re having this with me ! ”
Then another and another—
And the bobby after that
Leaves the shift boss and the missus
In the parlor on their pat.
The boss is not a jealous man,
He’s white unto the bone,
And by-and-bye he knows he’ll have
The barmaid on his own.
So he elevates the deaders
And the dying to their feet,
And conducts them to the woodheap,
Or the footpath, or the street.
He clears the bar and bangs the door,
And soon there’s not a sign
Or a sound to tell a stranger
It was pay day on the mine.
N.S.W.
TOM BEASLET.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP
Brisbane, January 30, 1909.
My Dear Tii a rgomindah,
Persistent report has it that Federal Chief-
Justice Griffith will shortly throw in the judicial
towel, and settle down at beautiful jVLerthyr,
where he will perhaps translate another Iliad.
There has been marrying and giving in marriage.
The only daughter of P.M. Boyce, of Townsville,
and one Mack—of your State—were using the
altar on a recent Wednesday ; and the same sun
shone down on the joining of the second Need-
ham damsel, the bud of F. H. Needham, of Can-
ning Downs, which eventuated with much ivory
satin cut plain to severity, and a wealth of Limerick
lace veil. Oh, yes ! and a bridegroom named
Beaumont Rumble. The bridesmaids were Miss
Philips (grown up) and three girleens belonging
to the Moncrieff Scotts and the Bells, all wearing
a variety of muslin confections and a heap of
bridegroom’s gifts.
A third tying of note was that of Willie Molle
(he who, in days gone by, won a champion aquatic
event for Queensland, merely by diving overboard
when his oar snapped, and lightening the ship,
a la Jonah) and the pretty daughter of bookseller
Robertson, of Toowoomba. Claude Musson, for
the sixty-ninth time, did nothing behind the
agitated groom. The guests didn’t clear off like
the ordinary variety and leave the bride
and her strange husband to escape into
the wilds. No, they saw to it that the
young couple came to no harm. They packed the
two into the mail train and brought them back to
town with them, and, by the time the metropolis
was reached, they had become so fond of them
that it was with difficulty Mr. Molle extracted his
bride from among them and got her safely into the
maternal residence at New Farm, lent for the
occasion.
“All celebrities have their peculiarities,” we
are told, and, therefore, when a genius turns up
to dine without his tie, we merely murmur that
the sacred fire is burning strongly within him, and
order up the fish. Such a case happened here
during the recent conference. A prominent
visiting professor was asked to dine and sleep at
the house of a brother in learning, and cheerfully
accepted the invitation. Aware of his absent-
mindedness, his host had some misgivings as to his
turning up. He arrived on time, however, but
quite without luggage. As a silver-tailed party
had been captured to meet him, the hostess was
somewhat agitated at his lack of evening clothes,
and being also a kindly soul, was even more
moved to pity for his scarcity of pyjamas. “Never
mind his clothes,” counselled the husband, “ they
won’t mind old W.” But the motherly heart
would not be soothed until hubby had promised
to remember to send along to the guest’s room a
suit of night attire. Behold the amazement then
of host and hostess when the noted one-arrived
in the dining room in all the bravery
of dress suit, blameless tie, handkerchief,
and collar. The evening was long and merry, but
at length the last man had gone, and the owner
turned up with the said sleeping things. As he
opened the door, with a murmured apology, and
poked in a night-shirt, there was the man of learn-
ing in his pyjamas. Then questions were asked
with great violence. “Well, you see,” replied
the wise prophet, “ I knew that I should lose the
wretched bag, so I just put on my pyjamas and
then my dinner things, and a tweed coat and
pants over them again, and it answered finely.”
And the thermometer stood at 89° !
Home-Secretary Joey Bell’s stepdaughter No.
2 is about the limit at writing stories for the
young. She has again been awarded the juvenile
prize in the Australasian this year. It makes
her fourth successful attempt, and she has only
been a trier four times.
The death of Speaker Leahy has given rise to
no end of conjecture as to the filling of the billet.
It might seriously alter the views of ex-Premier
Philp upon the retirement business. It might
provide a way out for the William person and the
Tolmie Downs push. It might with tact be turned
to account over the Young Queensland party ;
but, then, where is the tact tQ come from ? Any-
how, it will mean the healing of one sore heart at
least —that of the next Speaker.
Her Excellency arose from her couch on Mon-
day, and departed for the Toowoomba heights
once more. Mrs. Rev. Hone, who was the lady
in charge of the sick room (a cheerful blonde 6ft.
high), has returned to hubby in the wilds of
Beaudesert.
Our champion amateur piano banger (only she
wheedles rather than bullies the instrument), Elaine
Feez, returns to the Bull country, I hear, in
March to further pursue the by-paths of the key-
board. We have here another little piano pur-
suer, Florence Michaeli, who would repay with
interest a few years’ study among the music centres
of Europe, but alas ! the funds are lacking. The
great Mark Hambourg much fancied her work.
We have dealt another blow to the atom MAN.
Hitherto he has reigned supreme in the profes-
sional line of the Government service ; but Miss
Muriel Isabel Gregory, offspring of the recent
Government Printer, has now wrecked his pres-
tige by getting herself appointed a draftsman
in the Survey Qffice. Six months ago she went
up for an exam, with about 20 males, and the
males don’t know yet what struck them.
The president of the National Council of Women
has been in gaol. That is, she has been having a
look round, just in case, you know, and has found
that King Edward’s boarding establishment isn’t
such a bad crib after all. Personally I always
rather envy the appointments. There are no
marble baths where I doss, and the clocks don’t
keep anything like good time. However, there
are compensations. The hours of rising and retir-
ing are absurd at Boggo-road, and if I were a
boarder I should take out all my spare time
writing letters to the papers about it, and about
the unbecoming ideas that prevail there on the
matter of dress.
A new discovery is to the effect that our Gover-
nor has a German streak in him. In the very
remote ages it seems that a certain German pastor
had a difference of a doctrinal nature with
the Fatherland, and in consequence he shook the
sausages from his beard and crossed the Channel
to England. His name at that period was
something like Schlessingbacherbockstein, but
the British folk couldn’t get their teeth into
that. Somehow they called him Thessinge,
which later toned down to Thesiger, and there
you are. This probably accounts for the mid-
summer madness that led our Governor over the
water to take part m the Anniversary celebra-
tions to the glory of William the Sudden at the
Turn Yerein on Wednesday evening. Another
guest of the occasion was the Kidstocrat. He
didn’t seem to enjoy himself somehow.
They have had to get a special padlock made
for the big State motor in which the only Kidsto-
erat takes his daily airings. You see, it is stabled
in a convenient spot near the main entrance to the
Executive buildings, and every blessed man that
wandered there used to open the door, seat him-
self inside, pull out the travelling desk, open the
cupboard (where the whisky ought to be), and
telephone to the absent driver. In short, he used
to behave for one grand moment like the King of
Queensland. But this encouraged ambition that
was apt to become unhealthy, besides which it
wore out the car, so now the vehicle is strictly
tied up.
As I write a murmur reaches me to the effect
that the Kidston family is daily wandering round
in the car looking at desirable villa residences in
New Farm. This all comes of taking to the Mary
Ann habit, which leads to high-priced abodes and
society. Apropos , William and his wife are run-
ning an At Home on the Lucinda on Saturday.
(Guests must be prepared to shake hands with
all and sundry, including the Scotch tyke, before
venturing on board.)
There is sorrow among the Pioneer Clubbers in
this sad city. Miss Edith Dickson, by whose
efforts the cat concern has been kept going, has
announced her intention of removing her small
person England wards, and there is grave fear that
without her warmth of enthusiasm the frost may
become overwhelming. Wanted —oh! so badly—
a secretary with tact, and a few really interested
members.
Col. Foxton, Federal M.P. for Brisbane, is
going into the Pooh Bah business. Not alone is he
called upon to uphold his Parliamentary dignity,
but they have elected him president of the Cricket
Board of Control, and acting Military Comman-
dant during the furlough of the Lyster family.
He will thus be forced to be continually changing
his cricket togs for a court dress, only to jump out
cf that into his puttees and helmet of military
splendor. Meanwhile the Lysters wander in the
shadow of the Pyramids.
There was consternation on Thursday when it
leaked out that a real live Earl on board the
Manuka from ’Frisco had been in the bay, and
had escaped without one of our girls setting an
eye upon him. This is the second time we have
been done brown in the Earl line, the first escapee
being the Gov.-General.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
Owing to the comparatively high values of
pastoral properties, Kidston’s finances will get a
good lift from the estates of Speaker John Leahy
and William Collins, of Nindooimbah. Both
estates will pay fat death duties; Collins’ will
probably be the fattest since Tyson’s £60,000
made a futile attempt to prevent a Philp deficit.
Collins and his brother, R.M., owned or controlled
a million or two acres in Northern, Central and
Southern Queensland, including much freehold in
the well-watered South-East. A drought never
at any time extends throughout the whole of
Queensland, hence they accumulated wealth un-
interruptedly. J ohn Leahy was manager of a big
squatting concern, and the political agent and
organiser of the squattocracy. He and Philp
were jointly interested in pastoral property ; in
fact, it was to Leahy that Philp owed a good deal
of his second big financial lift.
“ The Australian Infant is the best possible
Immigrant for Australia ” has now passed into a truism.
But, unfortunately, through lack of proper training, the
average mother doesn’t know how to treat her first infant
when he arrives. In the Commonwealth and Maoriland,
during the 22 years from 1884 Co 1905 inclusive, no less
than 341,578 infants (42 daily) have died. Of these deaths
probably 170,000 could have been prevented. The pioneer
baby of a family has a hard row to hoe. He is the victim
of his mother’s ignorance, and by the mistakes she makes
with him she learns how to bring up his future brothers
and sisters. Much has been said about the iniquity of
the elder child getting the bulk of the property', but he
has earned it and paid for it too. Consider how he has
to suffer for the benefit of the younger ones. Every
woman should be instructed in the rearing of babies, but
till this desirable state of affairs comes about, the best
thing the intending mother can do is to read up the sub-
ject ; and there is no better work than “ The Feeding
and Management of Australian Infants in Health ami
Disease,” by Dr. P. E. Muskett, now running through its
seventh edition, and sold from The Bulletin Office in con-
junction with “The Australian Medical Guide” and
“ Thq of Health,” for £3 post free.*


CLASSES WHO SHOULD COME
TO QUEENSLAND.
Farmers.
Agricultural Laborers.
Men accustomed to Live Stock.
Miners.
Domestic Servants.
Men, with small capital, accustomed to out-
door life.
Men, without capital, not afraid of hard work.
Young men, without experience, who are willing
to take employment whilst they learn the
methods of work in Queensland.
Any steady energetic individual from the above
classes should have no difficulty in earning a good
livelihood and in making a comfortable home in
Queensland.

Queensland of To-day: the Queen

No. V.
MORE ABOUT CLIMATE.
By J. D. FITZGERALD.
Take, for example, the Darling Downs district
of Southern Queensland—“ the garden of Aus-
tralia,” as it has been well called. Here, where
the land space is not yet exhausted, where there
FLORENCE SCHMIDT.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney & Melbourne.
is still ample room for the small farmer, and for the
agricultural laborer (especially for him, with the
chance of his developing, after experience
acquired, into a thrifty small farmer him-
self) —here is a great tableland 2000 ft. above
sea-level, swept always by the breezes from the
north, south, east, and west, monsoonal and trade.
On this tableland the welcome settler will have
the finest climate in the world. It has been com-
pared to the Riviera, but it is the Riviera climate
without Black Auster and the Mediterranean
hot winds, sea-borne from North Africa.
Climate is proved by the products of the soil. All
the fruits, flowers, vegetables, and cereals of the
semi-tropical and temperate zones flourish exceed-
ingly on this vast, black-and-chocolate-soiled
plateau. The mean annual temperature on this
glorious spot is 61 degrees ; and the nights are in-
variably cool. Upon the rolling downs, undulat-
ing hills, and timbered ridges rare frosts may
occur in winter, but snow has only been seen there
twice within the memory of the oldest settler, and
snow in Australia is an affair of 24 hours, not of
months, as in Canada or Great Britain. These
downs are Devonshire without the snow, Kent
BRUNTON STEPHENS.
with perpetual sunshine, Italy without its July
heat.
And these are but a sample of the tablelands of
Queensland. Central Queensland’s climate has
been rightly described as “genial and health-
giving.” Of the latter quality the present writer
can give direct testimony from personal experi-
ence as already described” West of Rockhampton
there are the same characteristics, perhaps a
little accentuated, as were found in the Darling
Downs. On the tablelands, and even in the
coastal districts, you will find the fruit of tem-
perate zones side by side in the same garden with
those of the tropics—the pear, the peach, the
cherry, plum and apricot, even the strawberry—
alongside the paw-paw, the granadilla, the guava,
mango, the cocoanut and the custard apple.
What other climate in the world can stand that
test? I must quote one writer on the subject of
climate from the practical point of view: “To
the farming settler, especially, this question of
climate is a very important one. In other
countries, with more temperate climates and sub-
COASTAL SCENERY.—A SURF BATHERS' PARADISE.
ject to frost and snow in winter, there are many
disadvantages in connection with farming opera-
tions. In Queensland, cattle require little or no
protection in the winter season, and the land is
accessible for grazing and cultivation all the year
round, instead of being, as in some countries,
covered with snow and ice during several months
of the year.”
In Northern Queensland the tropical heat has
its advantages. It secures a steady and adequate
rainfall—a regular rainy season—and thus makes
Queensland less subject to droughts than some of
the Southern States. The general characteristics
of the climate throughout are (1) an even tem-
perature on the coast, without a very great
variation throughout the year ; (2) the climate of
the tablelands, with a wider range of temperature
NELLIE MELBA, THE WORLD'S GREATEST SOPRANO, WHO LIVED
FOR YEARS IN QUEENSLAND.
(Her memoirs are now running through THE LONE HAND.)
between summer heat and winter cold ; (3) the
western climate, with greater extremes of both
heat and cold, but with a siccity and a sparkling
clearness in the air which make for physical
energy in the individual, contrasting in the minds
of those who have experienced it with the lassi-
tude produced by the climates of the countries of
Southern Europe, which are described conven-
tionally as “Sunny Italy ” and “ Sunny Spain.”
And again, it must be remembered, in considering
the important question of climate, that, with a
plenteous rainfall, the fine days average about 255
in the year.
Again, it must be recognised that, after all, the
death-rate is the best test of salubrity of climate.
And the average death-rate of Queensland is
possibly the lowest in the world—certainly the
lowest of all the Australasian States —a death-rate
of but 9'56 per 1000 of the population ; and the ex-
pectation of child-life is vastly greater than in any
other country in the world. As is generally
known, the crucial period in the life of the indi-
vidual is during the first year of life. The wisest
efforts of the municipal statesmen and the hygienic
experts of Europe are exercised in the task of
grappling with the problem of preserving the
child during the first year. Depdts are provided
in large towns to supply free milk, pasteurised or
“humanised,” for the' use of the infants whose
mothers, victims of slum conditions, perhaps, are
unable to feed them from the breast. Nurses are
employed by the great municipalities to teach the
mothers the difficult art of mothering their own
children. Prizes are given to those mothers who
have safely brought their babies through the
perils of the first year of life. What a tribute,
then, to the climate of Queensland that in her
genial climate the child is immune from most of
the ills of this perilous first year ! For this is the
case. The death rate for the first year of infant
life in Queensland is very low.
A few comparisons will be instructive. The
infantile death-rate of Maoriland is the lowest
in the world, 68 per 1000. N.B.W. comes next
with 74’53 per 1000 in 1906, and Queensland was
at 74'68 in that year. England and Wales lost 128
per 1000 in 1905, France 144, Japan 151, and
Switzerland 129 per 1000. For the Common-
wealth, and taking the first year of infant life,
Queensland’s was by far the lowest death-rate,
being 1'94 per 1000, while N.S.W. was 2'04, Vic-
toria, 2'53; S.A., 2'02; W.A., 3 08 ; and Tas-
mania, 2'57. There could hardly be a better test
of the stamina of parent and child in the new
Queensland nation, nor a truer indication of the
virility which the climatic conditions of Queens-
land conduce to. This alone is an answer to those
who say that the benignant tropical climate of the
Northern State is not a “white man’s country.”
It is not only a white man’s, but is fit for virile
white women and sturdy white children.
Let us turn again to the people themselves as a
test of climatic influences. You may search the
world and not find a more stalwart people than
the native-born. As to the immigrants, we are
not responsible for their physique; that was pre-
determined before they reached our shores. We
may be sure that as only the most enterprising
would join the ranks of immigrants who come
across an immense stretch of sea, and endure
many hardships (now fortunately minimised by
splendid ships and governmental care and
thoughtfulness) to become Queenslanders, the
Northern State has secured the very creme de la
creme of the Old World races. Collateral evidence
of this, too, is available in two things—(l) the ripe
old ages to which the emigrants live; and (2) the
tall, wiry, and strong offspring —bold and inde-
pendent in their way of meeting the problems of
life ; democratic and free spoken in their politi-
cal views ; capable in dealing with the natural
conditions, and in conquering the difficulties
which confront the pioneer, even though he may
be “ native here and to the manner born.” The
longevity of the transplanted races is, surely, one
of the best signs which can betoken the salubrity
of the Queensland climate. The average shearer,
as a class, sons of yeoman farmers, well mounted,
splendid riders, would compare with the finest
English yeomen, the German Junckers, or the
members of the Roman Equestrian Order.
This Queensland race bids fair to develop cer-
tain special characteristics which will differen-
tiate it from other Australians. Fine athletes,
cricketers, footballers, oarsmen, expert bushmen,
adventurers in the best sense of the word, they
take their evolution into a distinct type from
their climatic environment. They are an outdoor
race, and therefore not subject to “ house ” dis-
eases. They exploited beach bathing long before
Southern Australians devoted themselves to it
with such passionate ardor. The signs of outdoor
life are upon them in their bronzed faces and lean
and wiry forms. And the women areevolvinga type
which is not unlike Spanish beauty—dark-haired,
passionate, home-loving, fond of freedom, bold in
their political opinions, recording their votes at
elections, and thus asserting their equality with
man ; but withal modest and faithful—good wives
and model mothers, not yet bitten by the craze
for dress and theatres and high living which ob-
sesses some of their Southern and their European
sisters. Those of the bush are daring horsewomen,
resourceful housewives, without whose loving aid
pioneering in Queensland would not have been
possible, and whose share in the wealth-producing
capacity of the nation cannot be over-estimated.
And with all this they are musical and artistic.
I*n art and literature the Queensland women are
known in the world. Nellie Melba took no harm
to her glorious voice from the years she lived in
the Mackay-Bowen district. Rather may we
assume that it needed only this touch of the
Queensland sun, which her residence gave her, to
instil that golden warmth into the vocal
liquescence of that lovely organ—a warmth that
has melted the hearts of millions of art-loving
people throughout the world. Then there was
that other of whom Roderic Quinn sang, the
girlish rival of Melba in her Queensland days,
who gave up the prospects of entrancing the great
world with glorious song, and having kings and
ESSEX EVANS.
Photo, by Poulsen, Brisbane.
queens at her feet, who surrendered a great
musical career for the noble duties of wife and
mother—she was a product of the climate of
Queensland. And last, but not least, so far
as singers are concerned, the brilliant Florence
Schmidt (now Mrs. Derwent Wood), who con-
quered the difficult musical world of Europe with
one of the most glorious voices ever found in a
human throat. She came from Rockhampton—
a river town in which the climate is alleged by
those who have never lived there to be intolerable,
but which those who have sojourned there for a
year and have enjoyed the alternations of the
seasons, from the gloriously mild winter to the
tropical heat of summer, will defend before all
men. Florence Schmidt is a testimonial to the
health-giving qualities of Central Queensland’s
climate. For the air which nourished that beauti-
ful soprano voice must have something fragrant
and ambrosial in it.
Nor is the climate inconsistent with success in
literature. For in Queensland dwelt Mrs. Camp-
bell Praed, a novelist of note in London ; William
Archer, foremost of London’s dramatic and
literary critics (who, if I am not mistaken, belongs
to the Gracemere Archers, of Rockhampton); J.
Brunton Stephens, whose “ Convict Once ” and
lighter verses can be counted among the corner
stones of the great Australian literary temple
“P. LUFTIG" (PETER AIREY).
Photo, by Poulsen, Brisbane.
that is to be ; and Essex Evans, Francis Kenna,
and “P. Luftig” (Peter Airey), whose work is
known throughout Australia.
And if the would-be immigrant has not yet
fully divested himself of his prejudices with regard
to the climate, we may point to its benign
seductiveness in the winter season, when the
Tasmanian, Victorian, New South Welshman
and Maorilander, in company with the tourist
who has heard of its mild benignity in far-off
lands, go on the winter tour to Cairns or Thurs-
day Island in one of the splendid liners, coastal
and deep sea, which run that route in a lake-like
sea, with smooth water, shielded from the rough
waves of the Pacific by the coral buckler of the
Great Barrier Reef. Of this the reader will be
told later on. For the present, this much need
only be said, that those who have doubts of the
salubrity and the tolerableness of the Queens-
land climate, from Moreton to Thursday Island,
and from Rockhampton to Barcaldine, are not
those who have lived there. Such a criticism of
Queensland is a criticism founded on ignorance.

The Inky Way.
— .
“Westerner”: The absorption of the Perth Morning
Herald by the Daily News marks the passing of Stirling
Bros., one of the oldest newspaper firms in Australia.
Edward Stirling, the founder of the firm, issued the first
newspaper in the West in May, 1832, in the form of a
weekly manuscript journal, price 3s. 6d. per copy. The
first printed paper, the Perth Gazette, was issued the
following year (January, 1833) at 3s. per copy. This was
the forerunner of the present-day West Australian,
which thus ranks second to the Sydney Morning Herald
in seniority among existing Australian newspapers—Mr.
“ James’ ” paper being first issued (as the Sydney
Herald) in April, 1831. Stirling went out of the
newspaper business with the launching of the
Gazette, but returned in 1840 to found a
rival weekly, the Inquirer, which he conducted for
50 years. He handed it over to his four sons (John,
Frederick,’ Horace and Lewis Stirling) in 1890. The Daily
News, an evening offshoot, was issued in 1881, the In-
quirer remaining a weekly. The Morning Herald was
launched by the same firm in 1896, Arthur Lovekin (now
proprietor, editor, manager and general Pooh Bah of the
Daily News) being first editor. W. F. Forster, who suc-
ceeded Lovekin in 1900, made a scoop by the alleged
discovery of the original of Boldrewood’s “ Captain Star-
light ” in the person of an old W.A. identity; and for a
term the paper boomed. Shortly after Forster trekked
East to find wider scope for his talents, and took a posi-
tion on the Age. A meteoric succession of editors fol-
lowed, until finally Jack Drayton, an old-time comrade
of “ Smiler ” Hales on the g-oldfields press, took charge
and ran the paper until its demise last week. Bishop Gibney
bought a controlling interest in the Herald a couple of
years ago, and practically conducted it from the R.O.
“ Bishop’s Palace.” The good Bishop made an attempt
to run the paper on reform lines, excluding all sporting
and betting news and advertisements ; but the circula-
tion went down with such a thump that the “ reforms ”
were hastily dropped. The Herald has always been run
in the Liberal interest, and its suspension leaves Perth
with a very poor choice between the stodgy West Aus-
tralian and the sensational, not to say shrieking, Daily
News.
• * * *
Died, the other day, aged 80 and a good bit, William
Hay, who had the job of buying and setting up the first
machinery for the Adelaide Advertiser ; and the Adver-
tiser was aged 50 last year. Hay was overseer when the
paper started, and had been in and out of the office a good
deal since then.
RUBAIYAT OF THE ROUNDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Awake! The noonday Sun has looped the loop ;
The Rounds await you—you must fly the coop !
The rival Journal, briefly scanned, has turned
Your heart to water with a column Scoop.
Though you have Space, yet cut your breakfast short,
’Tis time for picking up the City Court ;
For if unto the evening Rag you trust,
Twill ditch you with an Incomplete Report.
‘All dhrunks to-day,” the Watch-house Keeper saitli —
“ But stay, there’s wan good Case ” —you hold your
breath,
And pray to God that they have taken deps.—
“ Charge iv Assault—Young Woman Kicked to Death.”
Tis worth a good Half-Column, and you guess
You’ll get the details from the C.P.S. ;
But he has gone, and probably the Sub
Will say, “A Quarter —neither More nor Less.”
Your Luck stands good—the Plainclothes Man who gave
The Evidence is mighty still to save.
You take a Note ; then hey, the merry Morgue,
That half-way Hostel to the Pauper’s Grave !
Behold, an Inquest on. a Female Child 1.
“ Improper Feeding’’—hear the Nurse reviled !
It’s worth a Crosshead, but you’re pushed for time.
Why need the Coroner become so wild ?
For lack of Space, though Life was crowded out,
’Tis nothing new to make a Fuss about,
Respectability to scandalise,
Or staid Conventionality to flout.
There’s little else to cause you to abide—
“ Natural Causes ” and a Suicide ;
You wave the Orderly a fond farewell,
And swiftly to the City Hall you glide.
“Tar-Paving”— that’s a par., which having writ,
Upon the theme of “Dust” you spend your wit;
Behold herein your Day’s epitome—
To-morrow shall disperse each Word of it!
And now the City Clerk would fain forget
The long-deferred Report whereon you’re s.et;
You ask the same old Question once again—
“lt will be Soon,” he murmurs, “ but not Yet.”
Then to the Hospital at half-past three
You tramp in search of Death and Casualty—
The Swallowed Fishbone and the Fatal Fall,
The Draught of Match-heads and the Fractured Knee.
A moment’s halt —and then you make toward
The C.I. Branch, and there ring round the Board ;
You’re lucky if there’s “ Nothing for the Press”
And not a single Item to record.
Then “ Home, Sweet Home ” —but first your steps are
lured
To Eastern Hill, wherefrom may be secured
A Par about the Fire that swept away
Two Dwellings, Brick, One Storey, Uninsured.
’Tis Night; once more to Town your feet are bound,
To glean such News as haply may be found ;
Perchance a grim and gory Murder waits—
Perchance a Burglary : Ring Round, Ring Round !
Full to the Brim with Copy you return,
Wad upon Wad, for which the Printers yearn ;
When lo I “A Fire ! A Fire in Flinders Lane ! ”
“O H 1! ” you bellow, “ let the D d Thing Burn ! ”
But such a Bluff you know is all in vain ;
You’re there among the sparks that roar and rain ;
And when, all singed and drenched, you’re back at
Work,
The news comes in, “ It’s broken out again ! ”
Right here the Sub remarks, with eager Joy,
“ They’ve got a Murder out at North Fitzroy ” ;
Whereon the Shipping Man rings in to say,
“A Yacht’s capsized near No. 7 Buoy.”
The list of trains to Williamstown you scan,
Feeling as if your Brain were made of Bran ;
When tardily the Chief of Staff observes,
“ Perhaps you’d better take an Extra Man.”
Then, when at last your dripping Brow you wipe
(Your Murder Story on the Linotype),
The capsized Yacht’s collided with the Sub.,
Who yells, “ What made you write this blanky Tripe ? ”
A Hair perhaps divides the False and True—
And if you faked, what else were you to do'?
’Tis naught—the Sub. subsides—you thank your Stars,
Because the Sub. has been a Roundsman, too.
He knows it All—the Pitfall and the Gin
Waiting the Bluff that cannot always win ;
The Rope’s around his Neck as much as yours—
Pray then that he will let no Libel in 1
Forme after Forme is locked—each Proof is read ;
The Paper goes to Press—the Last Word’s said :
Three-seven-six is dumb ; ’tis Two a.m. ;
Let’s ring up one-ought-one, and get to Bed.

THIS Company is nov formed with the object of acquiring from William Goodall
Hearne, Chemist, Geelong, the RIGHT OF SALE of Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure,
Frootoids, and other of Hearne’s Medicines for Australia, and deriving all the
profits between the manufacturing price and the selling price of them ; and for
carrying on and extending the businesses as specified in the Memorandum of Association.
The purchase price of this Right of Sale for Australia of Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure,
Frootoids, and certain other of Hearne’s Medicines, which said Right of Sale will be
acquired as on and from the Ist day of February, 1909, by agreement dated the loth day
of December, 1908, is £12,500, to be satisfied by the issue to the said William Goodall
Hearne of Shares in the Company to the value of one-fifth part on the formation of the
Company and one-fifth part at the commencement of each of the four following years.
This agreement made between the said W. G. Hearne of the one part and W. G. Hearne
and Company Limited of the other part, or a copy thereof, can be inspected at the Regis-
tered Office of the Company, 127 Ryrie Street, Geelong, any day before the closing of the
subscription list, between the hours of 10 a.m. and 12 noon.
The Company being based on Co-operative Principles opens the door for splendid
possibilities, inasmuch as the Reputation achieved by these medicines is simply
Magnificent ; and the greater the increase in the sales of them, the larger will be the
Company’s profit available for Shareholders.
During the first five years, the said William Goodall Hearne guarantees payment of
the dividends to the Shareholders. As one evidence of the value of this guarantee, the
“fixed deposits ” (cash) at the guarantor’s credit in the various Banks of Issue named in
this prospectus amounts to £23,000.
The sales of Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure alone during the last three years amount to over
£89,000. The Profits of the business are considerably more than ample to provide for the
Dividends, and although only a nominal £lO per cent, per annum Dividend is guaranteed
on the Ordinary Shares, this should be immensely increased by the co-operation of those
who sell these medicines, and who now have the opportunity of becoming Shareholders and
participating in the Profits, because as a result of this co-operation a large proportion of
what is being spent in advertising can be saved and added to the Dividends.
Wide powers of operation and extension are secured by the Company in its Memorandum
of Association, so that the Business may be extended as shall be deemed advantageous.
This issue of Shares is limited to purchasers of Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure or any other of
the catalogue list of Hearne’s Medicines, and is intended to provide an opportunity of shar-
ing in the management, profits and benefits of further extension of business.
The preliminary expenses connected with the formation of the Company will be paid by
the said William Goodall Hearne.
The whole amount provided by this issue of Shares will be retained in the business of
the Company and devoted to its business purposes.
No Director will receive any remuneration for his services as such, but any Director
acting in the capacity of Manager, or otherwise employed on behalf of the Company, may
receive for services rendered in such capacity fair remuneration as may be decided on by
the Directors of the Company for the time being.
This issue has not been underwritten, and no commission or promotion money has been
paid, or is payable for subscriptions on Shares. /'
The minimum of subscriptions for Shares upon which the Directors will proceed to allot-
ment is 12,000.
Applications for Shares will proceed only on one of the forms of application referred
to below.
For the purposes of the Companies Act of Victoria, 1896, this advertisement does not
fully comply with the requirements of Section 104, and the public are referred to the full
prospectus, copies of which, with forms of application may be obtained at the registered
office of the Company, 127 Ryrie Street, Geelong, Victoria.
“ N.” writes :
M'Clurk’s, just to hand, has the story of the Vander-
bilts. The founder, the Commodore, was a fairly satis-
factory millionaire, if such a thing is possible, infinitely
more so than grasping, selfish, cruel Carnegie, who took
a fit and unloaded. Commodore Vanderbilt, when young,
pursued a business something like that of Melbourne’s
Pat Hanna, who worked a punting boat at Queen’s
Wharf, got rich, lived ; n a mansion, and became M.L.A.
While P. Hanna was“curiously out of tune at speech-
making, Commodore Vanderbilt was a distinctly valuable
public man, though self-centred. It is hardly credible
nowadays that it was once necessary to travel over seven
separate and disjointed railroads to get from New
York to Chicago, and drag baggage from one train to
another at every change. Vanderbilt created the through
service. To the end of his life the Commodore could
hardly spell a sentence correctly. He seems to have read
nothing but the Bible and the “ Pilgrim’s Progress.” His
ambition was to mop up the country for his own family.
His son William doubled the millions. Then they began
to fade away in branches of the family. The Vanderbilts
have sunk in the Capua of ease. There is no one of the
name in the first line.
“Where’s the Impertinent Clara lying?" he yelled at
the knot of wharf laborers outside the shed.
“She ain’t lying,” retorted a small man. “She’s
gorne.”
“Gone! She can’t be gone ! ”
“ But I tell yer she is ! She’s just pulled out from ther
wharf.”
“ Well, why th’blazes didn’t you say so before! Can’t
I get a boat to take me off ?”
“Yes; there’s a cove down there as will put yer on
board.” 1
The man with the yelling baby danced on the roadway,
“ Take me to him ! Show me the way, can’t you ! ”
The lumper detached himself from the group, and the
pair of them stumbled together to the water’s edge
where they shouted, a mad trio, with a gruff waterman,
while the baby howled a solo at the masthead lights of

The man with the baby instantly turned and fled up
the hill, closely pursued by the small laborer and the
cheers of the group by the shed. An opportune hansom,
returning- to the rank, came round the corner, and a
g*reat howl of welcome, led by the baby, went up to the
wondering stars. The hansom stopped, the three of them
fell in, and the conveyance disappeared over the hill in a
cloud of dust and yells. I hurried down to the tram, and
just managed to catch the Mosman boat. The Imperti-
nent Clara—a mass of lights—came gliding down past
Miison’s Point as our boat swept round the corner of the
P. &O. wharf. Out in the centre of the stream a lantern
leaped up and down and yelled with rage. At least, that
is what it seemed like ; in reality it was a boatman in a
skiff signalling that he had a man and a bundle of howls
to put on board. Abell rang faintly inside the steamer,
and she bore down like a moving mountain on the tiny
boat.

“ Kodak ” tells a story of the sea and an
infuriated baby :
It was 7 o’clock of a still summer night. The shore bell
rang sharply, and the passengers’ friends and relations
climbed ashore and stood in a group on the wharf where
the great port side-light, like a very bleary eye, glared
vinously at a pile of empty casks. “Any more for the
shore? ” cried a seaman. A man wrung the hand of hi 3
friend and hurried off just as a voice from the bridge
ordered the gangway to be taken in. There was a rattle
of iron and wood, a confused babel of last words, and
then the clear ring of the engine-room telegraph. The
steamer commenced to back out like a lady from the
presence of royalty, and two pages in moleskins, bearing
between them the loop of the rope train by which she was
to swing, walked carefully along the edge of the wharf.
The jumble of friends and relations walking behind them
and waving handkerchiefs made a good enough imitation
of a patriotic crowd. I waited till the vessel had
turned and was moving slowly ahead, and then I lit out
for home. As I reached the roadway an enraged man,
carrying an infuriated baby, rushed madly down Mar-
garet-street.

The trials of William King, Melbourne’s
specially ferocious negro burglar and brutal
assault expert, are humorous. King is doing what
is practically a life sentence ; that is to say he is a
nigger fury who is getting on in years, and he has
a very long sentence to serve, and after that he is
to serve an indeterminate sentence which will last
until he reforms, and as he has no idea of reform-
ing he is really in for life. William, who is a
huge mass of ink, batters warders almost to death
at times, and on account of the usual amusement
he was lately committed for trial. In such a case
the Law seems to have two alternatives—either to
gi ve William a long sentence to run concurrently
with his present one, or to give him a long sen-
tence which will start when his present (practi-
cally) life sentence is finished. Either way the
Law doesn’t seem likely to get much change out
of William the Murky—and he may burst out of
the dock and assault the Judge.

“ Harrison 0 his New Year narrative :
Walter and Dick and I always made it our business to
pet drunk on New Year’s Eve. Walter said that it was a
duty we owed to the community, and Dick and I signified
consent by admiring silence. It was 190 S that we started
our spree, and 1909 when we brought it to a regretful
conclusion. It was somewhere in between that we met
Jenkins. Jenkins was a married man, and should have
known better, but we allowed him to join us, because he
was a generous old boy who insisted upon paying for
every drink. We got rid of a good deal of moisture one
way and another. We started on beer —it was very nice
bber, good,, wet.stuff ; then Walter suggested creme de
mfenthe, which we accordingly sampled, solely out of
deference to his wishes ; besides it seemed a waste not to
take it when Jenkins..was paying for it. After creme de
menthe we tried several other forms of dampness, so as
not to offend old Jenkins, who had suggested them.
When closing time came Walter objected to leaving the
premises, but the retailer of demented and whirlingliquors
insisted. Jenkins ranged himself with Walter, andpleaded
piteously : ‘‘ A beer, mish’er ; jush one, f’r Gor’s shake—
hie—it—it’sh f’r me wifesh—shesh’s ill.” The dispenser
of bottled humidity remained quite unmoved by this
touching appeal, and we had to leave. Jenkins sobbed
heart-brokenly; great tears dozed from his eyes and
chased each other, down his cheeks until they at last
found refuge in his bushy white whiskers. Dick and I
left with some dignity. Not so Walter; he Was thrown
out and landed on his ear because he called the moisture
manufacturer “a callous Bacchanalian monstrosity.”
Then at Jenkins’ suggestion we went and had some cray-
fish. It was really delectable crayfish 4 eating i t was like
devouring a sonnet by Keats. It was not until we were
outside the fish emporium, and I was about to hail a cab,
t hat Jenkins suddenly discovered that he had forgotten
where he lived. He’ had a vague idea that it was in
“jSou’ Yarrish.” We were walking the streets of that
eminently respectable suburb in the first hours of
the New Year, searching for Jenkins’ elusive residence. <
We knocked up about a dozen wrong people. A.t about
the fifteenth house we knocked at, what looked like a
ghost with its hair in curl-papers, appeared at the
window: “ Ish thash you, Alish, m’ dearsh?” inquired
old Jenkins sweetly. “You beast! Oh, you BRUTE ! ”
hissed the ghost. “ Misht’r Jenkins hash jus’ be’n ’avin’
a—hie—beg pard’n—crayfish supper with us,” I explained
valiantly. “Mr. JENKINS! Isn’t that you, Albert?”
I KneW it was not because his name is Henry. The ghost
melted away, and the window closed with a bang. It
was well on in the New Year when we knocked at the
door of the twentieth'house. For a while there was no
reply—then it came. Jenkins was singing tenderly ;he
was as far as '
Zo-zo^-biC —Margot, Clo-clo,
Frou-frou— : ■
when a hair-brush struck him in the solar plexus, and a
cake of soap came'im eon tact with his pate. We also
made the acquaintance of various similar articles. For
myself I was content with a boot. Jenkins sat on the
footpath where he had fallen: “Than’ yo’ vere mush,
gen’men, f’r seein’ me ’ome, butsh” (beaming happily)
“ ’sail ri’t now. This ish th’ plashe, gen’men—hie ! ”

on the Melbourne trams as compared to the Syd-
ney rushers. I once noted a drunk on board the
dummy of a Carlton tram. He said he was from
Sydney on asb.nst, , Approaching a turn, in the;
line, the gripman sung out: “Hold fast going
round the curve.” Then the conductor yelled,
“Mind the turn, please.” Around in the car
where the drunk stood were notices warning the
passerigeps of theganger of tbemurve. The drunk
hung;on to the iron standard of the car tenaciously.
He ’exhorted , his fellow passengers to be “very
carefullsh,” The tram Slowly swung round a bend
and stopped. The conductor got out and pushed
the tram along. The drunk hung on to the iron
standard. After a while he awesomely asked,
“Are we coming to the curve?”. The gripman
replied that he had passed it long ago. Then the
drunk fell off in a faint through nervous exhaustion

Do you enjoy what you eat ?
Do you look forward to meal times with that pleasure that
all healthy men and women should ?

or domestic worries?
If you don’t, you have Dyspepsia.
Dyspepsia robs you of all that makes life worth living.

THE WEIGHT QUESTION.
In the weight of a bicycle there exists a point where safety is assured. Every ounce beyond
that point is superfluous and means wasted energy. Every ounce of that superfluous weight cut off
means increased ease of propulsion. In a Bicycle built of
GENUINE B.S.A. FITTINGS
the point of safety is assured not by a clumsy addition of weight, but by the use of the
tinest material the world produces, by the knowledge of the most expert designers, and
by the most scientific methods of manufacture known to-day.
Thus are secured the three essentials—Strength, Lightness, Speed. The fourth essen-
i ia y important is Suitability, and this can be secured by having your new bicyde
built to your order to suit yourself.

«A POLICY FOR THE COMMONWEALTH/'
Post Free 6d. from “The
Bulletin ” Office
Of You and July.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IF you had passed me by
One jewelled night of July,
When the jasmine, to halo your dazzling
head,
Trailed down from the balcony garlanded ;
When my acolyte sense, at thfe calling of you,
Drew in to the shining, swift thralling of you ;
Then, shamed (it may be) to self-scorning therefor,
In a half-willed revolting all haltingly wrought—
Self-gyved with its dear unforgettable pain—
Yet smiling, sank back to its thraldom again ;
If my pulse had not stilled in the nearness that
brought
(Through the rubbing of feet on the rustling floor)
My soul to run down like a palmful of sand,
To lie with my life in the cup of your hand—
Ah, if you had passed me by,
Nor forged me this shining thrall,
Nor sung me this strange, new madrigal
That harps in my blood, should I know what I
Had lost, that night of July?
I do not wonder, I
(Since one throned night of July)
That, vibrantly-strung, in your Presence’s span,
Thrill my days, who was erst but a masterless
man !
Or that drinking the exquisite fineness of you,
The flawless, compelling divineness of you,
You prisoned my sense with one curve of your
arm,
Drew my glad soul to be drowned in your eyes,
Checked my mad heart to lie palpitant there
With the loose veiling mesh of your luminous
hair ;
Yet alway this understream deepening plies
Down these veins running rich with the wine of
your charm—
“ Think deeper, love less ! For it may be in this
Perchance, that the truest of tenderness is.”
Yet what (if you passed me by
To-day) should I covet or choose
For this sweet, glad sorrow I would not lose,
This dear half-dying, that else had I
Foregone that night of July?
Queenliness, I would try—
Since one rare night of July—
Schooling each sense, so to fathom and know
What your Whiteness doth will me—to shapen
me so
That the very intangible sweetness of you,
The virginal, splendid completeness of you
Should rein the fierce blood that my heart riots
through,
Not to strive, but serve mute; since I would not
to plead
With my knees in the dust, and the trite lovers’
tale
Grows outworn (God’s pity) and tawdrily stale ;
Still hearken! The truest man hath not such
need
To love as be loved ; yet You always are You,
And lam but I; so I would not to kiss
But your steps, so I be where your perfectness is.
Yet, if you had passed me by,
Had my drab soul, otherwise,
Grown greyer, O cloud-grey autumn eyes ?
Had my shrunk life slowlier shrivelled dry
From that sad night of July ?
So through my life you lie
(Since one crowned night of July)
As a golden inthreading that one doth see
Run down through an age-dulled tapestry ;
And even the dearest, least semblance of you—
The rarest, remotest resemblance of you—
Is the hand on the censer that circles and swings
The incense of you through my centremost
thought;
For not in that comfortless cloister, my heart,
(I hold) are you penned, O my Whiteness, apart;
But regally here, all-pervasive, unsought,
The wonder that glories you ripples and rings
Through my brain’s most minutest and secretest
cells
Like a mellowing, wind-driven cadence of bells.
Yet, if you had passed me by
(Though gracious) according me
But coldest of finger-tipped courtesy,
Should I have seen it or sorrowed—I —
That idyll-night of July ?
VIC.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.
Her Escort.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was five past eleven. The out-pouring
theatre crowds were surging down bril-
liantly-lit Bourke-street in an unceasing
confused torrent of men, women, cabs and jangling
trams.
The girl, with her filmy-white skirts well
gathered over her arm and her silken wraps drawn
closely around her, turned off into the compara-
tive darkness and quietness of Russell-street, and
scurried along on the middle of the pavement
towards the station with a nervous little jog-trot
that spoke volumes. She Rad negotiated about
half the way in safety, in spite of the grave terrors
she half-fancied were lurking for her in every dark
shadow, when suddenly a man dashed out of a
dark lane, away down between her and the railway
lights, and, turning up the hill, came straight
towards her. There were few people about, and
she looked at the advancing figure in alarm, and
•doubted whether to cross over and give him that
: side of the road to himself or keep straight on and
••dodge him.
She decided upon the latter, and quickened her
pace.
He advanced with a backward, hunted look, and
•commenced, as he ran, to frantically tear off a
•light overcoat. He did this without in any way
■•slackening his speed, and threw the coat into the
shadow of a warehouse basement. A hard felt
hat followed the coat, and he dragged a cap on in
its place. She could hear his labored panting long
before he got near her.
In her terror and dismay she kept to the ex-
treme edge of the pathway, giving him a wide
berth, and almost breaking into a run herself.
He staggered on, his breath coming in great,
sobbing gasps, and as his wide-open, desperate
eyes fell upon her in p is.-dng, a sudden inspiration
of hope seemed to flash upon him. And as she
saw his face a great pity and curiosity came over
the girl in spite of her nervousness. What had
the poor wretch done to make him run like that ?
And then her blood seemed to freeze within her,
for he turned suddenly in his mad career and came
quite close to her, and commenced to walk back
at her side, with stumbling, staggering steps, the
way he had come.
For the moment she was too paralysed with
terror to shriek or run away.
“Madam,” he panted, holding her arm. “No,
don’t stop—and don’t be frightened—there’s no
harm for you. I’m a poor devil throwing myself
upon your mercy and kindness —I’m run to earth
—and they’re nearly upon me. Give me a chance.
Let me walk quietly down with you to the station,
and I think I can shake them off the scent doub-
ling back like that. If not, you can give me up —
for I’m done —but don’t be frightened. Do just
as you please,” he added, more gently, freeing
her arm. “Oh God!” he muttered, under his
breath, “ here they come.”
In spite of her fright, something made her
hesitate to raise an alarm.
“Is it something very bad?” she whispered,
fearfully, between her chattering teeth, looking
from him to the rapidly advancing figure.
“As bad as can be,” he said, briefly, meeting
her eyes.
She looked at him again, still mechanically
walking along, her thoughts in a medley. What
should she do? What was it he had done? Who
wanted him? A thousand conjectures flashed
across her mind. But there was nothing of the
conventional criminal about him. Except for
a certain dishevelment, he was well dressed and
looked appropriately in his place at her side.
And then she looked ahead. Fast coming up the
hill- was the pursuer —no, there were two now—
and there was another just dashing around the
corner, and among them was the ominous glint of
a helmet. Swiftly the hunted man’s appearance
and the hopelessness of his situation made her
feminine logic prevail against her conscience and
common sense.
“You can try it,” she said huskily, her lips
dry and parched. “Here, carry my things for
me—it will be something,” and she handed over
her opera-glasses and lolly-box. “It’s chilly;
turn up your collar and pull your cap over your
eyes—and perhaps you could smoke.”
He bowed and obeyed her. He got out a cigar-
case, chose a weed, and lit it with care and cool-
ness. They stopped a second or two to allow him
to do so, and thereby gained a few moments, and
then she slipped her trembling hand on his arm,
and it didn’t make her any more at ease to feel
that his heart was beating like a sledgehammer.
What if they were stopped ? What would her
friends say ? How would it all look in big head-
lines in the newspapers to morrow !
A stoutish man, puffing like a grampus, reached
them first.
“ Which way did he go?—man running up this
way”
“ Why, that will be the man we saw,” said the
girl hastily to her companion whose head was
enveloped in a cloud of smoke. “He had on an
overcoat and hard felt hat, and ”
“ Yes, yes !” panted the man, turning to her
eagerly; “that’s him, Miss! Down that lane
over there, you say. Thanks.” And he set off
running again, muttering and groaning, “Oh,
Heaven ! I believe the damn villain’s escaped us
after all.”
“Only two more!” gasped the girl faintly.
But they, seeing their leader punching on ahead
across the road, went steadily on themselves with-
out stopping, and the girl felt almost like crying
with relief, while the man at her side began to
breathe normally again.
They were at the lane from which the chase had
emerged, when a fourth man, tearing around the
corner, ran full tilt into them. The girl screamed
hysterically.
“I beg your” he pantingly began, when
his eyes fell upon the face of the girl’s companion
under the full glare of the arc light, and they
almost started out of his head in amazement as he
stammered, “Richard Kerry—you—by God!”
and he nearly sprang upon him. Then, to her
own horror, the girl broke into a laugh. It came
in so unexpectedly and naturally that the new-
comer hesitated, and looked from one to the other
in growing doubt.
“ What did he call you ? What is the matter?”
she went on, clinging closer to the arm she held.
“Why, he’s drunk, I should say. Never saw
him in my life before,” said the man at her side,
quietly. “ Gues t you’ve made a mistake this time,
old man. Now get out of our road, there’s a good
chap, or we’ll miss our train.”
The pursuer mechanically stood aside in com-
plete bewilderment, and let them pass.
They went on their way swiftly in silence, but
she saw his face was white and haggard, and great
beads of moisture glistened upon his forehead.
“Now, go quickly, for that man is suspicious
and will come after you,” she said as they hurried
along.
“No,” he said, firmly. “You are all alone,
and I am going to see you into your train first.
Yes, yes!” he went on, unheeding her protests.
“By the way, you know, when you are by your-
self you shouldn’t come lonely ways like this to
get to the station —they don’t pay.”
She smiled. He saw it and understood.
“True,” he answered, bitterly. “I’ve put it
out of my power ever to talk like that again,
haven’t I ? What is your train ? ”
“ The 11.20 to Hawthorn. But why won’t you
go and get to safety ? ” She gave a swift glance
behind them. “He hasn’t gone on—he’s still
standing there, looking after us.”
“You’ve just a few minutes to catch it—got
your ticket ? ”
But the girl was giving a last look behind
before they turned the corner.
“ He has made up his mind, and he is running
this way like mad—oh, why don’t you get away
while you can? ”
“Because I’m really safest in the crowd,” he
said, as they walked smartly along, and soon
mingled with the crush about the station en-
trance.
So he saw her to a carriage.
“Good-bye and better luck,” she murmured,
holding out her hands.
He hesitated, and a dull flush came over his
face, while it grew even more worn and haggard.
“No, I won’t give you mine,” he said thickly,
“ for it’s ”
The whistle blew.
“ Shake hands,” she commanded hastily and im-
peratively. “For you are going to get away and
commence everything over again.”
He hesitated no longer, but took her out-
stretched fingers in a feverish grip.
And then, as she glanced at their clasped hands,
she grew cold, sick, faint, and dizzy, for his cuff
and sleeve were stained with blood.
“Oh, what have I done?” she moaned, closing
her eyes, and shutting out the sight. She felt a
last pressure upon her limp fingers—and then
everything seemed rushing along in an indistinct
blur.
R. A. KENT.

The Contract of Six Months.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ \T ou see dot farm down dere ? ” asked
the German. “ Veil, ven I fus’coom to
dis countree, I vas engage to vork dere
by de contrac’ of six monts. Afder I vas vork
some time mit de farmer, I find I get more vages
farder on ; but de farmer, he not let me go. If I
go, mein,contrac’ I break, und lose mein vages,
und get in de lock-up put.
“Furst, I say to meinselluf, I do de vork bad,
so dot he let me go. Ach ! he vas a kerfluchter
pig-face. Ven I vork bad, he give mede bad food
—de tea dot has not de sugar in it, und de meat
niit de bad smell. Dot vas not good. Den I try
to make him so mad dot he vill let me go. Dis
farmer dat I vork for he vas a Velsh from Vales
—he never laff at noding; all de time he dink,
dink how he make for himselluf some more money.
“Veil, some time he go to de river to shoot de
wil’ duck nit de gun. He vas not shoot so well
as de haystack to hit. Ven he get near de river,
he vas throw off his coat, und crawl along de
groun’ so dot de duck vas not hear him, und fly
avay. Den he creep along, ver’ slow, und he cut his
han’ on de sharp stone, und dot make him svear.
Den de bull-ant vas bite him, und he svear again
some more. Veil, he crawl like dis for five
minutes, und he get mooch eggscited, und his han’
shake. Joost as he go to shoot, I vas run up de
oder side und say, ‘Boo, Boo!’ und de duck, he
say ‘ Quack, quack ! ’ und fly avay. Den de
farmer he say to me ‘Vat you do dot for, you
tam devilish son of de dog?’ I say, ‘I not see
you ; I vas singing.’ Den he dance aboud und
say he shoot me mit de gun de nex’ time.
“ Afder dot some time he go to shoot de duck
again. Dis time he haf friends. He again crawl
along de groun’, und dey all get mooch eggscited,
und for quarter of de hour dey all'sneak und
crawl, und take off deir coats, und make signs of
de han’ to each oder. Joost as dey go to shoot I
gallop up on mein horse und crack mdin stoekvip,
und de ducks say ‘ Quack, quack,’ und fly avay.”
“ Did hfe discharge you then? ” I asked.
“No, de tam pig-face dog, he shoot at me mit
de gun, und dree grains of shot go in mein leg.
He say afder id vas de accident, but I dink he try
to shoot me so dot he save de dree poun’s he owe
me for vages. He vas a cunning man, dot Velsh.
Afder dot, I take mein vages by de veek.
“Afder dot some time he vant to make him-
selluf some more money. Der vas un old shed in
de paddock, dot he has insured in de fire-office.
De shed id vas no good, so he try to have it burnt
und get de money. Veil, vun day, ven he dink
nobody see him, he set fire to de grass near de
shed, und den he gallop oop to me und say, ‘ Hans,
de bush fire, id has broke out. Coome qvick, let
us put id out. Do nod mind de shed, as ve gan-
not save id from de fire.’ So ve go de fire
und gommence to beat mit bushes at id. De
grass vas dry, und do Velsh, he pearly kill him-
selluf to stop id burning his paddock. Ven he vas
nod looking, I vas go to de ol’ shed und I vas stop
de fire from burning id. De Velsh he vork like
de teufel, und thrash at de fire und sveat, und
svear mooch bad language. Afder two hours he
coom to me und say, ‘ Veil, ve haf put de dam
fire out. It is a pity id burnt de shed. Vere vas
de shed ? I gannot see mit de smoke.’ I say to
him, ‘ld has nod burnt de shed. I have save de
shed.’ Und he say, ‘Veil, you vas de tamdest
fool dot in de vorl’ vas ever seen.’ Und I say,
‘ Vat, you vant de shed to be burnt?’ Und he
say, ‘ No, no, dot is nod so.’ For he vas afraid of
debolice. Und I laff to meinselluf mooch.
“ Afder dot some time, he set fire to de grass on
de oder side of de shed. It vas in de night, so
dot nobody vas see him. Soon afder, de breeze
id blow so hard dot de fire dravel var’ qvick, und
de Velsh he haf to call on de neighbors to help.
To me he say noding, as he dink I am de tam
nuisance. I vas always a goot, kin’ man, so I go
up und stop de fire from burning de shed again.
De Velsh, he vork like de teufel, und sveat und
svear, und at las’ at dree o’clock of de morning
dey put de fire out, afder it has burnt forty acres
of grass und a little hay-stack. Den dey got to
bed. In de morning, early, I coom to de Velsh,
und I say, ‘Veil, I vant more vages.’ He. say,
‘Vat for?’ Und I say, ‘ld vas a bad fire las’
night.’ Und he say, ‘ld vas; undid vas a pity
dot de shed is burnt afder all.’ Und I say, ‘No,
id is nod burnt; I haf save id again, und I vas
vant more vages.’ Und he say, ‘ Oh, you plasted
hound of de kennel, I vill flay de skin from you.’
Und he chase me mit de pick-handle, und foam at
de mout’.”
“ Did he let you go, then ? ” I inquired.
“No; as I vas say, he vas a pig-face dog, dot
Velsh. He nod let me go joost for spite. He
made me to clean de pig-stye out, und id haf a smell
of de mos’ horrible. Ach ! de stink of id ! Ven
I finish dot, he say, ‘Hans, haf you of enough
brains to clean de cow-yard out ? ’ Und so I haf
to clean de cow-yard. Himmel! Id smell of de
rotten egg. Dree days I vas clean id. Und dere
vas oder dirty places dot I haf to clean. I
vas tam sick of id all, I tell id to you.
Den at las’ he say, ‘ Hans, do you remember dose
vet sheep-skins dot vere thrown in de creek?
Veil you put dem in de dray und take dem
through de town to de farm of mein son, und give
to him dis letter.’ Veil, I put dem skins in de
dray. Dey vere falling to pieces, und dey haf de
tamdest stink dot in de vorl’ vas ever seen. I haf
to do dis, or I break mein contrac’ of de six monts
und lose mein vages. It vas for spite dot so he
did.. Veil, I drive de dray through de town, und
de people, dey hold demsellufs by de nose, und de
little boys, dey follow me, und jeer at me, und
announce dot I am de dirty man. Und de girl
dot Ido de mash mit, she see me. Id vas terrible !
“Afder dot some time, de mont’ for de regis-
tration of de dogs vas coom. De Velsh, he haf
seven dogs, but he not tell of dem to de bolice, so
dot if dey find de dogs on de place, he vould be
fined ten poun’s or about. Veil, vun day de
sergeant he coom to de place, und de dogs dey
are all in de cellar locked up, so dot dey are nod
seen by him. Dey say not’ing to me. Ven de
sergeant coom up, he say to de Velsh, ‘ Haf you
any dogs?’ De Velsh, he say‘No.’ Deni say
to him, ‘ Vat about dem seven dogs dot are in de
cellar? You haf forgotten dem.’ Und de
sergeant he laff so as to break his sides, und den
he go and find de dogs in de cellar.
“ Dot night de Velsh he coom to me und give
to me mein vages, und he says to me in a husky
voice, ‘ Now, you go avay, or I kill you.’ He
seem to me qvite mad an’ froth at de mout’, so
dot I am afraid, und pack up mein swag, und run
to de road. As I run avay he follow me, und flog
me on de legs, so dot I gannot sit meinselluf
down for two, dree days afder. Aeh ! he was a
bUggard-pig, dot Velsh, to so treat me who am
de quietest man dot in de vorl’ vas ever seen ! ”
A. P. ROGERS.
Riverine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ITS level lands are spread away
To meet the setting sun ;
Fierce summers o’er them scorch and slay
The grass blades, one by one ;
Long, wicked droughts have dried their breasts,
These virgin lands and clean ;
But still a fertile promise rests
Upon the Riverine,
The fecund Riverine.
It rises, and its worth attests
The Phcenix Riverine.
If breeds no wasters on its lands—
The grim, defiant plains
Are held by strong Australian hands
That firmly grasp the reins ;
Wild horsemen these, who race and wheel
The clustered gums between ;
They keep the stirrup to the heel,
Way down in Riverine,
Far out in Riverine ;
Undaunted souls and hearts of steel,
Are found in Riverine.
No green palms in the Sunlight sway ;
Nor doth the wild, red rose,
In dewy fragrance to the Day
Uncultured charms disclose :
But here and there a garden smiles ;
And when the Spring falls green,
She puts her feet a thousand miles
Across the Riverine,
Along the Riverine.
She wooes, with Amazonian wiles,
Her Lord the Riverine !
Then glutted ewes beside their lambs
Know well the season’s “ good ” ;
To billabongs and creeks and dams
Flocks down a feathered brood ;
Unto a rotund beast hath grown
The packhorse lank and lean ;
The squatter comes unto his own
Along the Riverine,
Across the Riverine.
The stock are fat and sleek and blown
Throughout the Riverine.
A wool-barge, in her steamer’s track,
Swings slowly round the bends ;
Her hawser may not fitly slack
Until the journey ends.
The flood is over reef and sand,
The channel’s wide and clean ;
There’s water in the rivers, and
There’s joy in Riverine,
Hurrah for Riverine !
The shearer and the steamboat hand
Find graft thro’ Riverine.
White snows upon Monaro die ;
Beneath October suns
Warm tropic rains in cowals lie
Along far Queensland runs ;
The ’Bidgee and the Lachlan swell
Their crumbling banks between —
They'll have a record clip to tell
This year in Riverine,
For once in Riverine.
They’re pulling round ; they’re doing well
At last, in Riverine.
I walk dull streets ; and in mine ear
A city’s tumult rings ;
But through my heart a river clear
Beyond the ranges sings,
And visions of the plains come down
The by-ways drab, unclean.
I see the trackless prairies brown
Of dear old Riverine.
God bless the Riverine !
I would that I might shed the town
For you, old Riverine !
There’s strength and effort in the West,
There’s mateship staunch and true ;
(And, sweetheart of my one-time quest,
’Twas there I courted you !)
A blight be on these city ways !
The wastrel and the quean
Can find no place where Manhood lays
Its grip on Riverine,
Its hand on Riverine.
No “ pocket Venus ” loud displays
Her charms in Riverine.
The chiming horse-bells clink and r: ig
From Bourke to Tocumwal !
Around their fires the drovers sing,
The old bush Voices call.
And I must rise and get me gone
To ease my longings keen ;
I’ll saddle up and journey on
Across the Riverine,
And down the Riverine.
I’m sure of welcome warm from One
Who waits in Riverine.
N.s.w.
E. J. BRADY.

RABBIT DESTRUCTION.
“ Currandooley,” Bungendore,
Ttrek- Limited, -4th December, 1908.
Circular Quay West, Sydney.
Dear Sirs, —I have now (riven your method of
rabbit destruction a thorough trial, and am pleased
to say that the result has exceeded my expectation.
The cost for clearing- a 360-acre paddock, badly
infested with rabbits, has amounted to a trifle
under 2s. per acre. The same paddock would have
cost £2 per acre to dig'out.
I am satisfied that this cost can be considerably
reduced by using your latest “Perfection”
machine. I am therefore getting two of these to
work in conjunction With the plant already on the
ground, and will let you have the result in a few
weeks. Yours faithfully,
(Sgd.) HARRY OSBORNE.
TYREE LIMITED,
CIRCULAR QUAY WEST( Opp. E. A A. Wharf).
